Fancy a Happy Christmas - without a
'cameo cigarette.
Don’t wish it for a moment. Lay in a stock,
and remember that 25 empty Cameo boxes
will give you a set of button portraits of
the English Cricketing Team. Ask your
tobacconist to show you these—they’re splendid!
THE RED PAGE.
Melb. University Prof. Morris’s ‘ Diotionary
of Australasian Words, Phrases, and Usages,’
(Macmillan and Co.) is certainly ‘calculated to
supply a Want.’ Pity the author hasn’talittlemore
fire and force and illuminating-power 1 Judging
him by his portrait and his Diotionary and his
Life of Higinbotham, he is a rather narrow, and
rather learned, and very conscientious man who
has heaped so many books on his brain that its
capacity for original impetus and stimulus lies
squashed as flat as a damper. He is professor of
English, French, and German languages and
literatures, and his books read as if he could be
Dull in all those languages and literatures without
the least difficulty—without even trying. And
Mr. Morris seems conscious that he haunts his
subjects like the ghost of a very heavy man who
died a very long time ago, for now and again he
jokes with great solemnity and an air of looking
round nervously to see if he’s observed—in the
guilty fashion of a man who laughs at a funeral.
E.g.—
THE MORRIS JOKE.
Much of Australasian nomenclature is flue to “the
man in the bush ’’—more precise address not recorded.
[This is a Joke.] Givers of new names may be benefaotors
to their language or violators of its purity and simplicity,
but in’either case they are nearly always, like the burial-
plaoe of Moses—[This is a Jo/ce]—unknown.
Good Heavens, what gambols 1
• * * *
Luoky that the slow, stolid mind has the merits
of its defects. The heavy man, getting no intense
moments, gets no reaction from intense moments :
breeding no exaltation, he misses the correspond-
ing depression : consequently he is able to peg
away on the low plane of effort with a persistent
industry which brighter spirits can’t achieve.
Ordinarily speaking, that is. Lots of men can
swing intellectual clubs slowly for eight hours a
day, with Sunday and holiday spells; lots can
swing fast for four hours ; but not one man in a
mental million can swing fast for eight hours, and
keep a-goin’.
* • » *
[Several people have written wanting to know
more about Sam Walter Fqss, the 39-year-old
New Hampshire Yank whose ‘ Keep A-Goin’ 1 ’
was reprinted in Bulletin not so long ago. Pre-
sently. Meantime, here’s a good thing (with a
touch of Kipling’s ‘ For to Admire ’) from his last
book of verses—
TO SEE THE THING GO ON.
‘ What’s all this thing about ? ’ says he.
‘ Wall, I dunno,’ says I.
‘ What good is all this worl’ to me,
This lan’ an* sea an’ sky ?
The same ol* thing! Git up an* dress,
An’ eat an’ work like sin;
Then go to bed, git up an’ dress,
An’ eat an’ work ag’in.
What’s all this thing about ? ’ eays he.
Says I: ‘ Can’t tell ye, John ;
But, as for me, I like to see,
To see the thing go on.
‘ There ain’t no end to this machine,
An’ no man hareabout,
Bo fur as I have ever seen,
Can tell what it grinds out ;
Its belts are hitched to far-off gears,
Far out he-ond the sun,
An’ I’ve no doubt ’twill run for yean
The way it alius run.’
‘ But what’s the thing about? ’ eays he;
Says 1, ‘ Can’t tell ye, John;
But, as for me, I like to see.
To see the thing go on.’
‘This day an’ night an’ night an* day.
The same ol’ thing,’ says John.
• I guess it is,’ says I, ‘ but say,
Let’s watch the thing go on ;
For all the grass an’ things that grow,
An’ stars, it seems to me,
Are jest a free-for-nothin’ show
For us deadheads to see.
An* I ain’t tired of it yit,
It’s pretty middlin’, John:
An’ as for me, I like to see,
To see the thing go on.
‘ I like to see the thing, my friend,
’Tis healthy sport for man.
Though I can’t tell ye where ’twill end,
Nor where the thing began.’
‘What’s all the thing about?’ ‘Dunno:
'Tis fun enough for me
To jest lay back an’ see the show
An’ wonder ; yes, sir-ee!
An’ so I guess that we are here, v
An’ that’s our business, John,
To work an’ git ourselves in gear
To help the thing go on.’]
• • • *
Prof. Morris deserves credit for his Dic-
tionary. Many people have had the idea of such
a book, but he is first to milk the idea into a good
substantial bucket. And the next man will
lean a lot on Professor Morris’s merits, and learn
a lot from his defects. His book—ill-illumined
compilation though it be—is in part a solid and
valuable contribution to Australian literature.
• * • ■
But—
1. The Dictionary contains many misprints.
E.g.—
“ Barfere ” for Barrere, p. 88, col. 1.
“ E. Meston ” for A. Meston, p. 236, col. 1.
P. 120, 001. 1, a reference to dilly-bag is placed under
dingle-bird.
And there are many others.
2. The type of the subject-heads and of the
page-heads is not sufficiently bold. It’s the differ-
ence between a freshly-painted sign-post and a
sign-post half-obliterated. t
3. The Dictionary is Mixed. Names of plants
and animals run in alphabetical order with the
Australian ivords which the dictionary professes
to connote and explain. There are often whole
pages of botanical references and descriptions
borrowed from Maiden or Von Mueller or others.
Without these and similar lists of fishes, birds,
and so on, the Dictionary would have been a
much less imposing volume. Yet it is not clear
why botany and philology should be confused.
But since it was decided to include lists of
animals and plants, with their vernacular names—
and these lists, in a popular, pan-Australasian
form, were oertainly much needed —why were nob
plants and animals listed separately, and separately
from words included for linguistic and national
reasons? It seems as if almost everything in
clarity and convenience was to be gained, and
hardly anything lost, by such separate classifica-
tion. At present the searcher has to waste much
time unnecessarily in scanning pages and turning
over leaves.
4. The Diotionary is Misleading. Even an
Australian is liable to be put wrong : a foreigner
can hardly go right. This is because Mr. Morris,
with a stupidity oertainly unexpected in a man of
his attainments, has included (a) obsolete or
obsolescent words without the least hint to show
that they are not in daily use like their next-
column neighbors; has included (b) words of
limited use in a particular Australian district
without the leasb hint to show that they are not
used like their next-column neighbors, throughout
Australia; (c) has curiously failed, in some im-
portant instances, to supply adequate definitions ;
and (d) has supplied some definitions altogether
erroneous.
• • • •
[The essence of two of the most obvious of these
blunders (a and b) is a leap from the part (of time
or space) to the whole; and the blunders them-
selves are singularly common among writers on
Australia. Marcus Clarke, who should have
known better, was a notable offender; and,
despite the interest and rhetorical oharm of his pre-
face to Gordon’s poems, it is questionable whether
it would nob have been wiser, in Australia’s
interest, to have suppressed that famous prefaoe
altogether—such an untrue impression does it
make upon strangers who do not know our
country. Olarke argued from Victoria, of which
he had seen a little, to Australia, of which he had
seen comparatively nothing, and made the widest
possible statements on the slenderest possible
grounds. ‘ Our trees without shade ’is nonsense as
a general statement of pan-Australian application;
but possibly Olarke never saw a Moreton Bay fig,
or a cedar, or a myrtle, or a dozen others.
Certainly he never saw that fig in its native lair,
or he would never have written “in the Austra-
lian forests no leaves fall." “ Our flowers without
perfume ” is nonsense as a general statement;
but probably Clarke never smelt a N.Q. orohid
perfuming the air for hundreds of yards around it.
And so this very clever craftsman goes on with
statements literally true in a sense and to a degree,
and so made as to be thoroughly misleading to
the foreigner who takes them literally.]
• • • *
Prof. Morris should assuredly have known
better. Didn’t he acquire any logio when he was
assimilating English, French, and German litera-
ture? And if he did, Barbara Celarent should
have taught him that (a) and (b) discount by 33|
per cent, the value of his Diotionary. For ex-
ample—
Fist, v. To use the hands. The word is not unknown
in English in the sense of to grip. (Shakspere, ‘Cor.*
iv., y., 124).
1846. C. P. Hodgson, ‘ Reminiscences of Australia,’
p. 366:
“ • Fist it,’ a colonial expression, Ac.
If Fist It ever had any vogue, it’s dead long
ago. And Mr. Morris cannot argue that his
quotation-date alone suffices to show that; sinoe
the quotation for Fish-hawk, immediately pre-
ceding, dates 1848; and Fish-hawk is to-day’s
name for a well-known bird. Further, in some
cases (as Dungaree-Settler) Prof. Morris does add
‘Now obsolete,’so the foreign reader is left tc
infer that Fist It is in common use at the present
moment.
Skullbankhr, or Soowbankeb, a slang name in Aus-
tralia for a loafer, a tramp.
1866, A. Michie, ‘ Retrospects and Prospects of the
Colony,’ p. 9:
‘A skull-banker is a species of the genua loafer—half
highwayman, half beggar, He is a haunter of stations,
and lives on the squatters, amongst whom lie makes a
circuit, affecting to seek work and determining not to
find it.’
Not many people hear SkullbankEr nowadays,
even in Victoria. And Mr. Morris deliberately en-
larges Michie’s Victorian reference to include all-
Australia, and adds a present-day inference!
There are dozens of similar cases. And for
examples of (6)
Jack the Painter, n. Very strong bush-tea, so called
from the marK it leaves round the drinker’s mouth.
Jack the Painter had some Victorian vogue,
but is obsolescent, and it is hardly, ever used
beyond Victoria, if at all. Yet Mr. Morris in-
ferentially applies it to all Australia to-day.
Coast, v. To loaf about from station to station.
1890. ‘Rolf Boldrewood,’ ‘Squatter’sDream,’xxv. 296:
‘ I ain’t like you, Towney, able to coast about without
a job of work from shearin’ toshearin’.’
Coast is obsolescent Vic. and N.S.W. slang,
doubtfully used elsewhere: Mr. Morris gives
it full present-day currency and all-Australian
referenoe. He makes no reference to the much
more common use of ‘ coast ’ in cycling. There are
dozens of similar cases.
* * • *
Keliance on ‘Rolf Boldrewood’ often leads
Mr. Morris into error. ‘ Rolf Boldrewood s ex-
perience covered limited districts of Victoria and
N.S. Wales, during a limited time, and his novels
reflect truly the language of those districts and of
that time —and no other. Prof. Morris cheerfully
gives * Boldrewood’s ’ Vie. slang of 20 years ago
present-day and pan-Australian currency. E.g.—
Count-muster, «., a gathering, especially of sheep or
oattle in order to oount them.
1891. Rolf Boldrewood, * A Sydney-side Saxon,’ p. 1:
•The old man’s having a regular count-muster of his
gons and daughters, and their children and off-side rela-
tives—that is, by marriage.*
COUNT-MUSTER is rarely nowadays heard of —pro-
bably never beyond the S.E. corner of Australia.
The related terms ‘ book-muster ’ and ‘ bang-tail
muster,’and the generic and well-known ‘muster,’
Prof. Morris omits altogether.
Rager, »., an old and fierce bullock or oow, thit always
begins to rage in the stock-yard.
1884. Rolf Boldrewood, ‘ Melbourne Memories,’ o.
xxiv.. p. 105:
• Amongst them was a large proportion of bullocks,
which declined with fiendish obstinacy to fatten. They
were what are known by .the stock-riders as “ ragers ’’or
“ pig.meaters ’* ’ [g.v.].
Ragers is hardly known out of Vic., and is
obsolescent there. The reference to ‘ pig-meaters ’
blunders similarly. The best-known nowadays
term for oattle chat won’t fatten is ‘wasters’ —
which Prof. Morris omits altogether. ‘Piker’
and ‘scrubber’ are also omitted; but Prof.
Morris is indebted to ‘ Rolf Boldrewood ’ for
Sorub-danGX.ER, now very rarely heard in Vio.,
and never elsewhere. There are other similar
cases.
* * » *
Examples of (c) inadequate definitions—
‘ Scrub’—one of the most characteristic Austra-
lian words, Prof. Morris defines simply as
‘ country overgrown by thick bushes.’ Not to go
further, does this adequately describe mulga scrub
—which isn’t ‘bushes,’ but trees —or the scrub
of the N.S. W. northern rivers, or of N.Q. ? Would
not a foreigner receive from such a bald statement
an altogether false conception, of most of the
kinds of sorub ? And what is the editor of such
a Dictionary as Prof. Morris’s supposed to do, if
not to spend time and pains in making his defini-
tions full and true enough to fit all cases—just as
a law-oodifier spends time and brains in making a
new sentence which covers all the implications of
existing law? Certainly, Prof. Morris’s quota-
tions help to elucidate the meaning of scrub ; but
they are mutually contradictory, and a foreigner
wants an authoritative statement. Why doesn’t
he get it ? Why did not Prof. Morris submit his
stock terms to an experienced stockman, and his
bush terms to an educated bushman, instead of
issuing an imperfect or misleading definition with
all the weight of a * professor of English, French,
and German languages and literatures in the
University of Melbourne ’ ?
Under Bush, adj., is this note—
In Australia, the word ‘ inn ’ is now rare, the word
• hotel * has supplanted it.
Nothing about the ‘pub.’—which is practically
the only bush term in current use. 11a this and
other cases Prof. Morris seems hampered with
uneasy gentility—like that which, in H. A. Hunt’s
Sydney essay, refined the popular ‘southerly
buster ’ into ‘southerly burster,’ as more fitted for
ears polite. The spectacle of anyone sitting down
to compile an Australian slang diotionaxy with
kid gloves on his hands, wadding in his ears, and
blushes flooding his manly cheekß, would interest
H. Parkes and others.
•' * » »
[Just here it will be pleasant to rest the mind
awhile on a piece of verse by an anonymous writer
in the * Pall Mall Budget ’ of aforetime—
TO A BRETON PEASANT.
Tou bent to the sickle’s eager sweep,
A living note in a wordless song.
Tou held the spirit of all that reap
In your slight girl form, divinely strong,
As you stood in the last flare of the sun,
The harvest ended—the reaping done.
You were not, I think, made perfectly ;
Yet had you been flawed from head to feet,
That one strange hour in the dusk would he
More true than half that I deemed most sweet;
For the tide of the world raved far away,
And I was at rest in a windless bay.
you brought a sense of the breaking bud
To woo me baok to the old green things;
To you all life was a sea at flood,
Too full for vague imaginings,
And your wildest fancy never flew
Beyond the pale of the land you knew.
My feet are set upon other ways,
My hands reap best in the fields of men ;
You follow the course of quiet days,
Till the quiet twilights fall again ;
I strive to enter where none have trod,
You walk in the pleasant fields Of God.
And refreshed, returning—]
* • * w
Examples of ( d) erroneous definitions —
Tambaroora, n. a Queensland game. More generally
known as * A shilling in and the winner shouts.’’ From a
town in Queensland.
It is to be feared this is a Morris joke. The
essence of a present-day tambaroo. i is a sweep for
the purchase of drinks —frequently on the prin-
ciple that more liquor can be purchased wholesale
for Is. 6d. than six thirsty people can buy for 3d.
each. Hence ‘ tambaroora muster,’ when the
droughty party musters all the coin it’s possessed
of, and one individual goes and bargains for the
beer. In the towns, dice are usually dispensed
with. In the Q. bush, the name still has some-
thing of the older and gauiey significance ; but
the drink idea always predominated over the
game-idea. And the ‘town in Queensland’ hap-
pens to be in N.S. Wales.
Tanneiqrams, n. very recent New Zealand slang. On
Ist June, 1896, the New Zealand Government reduced the
price of telegrams to sixpence (slang, a ‘ tanner ’) for 12
words.
Probably nine-ten! hs of Maorilanders never
heard of Tanner Grams. The word may have had
a local and limited currency in Oamaru, whence
Prof. Morris extracts a reference ; but to apply it
to all Maoriland is utterly unsleading.
Caddie, n., a bush name for the slouch-hat or wide-
awake.- In the Australian bush the brim is generally
turned down at the back and sometimes all round.
This is type of a class of inept notes numerous
in Prof. Morris’s Dictionary. Caddie (more often
cady) is rare enough, and merely reproduces the
British cady. The word’s chief use is to give
hard-pushed bards a rhyme to lady. The ‘brim ’
remark is nonsense. Every man wears his hat as
bq pleases. If the brim generally turns down,
it’s because the hat is so old and feeble that the
brim won’t stay up on any consideration.
* * * *
By this time readers and Prof. Morris will have
a pretty fair idea where ’e are as a Dictionary-
maker. In addition to' his sins of commission
there are many of omission. What charming
notes, for example—with real historical and
national value—could be written on words like
sway! —concerning which Prof. Morris merely
remarks that it is ‘a tramp’s bundle, wrapt up iu
a blanket called a Bluey (q.v.). Used also for a
passenger’s luggage.’ The note to bluey is as
curt; and Matilda isn’t mentioned. Or what
a characteristic-Australian note; could be made
on “ sweep ’’ —altogether omitted. In scores
of similar cases Prof. Morris has failed to rise
to the height of bis opportunities. He may say
that the plan of his Dictionary didn’t contemplate
disquisitions, however curious and interesting.
All the worse for the Dictionary. Prof. Morris
would have been much bettfer employed in making
original research and comment than in amassing
whole pages of stuff like this —
Native Damson, n. See Damson.
Native Dandelion, n. S ee Dandelion.
Native Daphne, n. See Daphne.
Native Date, n. See Date-.
from Maiden and Yon Mueller and the rest. But
he seems to have tried—not how much, but—how
little original research and comment ho could put
into the book.
* * * •
All this doesn’t prevent the Dictionary from
containing much valuable information. The
references to book-use of words are : frequently
very full and helpful. Many of them, such as a
reference to Wattle-bark—an obvious word, of
little or no philological interest apart from the
substantive wattle—
-1877. [? Exact date lost,] * Melbourne Punch ’;- ,
* What’ll bark ? Why, a dog’ll.'
are inane and ridiculous. In fact, quotations
have been rammed in all-through as if the com-
piler’s object was to make a big volume to sell at
a high price—rather than a valuable book, without'
reference to price. But the quotations for ‘ squat-
ter,’ ‘brickfielder,’ ‘bush,’ and many other words
are most interesting. No fault can be found
because derivations of words like duffer (of cattle),
brumby (probably aboiiginal), jackshay (possibly
from one Jack rihea, the maker or’introducer),
billy, and many others, are not yet authoritatively
fixed. A man does what he can. And some of
Prof. Morris’s (or collaborators’) derivations are
excellent — e.y. , chyack, from chi-i-ke, an Austra-
lian pronunciation <>f the English verb to cheek;
post-and-rail, applied to tea, from the appearance
of the alleged tee-iwigs floating on top; box, as
applied to sheep ; and so on.
• *' * *
To sum up, where Prof. Morris and his assist-
ants have simply compiled 'and classified the
previous work of trained minds—as in the animal
and plant sections —-their work is good ; but these
sections should be set apart from the language-
section. As a guide to Australian language, the
Dictionary is worthless except to an Australian
expert who can supplement .with local knowledge
the makers’ numerous errors and omissions. To a
foreign reader, it is worse, than worthless in this
respect, for it is often positively misleading—
because insufficient brains and labor.and care and
time have been applied to it. A substantial body
of information lias, however, been collected, and
in the hands of capable . persons this collection
will have permanent value -as part of the basis of
an adequate dictionary of the Australasian lan-
gunge. _ YjgJ.- _

Hides and Tallow.
A STEVEDORING RHYME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here ain’t a lavender ditty,
Sung by a sweet-scented cove ;
Here ain’t no wine-inspired, witty
Story of ’onor an’ love :
Here is the song of the taller,
Likewise tbe chanty of ’ides—
Greasy an’ dirty an’ yaller,
Gritty an’ stinkin’ besides—
’ldes an’ taller ; taller an’ ’ides.
‘■Potes ” who ’ave nourished on roses,
Given to sippin’ of dew ;
“ Potes ” with susceptible noses,
This ain’t intended for you.
These are the lands that lie fallow,
Unploughed by the pens of romance ;
This is the ode of tee Tallow—
Odorous tallow perchance.
The whenceness of which an’ the whither
The creed of no church ain’t secure,
Old fancies an’ fashions may wither
One fact it is certain an’ sure —
There’s nothing smells worse nor the taller,
Always exceptin’ the ’ides;
Grimy an’ sweaty an’ yaller,
Greasy an’ gritty besides,
’ldes an’ taller, taller an’ ’ides.
The wool-bales is easy to lumber,
We know ’em, the same as a book ;
The clerk keeps his eye on the number,
You cop ’em right side with your hook.
You knows a damn dump when you spot ’im,
You ’ump ’im an’ truck ’im away,
A cask you’ll per-ceive when you’ve got ’im
Ain’t never constructed that way.
’E slips an’ ’e rolls an’ ’e shices,
’E bucks an’ ’e wobbles an’, worse,
’E jams an’ ’e rams an’ entices
’Ard-workin’ pore chaps for to curse.
Oh, damn all the product of taller,
An’ blast all the product of ’ides,
It’s ’eavy an’ dirty an’ yaller,
It’s greasy an’ stinkin’ besides,
’ldes an’ taller, taller an’ ’ides.
The thing that gits over a feller
Is kids and a missus to keep.
It don’t make ’is life none too meller,
It don’t much provoke 'im to sleep.
’E ain’t got no time to grow lazy,
’E’s got to look limber an’ slick,
Though taller ’ud drive a cove era- y
An’ ’ides make a feller go sick.
An’ wherefore an’ whyfore we’re lumpin’
Them products that’s awkwardly rolled ;
A thumpin’ our shin-bones an’ bumpin’
The same to their place in the hold.
If ’ell is as ’ot as they tell us
We needn’t be gallied by that,
The devils will strike when they Bmell us
A renderin’ up of our fat!
The preacher whose pulpit is furnished
With cushions of velvet and silk,
With bloomin’ brass-rails brightly burnished,
Who scoffs all the honey an’ milk,
’E often gets up an’ ’e preaches
A sermon on cussin’ and beer ;
On liver an’ bacon an’ peaches
’E guys us pore sinners down here.
But, Lord ! let’m rip off ’is cassock,
An’ peel to ’is sanctified pelt,
Give over ’is nice feather hassock,
An’ kneel where us jokers ’as knelt,
With sweat an’ ’ard graft for to halier
’ls soul an’ ’his body besides —
Contrition ain’t nothink to taller,
An’ prayin’ ain’t in it with ’ides;
’ldes an’ taller, taller an’ ’ides.
We ain’t much addicted to sorrow,
We’re given right over to slang.
It’s yakker to-day, an’ to-morrow
You’re smashed, an’ they don’t give a hang.
There’s Jones—’e was workin’ last Monday,
Cask rolled, an’ she pinned him ’longside ;
They’ll carve up ’is innards by Sunday
To find out the reason ’e died.
Them buyers is scoopin’ their profit,
It pays ’em right up to the hilt;
Champagne is their tap, an’ they scoff it—
The sellers don’t growl if it’s spilt.
But beer’s our old tank, an’ we booze it—
’Tis good for our “common ” insides ;
’Tis good for your soul if-you views it
All right through the taller an’ ’ides—
'ldes an’ taller; taller an’ ’ides.
This ugly four-master, ’e offers
A hold that’s as deep as the deuce,
The takings will bulge their fat coffers,
By gosh but we’ll stew in our juice ;
The mess-kids is smokin’ up for’ard,
My breakfast it mainly were bread,
That feel in your stummick is ’orrid,
It’s worse nor the feel m your ’ead.
By God ! if I’m tempted to leave ’er
To get one more sniff of the sea
My bloomin’ Ole Dutch were the griever,
It’s longshore an’ cuss it for me ;
It’s humpin’ the wool in hot seasons,
It’s rolling these casks in the cold,
It’s “ stand by the slings ” for good reasons,
Get graft, an’ more graft an’ grow old.
I’m clewed to four walls an’ a table
The chairs an’ the kids an’ the wife •
I’m petticoat tied an’ ain’t able
To kick for the old rovin’ life ;
I’m hitched to the wool an’ the taller,
The copra an’ sich like besides ;
I’m spliced to the bales an’ the taller,
The ’oms an’ the bones an’ the ’ides—
’ldes an’ taller, taller an’ ’ides.
I’ve got a spare judy out yonder,
I ’ad a nice gal in Bombay—
Wot’s Nelly a-doin’, I wonder !
I’ll cut my stick over some day;
By God ! were I just a bit younger
I’d slip in the turn o’ the tides,
This blessed ole tank she could ’unger
For me for ’er taller an’ ’ides,
’ldes an’ taller, taller an’ ’ides !
Sydney, 1897.
EDWIN J. BRADY.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
1 Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for-its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 18, 1897.
Concerning Some Old Legends.
When Manager Williamson recently staged
his semi-historical drama, 44 A Royal Divorce, ’ ’
in Sydney, it was noticeable that the Sydney
audience rose with a roar of joy at exactly
the point where the Melbourne audience
roared also, which, considering the remark-
able difference in the dramatic taste of the
two cities, was just a trifle unusual. And
the place where both cities roared was where
the lost Bonaparte, the night before Water-
loo, maundered about the stupendous—he
almost said the incredible—fighting qualities
of the English race. It was one of those
purple patches that delights the soul of John
Bull away in the foggy North Sea—and it is
quite natural and reasonable that it should.
In a fighting sense, even after making all
possible allowance for the good luck and the
clever diplomacy and the great lack of
scruple which helped him out of many dif-
ficulties, the Briton—both the part of him
which is Bull and the part which isn’t—has
a splendid record. It comes a good second
to that of Rome, and possibly wipes out that
of Assyria, which latter has become rather
blurred in the remote haze of history. And
the Briton is naturally proud of it with a
great pride, and lies about it immensely, to
make it even greater than it is. So does
every other nation, about its record ;
every race that means to become anything,
or to remain anything, must believe in
itself and its past; and no nation has a past
that can’t be improved by little judicious
suppression and a lot of judicious perversion.
Therefore the Briton lies about his achieve-
ments of necessity, like everybody else, and
there is probably no reason why he shouldn’t.
But why the Australian, who is as far away
from Bull as the size of the earth will allow,
and who has his own nation to construct,
and his own separate record to put up, and
who has left the Bull island and the
Bull traditions behind him, should want to
lie on the subject, or to help anyone else to
do so, is less easy of comprehension.
* * * *
For a long time back there has been a
demand for a new Australian history for use
in schools. An equally urgent need is for a
new British history for school use—one that
will tell the straight, unvarnished truth, or
as near to it as possible. The average Aus-
tralian still believes that it was Britain which
wiped out Napoleon, practically single-
handed, and he still regards it as quite
natural that a stage Bonaparte in William-
son’s drama should pour the grease of adula-
tion on the British lion. Yet Napoleon
had hardly even seen that animal up till then,
and he probably felt no respect for it at all.
And Waterloo never was the decisive battle
it has been cracked up to be; the lost
emperor, with his skeleton army, consisting
largely of conscripts, and his failing energies,
and his incurably diseased stomach, had
enemies pouring in on him from all sides,
and his chance, so far as can be seen at this
distance, hardly amounted to a row of pins.
Possibly he regarded the British as the
least of his troubles. He had been person-
ally licked by the Austrians, the Muscovites,
and the Prussians, and he knew them ; he
had never been wiped out by the British on
his own element, and they were probably
the one item in the programme which he
viewed with very little respect, and didn’t
make any eloquent remarks about at all.
* * * *
To the Briton it was Wellington and the
Peninsular war which wrecked the emperor.
It is difficult for him to understand that that
war was a mere diversion—a disastrous side-
scrimmage certainly, but yet only an out-
lying row on the remote flank of a great
campaign. Also, it is naturally difficult for
him to realise that the first man who de-
stroyed the spell of the emperor’s personal
invincibility on land was not Wellington
but the Archduke Charles of Austria when
he broke the greatest French army that had
been assembled until then at Aspern and
Essling, and made a drawn battle of Wag-
ram. With a less vacillating sovereign to
back him there was even a chance at that
time that he would have made a Waterloo for
Bonaparte outside Vienna. Also it is nowhere
mentioned in the history which feeds Bull’s
infant mind and the infant mind of young
Australia, that almost the worst single blow
of the Peninsular War itself was the capture
of an entire French army by Castanos at
Baylen and of a French fleet by the Spanish
patriots at Cadiz, or that the French power
in Spain had been broken once by Blake,
Castanos and Palafox before the British in-
vasion commenced. Whether it could have
been broken again without British assis-
tance no one knows, but even the great
Peninsular campaign wasn’t all Wellington,
or all John Bull, by a very long way.
* * * *
And, while this side-issue, which Napo-
leon regarded as of but little importance,
was being fought out in Spain, the doomed
emperor lost 500,000 men in Russia, and
330,000 more in the awful campaign which
Blucher and Schwartzenburg finished up
when they climbed over the corpse of the
empire into Leipsic. Nextyear, they followed
Napoleon into France and ended the empire
without Wellington appearing on the scene.
Possibly, if Wellingtoii had not been carry-
ing on his campaign in Spain, the French
troops which he kept occupied there might
have been sufficient to turn the scale at
Leipsic—or, again, they might not. Pos-
sibly the Spanish insurrection alone
would have kept them sufficiently em-
ployed. Or, possibly, if Blucher and
Schwartzenburg had not fought their
way over 100,000 dead into Leipsic,
Napoleon might have got into Spain himself,
and met Wellington, while he still had an
army worth calling such to meet him with,
and no one knows what the results might
have been. It was Wellington’s luck never
to run against Bonaparte until after the
Grand Army was practically buried and for-
gotten, and until its leader was far past his
prime, and also to carry on his campaign while
the bulk of the emperor’s gaudy legions were
employed many hundred miles away. Also
it was his luck not to appear until the wreck-
age of Aspern and Essling had partly broken
the spell of Napoleon’s personal invinci-
bility. He was a very great man, and an
amazingly fortunate one as well, and there is
no reason why John Bull shouldn’t regard
this brilliant Irish commander as the greatest
Englishman of his day, and teach Bull
junior that he wiped out the French Empire
all on his own. The dust of Blucher, and
Kutusoff ( who drank horribly), and Charles
of Austria (who used to have fits when he
was alive), and old Bulow, and Castanos,
and Blake, and Schwartzenburg, and
Benningsen (who had amazingly swollen
feet), probably doesn’t mind. Singly none
of them—except Blucher and Benningsen,
and Charles of Austria—amounted to very
much, and Wellington, anyhow, was the
biggest single dog in the pack of twenty or
more. If Britain likes to regard him as the
only dog in the pack it matters very little to
anybody.
* * * *
All the same, The Bulletin reckons that
Young Australia might as well get his history
undiluted. Wellington was Irish, and
Australia in the main isn’t, and this country
has no sacred call to lie about him. His
armies were a weird mixture of English,
Scotch, Irish, Welsh, Portuguese, Dutch,
German and miscellaneous. This country
was once mostly English, Irish, Scotch and
miscellaneous, but it is. steadily becoming
Australian (whether it will be better or worse
for doing so is another matter, but it has got
to be Australian and must needs make the
best of it). And when it does become Aus-
tralian it will take it all its time to lie about
its own history. Already, though it is very
young and has no war-record, it has a history
behind it which requires a good deal of able
lying to make it presentable, and it is only
fair to add that the owners of that history
seem quite capable of doing all the perjury
that is required—so long as the borrowed
money for the statues of its early gaolers is
available. Even 80 or 85 years ago, at
the time of the great Wellington legend,
Australia had a tale of its own which requires
all the romance that an unreliable record
can throw over it—a tale that was mostly
written in bits of mangled convict and a cat.
And when its educational system spends so
much valuable energy in casting a glamour
over the British doings in Europe, at a period
when the glamour was so badly needed for
local consumption, it somehow-seems a waste
of valuable inaccuracy.
Concerning the Cronk Assurance Office.
Australasia, of late years, has commenced to
work up an unsavory reputation as a haunt of
small cronk life-assurance companies. In this
respect it hasn’t yet begun to approach Britain,
which possesses some of the very best, and
many of the very worst and awfullest, assurance
institutions on this earth ; still, in the matter
of the life-assurance swindle, these provinces
are making very fair progress. The Bulletin
can lay a linger on three or four concerns now
assiduously gathering in the money of a confid-
ing public which, if the available evidence
counts for anything, a decent system of State
supervision should blot out of existence inside a
month. To all intents and purposes, Austra-
lasia has no life-assurance law whatsoever,
and any blatant swindler who advertises
largely, and gets in enough premiums to pay
death claims and expenses, and issues a lying
balance-sheet, can defraud the public for an in-
definite period with impunity. This particular
fraud, too, is likely to come into greater favor
as time goes on, because it can be carried on
longer with less assets behind it than any other
fraud of its size. The bogus bank may come to
grief anj 7 time when there is a run on the
deposits. The bogus assurance office can go on
for 20 years, or longer ; it only needs to close
when it can’t get in enough premiums, or
raise enough money on overdraft or by
pawning the office furniture, to pay some
claim, and the executors refuse to wait
for their money any longer. And, as
premiums can almost always be obtained if the
concern employs enough agents, and pays them
a large enough commission, and advertises
enough, and presents a good enough balance-
sheet, and declares large bonuses out of the
large imaginary profits, there is no reason why
the cronk assurance office should close up until
the liabilities are £1,000,000 and the free assets
only 2d. In fact, there is no reason why it
should close till the liabilities are £2,000,000
and the unencumbered assets only Id. Under
really able management, the only person who
could detect the real inwardness of the swindle
is the auditor, and The Bulletin doesn’t re-
member one single case in the Seven Provinces
where any private auditor ever detected any-
thing until after the smash.
• ♦ * *
Now and then some one so far realises the
blind foolishness of this system that a feeble
attempt is made at legislation on the subject.
But the public, which will apparently keep on
confiding till some big life offiee'goes broke and
buries millions of savings in the ruins, takes
very little interest in the matter. Also the
attempt, as a rule, is very weak and miserably
inefficient. A measure lately introduced by
politician Cross in Queensland Assembly
is a good sample of its kind. Cross’s Bill
starts by providing that every life assur-
ance office doing business in Queensland shall
deposit £5OOO with the Treasury as security for
the due payment of claims. This demand may
prevent the very smallest and meanest and
most squalid kind of assurance swindle from
starting, but it is a very, very small and squalid
concern which can’t raise £5OOO in some way or
other. Also, considering that it is a very small
concern that isn’t liable for at least £1,000,000
under its policies, the idea of £5OOO being any
kind of security to policy-holders is wildly
grotesque. Further, each company is to show
every year the receipts and the expenditure of
its Queensland business, and to deposit one-
fourth of its surplus receipts with the Treasury
until its deposit reaches £15,000. Considering
that a very ordinary insurance concern may
easily have a possible liability of £5,000,000
under its policies, the idea of protecting the
policy-holders to any extent worth mentioning,
by demanding even a £15,000 deposit, is also
somewhat grotesque. Undoubtedly this pro-
viso would make things a little better than they
are, but the improvement in many cases
wouldn’t amount to more than a few coppers in
the £.
* * * »
Cross’s proposed law also sets forth that an
assurance office may be wound up if five or more
shareholders or policy-holders make application,
and give security for an unlimited sum in costs,
and establish a prima facie case before they get
a hearing at all, and finally prove to the satis-
faction of the Court that the concern is insol-
vent. This is very good, in a limited sense ;
the trouble is that, under present conditions,
there is practically no means by which five
policy-holders, or even one policy-holder, can
find out w'hether the concern is insolvent or
not, and apparently the applicants have to
prove their case without access to the books or
papers or securities or any other evidence. It
is also proposed that, when the insolvent affair
is being wound up, all the Queensland assets
shall be seized for the exclusive benefit of the
Queensland shareholders. This is another
clause which doesn’t make the business any
more honest; it merely provides that after the
dishonesty is fully exposed the Queensland
creditor shall, if he gets the opportunity, rob
the N.S.W. and Victorian and other creditors
to the best of his ability. It is further pro-
posed that the accounts shall be presented in a
certain form, which is a remarkably clear and
definite one so far as it goes, and a number of
valuable ideas are embodied about the protec-
tion of policies from seizure by creditors, and
the filing and publication of accounts, and other
etceteras.
* * * .
Then Cross’s scheme stops short just when it
might have commenced to grow interesting,
and just when it was in danger of being of
some real use. It doesn’t declare that any
specific mortality table shall be used in reckon-
ing the liabilities; even the most insolvent
office can make itself solvent on paper bv
reckoning that its insurers will live long
enough, and there is a wide range for imagina-
tion in this respect. It doesn’t provide that
the future interest estimated to be derived
from the accumulated funds shall be not more
than a cast-iron maximum, and any office that
has any funds at all can make itself solvent on
paper by assuming that these funds will bring
in a large enough rate of interest. I.t is pro~-
vided that if the balance-sheet is incorrect in
any way, and it can be proved that the persons
signing it knew it was incorrect (a matter
which can hardly ever be proved, for it is next
to impossible for any person to legally demon-
strate what any other person was thinking
about), the signatories may be severely
puxrished. But there is no proviso that they
shall be punishable for signing a balance-sheet
which is grossly incorrect without their know-
ledge, said want of knowledge arising through
gross and shameful neglect of duty. And
except in the case of an unspeakably idiotic
person, there is never any evidence that he
knew of the fraudulent character of the bal-
ance-sheet ; he was always led away by his own
sanguine disposition, or he trusted entirely to
the assurances of somebody else, who was mis-
led by a third party, who relied with unques-
tioning faith on a fourth individual, who was
grossly deceived by some one who is dead, or
has gone away. What is wanted above all
things is a law which will make somebody who
isn’t dead personally responsible for things,
whether he says after the smash that he knew
them or not—on the sufficient ground that he
said before the smash that he knew all about
the business, and took his salary for knowing
all about it. Moreover, even in the above
limited sense, Cross’s little Bill makes no one
responsible for the value of the assets. It is
only necessary to state truly that the sum lent
on inortgages is (say) <£500,000 without any
obligation to add that many of the securities
have so far depreciated that a loss of £lOO,OOO
is distinctly in sight. Also, that loans on per-
sonal security are so much, without adding that
the personal security has just done the
Pacific slope. Also, that agents’ balances are
so much, with no obligation to mention that
some of the agents have completely disappeared
with their balances; and that shares and
debentures are a certain amount, without any
call to go into details as to the depreciation of
the shares and debentures since their purchase.
Further, the account is to state that interest in
arrear is so much, but this item doesn’t read
“ all interest in arrear.” Therefore, the
management, when £25,000 worth of interest
arrears rise up to show that the mortgages are
largely irrecoverable and that the loans on per-
sonal security are very insecure, may truthfully
put £5OOO under this heading and bury the other
£20,000 among the principal, or drop iw c ut
altogether.
* * * *
There is no provision for a State audit and
valuation in Cross’s scheme. There is also no
definite provision for any audit. If there is
an audit it isn’t provided that the auditors’certi-
ficate must say that things are right or wrong,
which means everything, instead of simply
saying that they are right according to the
books, which means nothing. There is no pro-
vision for any regular valuation of the securities
by any one. The directors are not called upon
to say in the balance-sheet that the assets are
worth so much, or even that they believe them
to be worth so much—and the mere statement
that mortgages are held to a certain amount
counts for nothing. If an institution bought
a J-acre allotment for £lO in the name
of the office-boy, and he then mortgaged
it to the institution for £lO,OOO in con-
sideration of £1 cash, it would be abso-
lutely true, so far as that transaction was
concerned, that the co. had £lO,OOO in mort-
gages, and the fact might be truthfully inserted
in the balance-sheet as that document is now,
and as it will still be in Queensland if the pro-
posed law comes into force. No politician in any
of the Australias has ever ventured to propose
legislation upon any of these points, mainly, it
would appear, lest he should be accused of
making base insinuations about the solvency of
this country’s big life-assurance societies. The
average Australian views these concerns with
awe, and is proud of them, and takes their
solvency for granted ; he would just as soon
th-ink of subjecting them to the indignity of
any real supervision as a pious Israelite of the
ancient time would have thought of aiming a
dead cat at the Ark of the Covenant. He had
the same sentiment once about his big and
glorious banks, the Queensland National among
the rest; but that is past now. By-and-bye the
present sentiment may pass also.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Berry Pension Comedy.
Probably no Premier except Turner would
have taken seriously the proposal that old
Graham Berry should be provided for by
passing round the hat among the Vic. legis-
lators. Also, it is probable that no one except
Turner supposed that the legislators intended
it to be taken seriously. The general feeling
was that Berry had to be provided for at
somebody else’s expense, because, if the grand
old warrior was allowed to drop from one
depth of poverty to another in his last days,
and w'ander in frayed raiment about the scene
of his bygone glories, the electors might so far
remember their Tribune as to say or do some


tiling unpleasant over the matter. I hey might
not subscribe handsomely for their fallen idol
themselves, but they would probably sympa-
thise in a cheap sort of way, next election time,
by hooting at the men who left Berry in the
lurch. No one, probably, really wanted to put
up his own money for Berry. No one had the
courage to cold-bloodedly say, in so many
words, “Chuck him out ! ” No one wanted to
take the direct personal responsibility of
saddling him on the taxpayer as a pensioner in
these times of retrenchment, but if Turner
was willing to take the responsibility any
number of politicians were willing to follow him
reluctantly, and explain afterwards, if neces-
sary, that they only did it to avoid breaking
up the party. But Turner flatly refused to
take the responsibility, as something that wasn’t
in the regular day’s work ; his tacit attitude
was that if the party would unanimously
resolve upon establishing an embarrassing pre-
cedent he might be driven into agreeing, but it
must be clearly understood that he was driven.
Also Turner refused to take the responsibility
of leaving Berry out in the cold, as something
that was also not in the day’s work ; he simply
proposed to be a private member of an amateur
benevolent society and lend a listening ear like
any other member. As for taking any definite
lead, that wasn’t in the day’s work. Under
these difficult circumstances the meeting
was driven into stating that it would gladly
sacrifice part of its own salary in the good
cause, though it didn’t look at all glad. The
idea probably was that, at the worst, the
voluntary hat would go round, and anybody
who liked could be judiciously absent that day,
and when the subscriptions fell off the State
would come to the rescue after all. But the
callous Turner either took or professed to take
the offer in dead earnest, and is introducing
a bill under which a percentage is to be
forcibly deducted from every member’s salary
to pension Graham Berry so long as he
survives. If Parliament doesn’t assent, all
manner of unpleasant things will be said about
the meanness of a very mean majority. If it
does assent, it won’t even have that inward
glow under the waistcoat which comes of volun-
tary benevolence; its benevolence will be
forcibly deducted from it every month. Pro-
bably the average member will vote for the
proposal with an inward hope that the other
fellows will vote against it, so that he can go
round afterwards and say that he wanted to
provide for old Graham Berry at his own
expense and was forcibly prevented. Parkes
would have simply revelled in the irony of the
situation, yet it is questionable if Turner even
sees the humor of it. Anyhow, he spoiled a
beautiful dramatic effect. If things had gone
right, when that generous offer was made
Turner should have risen with tears in his
voice, and said he was deeply touched by the
generosity of hon. members, but he really
couldn’L permit such a noble sacrifice, and he
felt it his duty to give way, and a vote would
be put on the estimates, &c. Then there would
have been cheers and drinks, and the meeting
would have presented a fine aspect of self-
sacrifice at no personal expense, and Turner
would have advertised his yearning for economy
at no expense also, and the Berry incubus
would have been off everybody’s mind. And
instead of doing his part gracefully Turner
didn’t even thank the meeting; he merely
went off in a grumpy, business-like frame of
mind, and drafted a dashed, infernal Bill.

Panton’s Logic.
In this country the man who blows down the
muzzle of the Law to see if it is loaded gener-
ally discovers that it is, and has his eye wrecked
in consequence. For a long time the Mel-
bourne rum-interest persistently objected to the
presence of magistrate Lormer on the bench in
licensing cases on the ground that Lormer,
being a teetotaller, was almost certain to suffer
from unconscious bias against publicans in
general. Solicitor David Gaunson was one
strong objector, and every time he appeared
before Lormer in a licensing case his voice was
raised in protest like the stentorian yell of
Alexander the Coppersmith. Finally, the
Supreme Court considered the case, and decided
that Lormer had better not adjudicate in such
cases. Then the Cold Tea party started.
Police - Magistrate Panton is alleged, on
good or bad authority, to be part - owner
of a vineyard somewhere, and so directly
interested in the wine industry—anyhow, the
statement about the alleged vineyard has never
been disproved. And, whether he has a vine-
yard or not, he is not a teetotaller, so far as
anybody knows, and a man who wants a little
liquor occasionally may easily have an uncon-
scious bias in favor of the publican. There-
fore, the Cold Tea party, quite logically
and respectfully, objected to Panton’s pre-
sence on the bench in a wine-license case.
Whereupon Panton exploded with a loud pop,
threatened to commit the objector for contempt,
said he had been insulted, and hove terms like
“blackguard” and “scoundrel” round pro-
miscuously, and, in addition, said the objection
was an outrage and the objector a “ creature,”
and that the Court had been “polluted.”
Therefore, apparently, it is one thing when a
Victorian grog-dealer hints at possible bias on
the part of a teetotal beak, and quite another
when a teetotaler hints the same thing
at a non-teetotal beak. Panton was, to
all intents and purposes, the defendant, on a
charge of “ unconscious bias.” And, instead of
the case being argued in a sober fashion, the
defendant was allowed to decide in his own
favor without a hearing, and slang the plaintiff
as well, and even to threaten him, by implica-
tion, with awful consequences. Then why
didn’t Victorian law simply settle the charge
against Lormer by letting him decide in his
own favor, with strong language thrown in ?
The spectacle of Panton deciding, in a whirl-
wind of rageful adjectives and abuse, that he
(Panton) is a perfectly calm and equal-minded
individual, with a judicial mind, is a trifle
ridiculous.
Another Shindykit.
The other day the- Lower House of the Q.
Boodle Parliament passed the second reading of
a Bill authorising the construction of a private-
enterprise railway from Mareeba to Chillagoe,
N.Q. Mareeba is the present terminus of the
brief but celebrated Cairns swindle-line, so if
the extension should also turn out disastrously
to the public exchequer, there will be no want
of concord and harmony about the matter, any-
way. The shindykit of the suggested line
announces that it wishes to extend the Cairns
railway due west about lOOmiles to theChillagoe
district, where the same shindykit owns leases
of valuable tin, lead, and copper-mines,
which leases it can’t work profitably
for want of carriage to the seaboard.
Its leases total 2000 acres ; and at present it
is supposed to pay 10s. an acre rent, and is also
supposed to employ one man per five acres—
unless it can evade the painful necessity by
exemptions and Ministerial concessions while
still monopolising the land. Under the new
Bili the shindykit proposes to annul the labor
conditions, and pay—or dodge payment of—<£l
an acre rent. Taking a miner’s wages at the
loeally-current rate of £3 a week, its present
liability is ,£62,400per annum, plus rent ,£1000;
and it cheerfully offers to waive this liability in
consideration of paying ,£2OOO per annum altoge-
ther! This is so very like a shindykit. Further,
the shindykit is to have land for its line and
stations given free, and is to be allowed to
charge half as much again as the State railways
charge for freight and passengers ; and its
monopoly of the mineral leases and the line
without further taxation is to be assured for
50 years, at the end of which time the Govt,
may, if it chooses, buy the line for whatever it
may then be worth. Perhaps the most amazing
thing about these proposals is the fact that
the shindykit apparently doesn’t intend to put
up any money or take any risk whatever. It
merely proposes that Q. Boodle Parliament
should give it for three years, in considera-
tion of its promise to build the line at the
end of that time, what it apparently re-
gards as a highly-valuable concession; and,
during these three years, the shindykit
may hawk its concession round the money
markets of the world, and sell, if it can, to a
bigger shindykit; and if, at the end of three
years, not a pick has been pu-t in the ground,
the project apparently drops dead, and the
shindykit is not a stiver the worse, and no line
is built after all. As it isn’t clear whether the
abrogation of the labor conditions is or is not
to follow immediately on the passing of the
bill, the shindykit may really design to be some
hundreds of thousands of pounds the better—
that is, it may, so far as can be understood,
monopolise its leases on specially profitable
terms for three years without fulfilling the
special conditions, and without being liable to
be called to any account. This confidence of
the shindykit in its own bonct-Jides is a remark-
able circumstance.
Tlie Fatuous Fat Men.
The great and wonderful incapacity of the
N.S.W. Legislative Council never stuck out
more conspicuously than it did last week. The
Upper House was undoubtedly in an unplea-
sant mood to begin with ; it had just been told,
for the several dozenth time, that it was an
incubus and an anachronism, and that the
mendiug or ending of it would be the Minis-
terial war-cry at next election. In addition to
this, it doesn’t like Premier Reid at any time,
and would gladly see that artful dodger of
finance run into a corner. And then a great
opportunity came to it to run Reid into a
corner, and burst his finance into small pieces,
and damage his reputation, and get for itself
the biggest and best advertisement it ever had,
and thereby discredit Reid’s schemes for
Council reform—and it made a horrible mess of
things, as usual. It had two finance bills before
it—the Loan Bill and the Probate Duties. The
former was an infamy of the worst description ;
it was a scheme for charging to posterity about
,£BOO,OOO of ordinary expenditure in order to
make a bogus appearance of solvency and keep
the Free-raid myth afloat; but it didn’t per-
sonally injure the pockets of councillors. And,
though every private member of the Council
who spoke on the subject denounced this great
swindle in the strongest language, not one
voted against it. There wasn’t even a division.
The Council, by its own showing, allowed a
swindle of the worst kind to pass without
serious opposition, and thereby advertised
itself as an institution with neither soul nor
conscience. The Probate Duties Bill did affect
councillors, or their families, personally ; the
Council spoke against it much less vigorously
than it did against the Loan Bill—and threw it
out by an enormous majority. If the N.S. W.
Upper House had known when to came in out
of the wet it would have practically reversed
the order of proceeding. It would have in-
sisted on amending the Probate Duties Bill to
bring it at least partly in line with the Victorian
Act (as Premier Reid distinctly promised to
adopt the Victorian system it had a fair excuse
for doing this); in other words, it would have
insisted, in addition to the £lOOO exemption
already granted, on letting the widow and
children off at half-rates when the estate was
valued at less than (say) £15,000 or <£20,000.
As the Victorian limit is £50,000 it would thus
have been keeping Reid to less than half of his
promise. Then it would have passed the Bill
with that amendment, and laid its jewelled
hand on its large white waistcoat, and drawn
attention to the fact that it wasn’t trying to
save its own big estate, but only looking after
the moderate-sized property of the “ widder
and the horphan.” Thereby it would have
widely advertised Reid’s broken promise and
its own disinterestedness, and also left its
enemy in a considerable difficulty about re-
venue. Then it would have throw'n out
the Loan Bill with a deafening bump, on
the ground that the practice of charging
£BOO,OOO a year of current expenditure to loans,
and thus laying huge burdens on posterity, W'as
a species of profligacy which was past endur-
ance. That would have left Reid financially
stranded, and it w'ould have drawn the coun-
try’s attention to his utterly insolvent financial
policy at a very awkward time. Under these
circumstances, the real issue before the country
at the general election would have been the
right of the Government to charge posterity
with new crockery for the Jenolan accommoda-
tion house, and things like that, as “permanent
and reproductive works,” and probably Reid
v >uld have gone down wallop, and a new
Government come in which would have taken
the Probate Duties off again. Now the issue is
the right of the Chamber of Fat Men to reject
taxation proposals which affect its particular
class—and probably that war-cry will save
Reid. Premier Reid has already started to
work up general hostility against the Upper
House by withdrawing the municipal subsidy
on the ground that the Council has refused to
supply him with revenue; whereas if the
Upper House had instead refused to supply him
with loans, and he had then withdrawn the
municipal subsidy, he would only have rubbed
in the damaging fact that the subsidy was
really paid out of borrowed money. A House
which misses opportunities like these deserves
extinction.

On Behalf of the Undeserving Widow.
King O’Malley, who might be a useful and
influential Democratic legislator but for his
awful eloquence and his bursts of gas, has a
bill before S.A. Assembly providing that a
widow shall have an inalienable claim on one-
third of her late husband’s estate, no matter
what will he may have made to the contrary.
Curiously enough, O’Malley forgot to make
any similar provision for the young children of
the deceased. Still, his idea is a good one, so
far as it goes. No matter how much the widow
may deserve to be disinherited, it generally
boils down to this : she must either be sup-
ported out of her husband’s estate or out of the
estate of the general taxpayer. And no matter
how sinful or how horrid she may have been to
her husband during their married life, he at
least enjoyed the illusion of thinking her an
angel before they were married, which the
public didn’t. Also, it was he, not the public,
who made the mistake of marrying her.
Further, he promised to support her, and
the public didn’t. And, if she did turn
out a she - fiend, and kept him all his
life in misery and subjection, he isn’t wholly
deserving of sympathy ; if he hadn’t married
her some one else probably would, and that
other person might have been large enough and
fierce enough to keep her in subjection and
make her a model female of the most humble
and subdued description, and by rushing into
a contract for which he was wholly unfitted the
deceased probably, to some extent, caused the
lady to turn out badly. Even if the widow
drinks horribly she ought, at least, to have a
claim on enough of the defunct husband’s*
estate to supply her with a domicile to drink in
so that she won’t be more of a public eyesore
than is really necessary. Also, she should have
a claim on enough of the estate to enable her to
pay for her own gin ; a red-nosed and hair-
tangled widow who has to cadge for her gin is a
public nuisance. So far as The Bulletin can
see all the logic is with O’Malley’s view of the
case.
Stoush.
Lyne’s abrupt offer in N.S.W. Assembly to
personally bash Premier Reid may possibly be
a sign of assault and battery to come. The
growing desire in some parts of the House to
bash Reid almost anywhere arises mainly from
that unmannerly person’s habit of constant
interruption. When he is in really good form
he breaks in upon an opponent’s speech about
twice a minute, and if the orator is a con-
scientious, laborious man he is led away from
his point in pursuit of fleeting interjections, and
beguiled on to side issues, something after the
fashion of a dog that tries to run simultaneously
after ten rats all vanishing in different direc-
tions. After a quarter of an hour of this sort
of thing he doesn’t-catch anything and sees the
tail of the last rat vanish round the corner, and
the thread of his discourse is hopelessly gone.
There is no man in the House who can match
Reid at this kind of mischief, yet there is
hardly one who couldn’t lay him out in five
seconds if he could get a straight rush at the
Premier’s stupendous bingy. Lyne’s sudden
yearning to get back to savagery and bounce
a huge volume of “Votes and Proceedings”
on his enemy’s helpless corporation was the
yell of the undressed primitive man anxious
to return to first principles. For three years
Reid has mercilessly baited his unwieldy oppo-
nent, who is big enough, if he only got loose on
the foe in a free scrimmage with chairs, to make
every portfolio, save Jack Want’s, vacant
inside 10 minutes. Ten years ago, when the
N.S.W. Assembly was more aboriginal than it
is now, he might have settled a long score by
simply stoushing Reid in the refreshment-
room, when that ever - ravenous statesman
dropped in at 11 p.m. to have his little snack
of six chops and the best part of a loaf, and two
large plates of something else; but these free-
and-easy days have gone by.
At Cobar (N.S.W.), a few days ago, 19 em-
ployes of the Cobar Gold Mines Co. were fined
ss. each for working on Sunday. Previous to
this the co.’s battery, which is situated a mile
or two out of town, pounded away on the Day
of Rust just the same as on any other day, but
the Sabbath Observance law has now brought
it up short. But right in the town of Cobar
the smelters of the Great Cobar copper-mine
roar seven days in the week, and the grimy
toiler trucks the ore and hoists it into the fur-
nace on Sunday just the same as on any other
day, and thereby the people going to church
have their thoughts disturbed from peaceful
contemplation of the butcheries done by the
ancient Israelites. And no one, so far as The
Bulletin has heard, has even proposed to pro-
ceed against this co. for wreckage of the blessed
Sabbath. And Minister of Justice Gould is a
large shareholder in the enterprise which thus
disturbs the Sunday calm with impunity.
♦
Albert Norton, M.L.C., a discredited Queens-
land politician of the old MTlwraitii clan,
bolds a brief now for the bloated squattah.
He has been a bit of a coastal cattle-squattah
himself. “We want our expired and expiring
leases renewed for 10 years,” is the burden of
Norton’s cry. “We won’t stock up on annual
leases. The country we want won’t be asked
for, for close settlement, for years and years.”
In that case, of course, an annual lease, with
six months notice, is as good as a tenure for
years and years.” As a matter of fact,
Minister Barlow tried the same dodge and told
the same tale in ’95, and failed. The land which
-Barlow declared no one but the squatter would
take at any price was thrown open and eagerlv
taken up to the very last acre. “Anyhow ”
continues Norton, “the squattah should be
given a pre-emptive right to a grazing-farm on
ins run, instead of being only made equal to a
common stranger. A ‘ gross injustice ’ has been
done to the squattah by not including such a
clause in the Bill.” The wail of the insolent
cattle magnate about the gross injustice done
to him is interesting. He made a bargaijj
with the Sta.te. The State has faithfully
adhered to its bargain, and the Queens*
land squatter is now a sturdy mendicant
howling truculently at the gate, and de-.
manding concessions which were not in the
bargain and which were never paid for. When
Barlow, in 1895, proposed renewing the leases
of the coastal squatters for 10 years, and block-
ing settlement till well on in the twentieth
century, the Assembly threw out the proposal
with both feet. But the huge pastoralist
lamented at the gate and implored his suffering
country to “gib it tikpence ” ever since, and
the Leg. Council was so moved the other day.
by Norton’s woe and its own, that it inserted
a clause in the new Land Bill granting the ten
years’ renewal.

A Victorian State school teacher thus de-
livers himself in a letter to the Age on the
Scripture lesson question : —“All teaching
worth the name is dogmatic. In mathematics,,
grammar, geography, etc., we are nothing ife
not dogmatic, and religion when divided from!
dogma is nothing better than a body without ai
soul.” Here we have a good old orthodox
view, such as might have come from the lips bf
a bisliop. But it sounds odd from those of-a
schoolmaster. Who should know better than
he that the dogmatics of fixed sciences, such as
mathematics, are based upon evidence that is
incontestable ? Geography and mathematics
have been built up by experience, analogy, and'
proven evidence. But can the same be said of
“religion?” Does not all dogmatic religion
rest upon tradition, and does any one really
know what the tradition rests upon? Is there
a more doubtful thing than the “ trewth ” as.it
is taught by the dogmatic parson ? No sensible
man dogmatises very hard about history—at
least about remote history, which is always
shifting and changing, and having new lights
cast upon it. And a great deal of the Bible is
the remotest and least corroborated and most
incredible-looking history extant. :
+
Has anybody ever been curious enough to
analyse the Crown theory of “ design,” upon
which the whole case against Suffield rests,
and failing which it falls, like Lucifer ? The
Crown, not finding it convenient to disregard
the evidence of Mrs. Warren and her daugh-
ter while it was supported by that of James
Warren, had to accept Suffiei.d’s change of
clothes as a proven fact. Now, Suffield stated
that he changed his clothes for the following
reasons : —(1) He had to go to work some time
that night. (2) Prior to going to work' he was
tired and feeling out of sorts. (3) It was a hot
night: his working clothes were spotlessly
clean, soft, and cool. (4) So he put them on to'
lie down in, not only to save his good clothes, ;
but because they were more comfortable. The'
Crown lawyers obviously couldn’t accept reasons
so simple and natural. If they did, their case
was at an end. No ! Suffield changed his
clothes as part and parcel of his nefarious
design to waylay and rape Ann Chambers.-.
That is, he actually donned a pair of clean-
white moleskin trousers in which to perpetrate'
a horrible and gory outrage by night ! The'
Crown didn’t suggest what particular ad van-'
tage was likely to accrue to Suffield’s “de-
sign ” from his assumption of the white unmen-
tionables. Can anybody else ?
The Suffield Case.
The Bulletin will be glad to receive (with’
date, &c., attached) marked copies of or clippings'
from any newspapers containing comments on the
Suffield case.
Summing-up on the Suffield case, Judge
Windeyer glossed over the high testimonials as to
the character of the accused, volunteered by
respectable and independent witnesses. Suffield,
alleges that he bluntly directed the jury to dis-
regard this testimony, on the ground that the
witnesses “must be mistaken in their estimate of
the accused man’s character.” Why ?
Writes Mr. W. 0. Hodgkinson, formerly Q.
Minister for Mines and Education, and long a 1
police-magistrate and goldfields-warden
Sydney, 26th November, 1897. '
The Editor Bulletin.— Sir,—l have carefully read the’
Suffleld-ease pamphlet. My experience as a magistrate
and, may I add, student of sexual physiology, has long
since taught me how cautious oneshouid be in matters of
this kind. Take away Ann Chambers’ hysterical, incon-
sistent statements, give proper authority to the evidence,’
(unimpugned except by the cowardly sneers of the Judge)
of the Warrens, allow in a case of such doubt due weight
to SufSeld’s unblemished character, and what remains ?
“ Nothing but a voiceless bull ” and a further page in the
black records of Botany Bay judicature. Cannot some of-
the thousands who impartially applaud our athletes
clamor for fair play for a man eating out his soul with
perhaps the iron of injustice searing its very fibres? I
trust that The Bulletin will wrestle with this apathy so
disgraceful to a community which has the remedy in its
own hands, and on the eve of a general election has only
to demand the common justice of enquiry to make every
official reed tremble before the blast of popular indigna-
tion. It is strange the precedent, to legal mind so dear,
of Europa was not quoted. It is a classic instance based
on overwhelming evidence, vide Lerqprifere.
“The accused,” said Judge Windeyer, referring to Suf-
field in the dock, “ I regret to say, does not impress me
favorably. lam of the opinion that,, certainly possessed
of some manly advantages, he has been living in the
neighborhood of the Latnbtons, and using these advan-
tages for the purpose of seduction.” Not a shadow of
evidence to support this supposition. And where were
the arts of the professional seducer, according to the
story of the prosecutrix ? A brief “ Good-night! ” a crash-
ing blow on the head, with “ a fist”—“ a stick”—or “ a
stone” !
Relevant to the Suffield case. Baron Bramwell (at his
jubilee anniversary celebration) : “ In the course of my
long career, I have found that 70 per cent, of the accusa-
tions of women against men have been proved to be lies ;
4 per cent, of the remaining 30 percent, have not been
proved to be true. It is very hard to disprove such
charges.” “The last of the Barons” also spoke on that
occasion of “the amiable imbecility of legislators who
take the part of women against men.” “It is the men
who should be protected.”
