At Sydney Lyceum on Saturday night the
new curtain with the boot advertisement on it rose
in the presence of a stupendous audience. It was
a bushy audience, which had mostly come down
from the dingo’s lair to see the cricket-match, and
was taking ‘ Arrah-na-Pogue ” in passing. It was
also an audience which breathed the breath of
gum-tree and wept copiously over the old Irish
drama, and it never noticed the dreadful anti-
climax at the end of each act. Every act in
“ Arrah-na-Pogue ” winds up with a strong
situation especially the first one and then,
when the audience had just melted into one
unanimous snuffle, a curtain descended on which
they were implored in large letters to try Hunter’s
boots. The management which puts up a boot
advertisement at such a moment may have a lot
of business instinot, but it has no poetry in its
soul.
• • • •
Grattan Riggs is taking a short rest at pre-
sent, and John O’Neill fills the part of Shaun, the
Post. O’Neill is a moderately brief man of enor-
mous girth, with a reliable brogue and much
humor. He tackles his Shaun with the largest
corporation that was ever brought to bear on the
}>art within The Bulletin’s knowledge or recol-
ection —also with the most stupendous treble
chin on record. And he carries the audience with
him all through ; even in the pathetic parts it
weeps with him instead of weeping at his mighty
stomach as might have been expected ; so the in-
ference is that O'Neill is an Irish comedian of no
mean order. The man who can throw so terrific
a weskit into a drama and make it go all the time
inspires respect. J. Driscoll Foley is another dis-
tinct success of the orthodox kind as the Informer
—the small rag of a man who never straightens
his knees or his back, and who rubs his two loamy
hands together incessantly. The small informer is
hallowed by time and long usage, but all the same
the present complainant would like to see ; a
long, gaunt one for a change ; surely the in-
former grew up occasionally. Miss Julie Woodville,
who toils after the character of Arrah, has an un-
even brogue, and acts somewhat patchily, and
looks as pretty as a picture ; also Miss Maud Wil-
liamson is reasonably good throughout ; and
Diver, who has made many good villains, doesn’t
make any too good a hero, though the gorgeous
he-millinery of last century suits him well. But
as long as the comic bhoy and the informer are in
good hands nothing can go very far wrong with
any Irish drama.
* * * •
The MacMahon management gets in early
•with its pantomime by opening on Christmas Eve
(Friday). Said panto, is supposed to be about
“ Dick Whittington and his Cat at least, it will
be as much about that person and his animal as
any pantomime is about anything. The main
fact is that Fanny Liddiard and the shapely Marie
Luella, whose limbs are a poem, are to take the
leading parts.
*m * m
The “ Royal Divorce ” ends its career at
Her Majesty’s on Saturday. The latter days of this
show were e'-en more prosperous than the early
ones, and only the facts that the co. is booked for
a Maoriland tour, and that the pantomime is due
on the 27th, prevents Bonaparte retreating from
Moscovv indefinitely. The Firm this year is going
back to panto, on the orthodox lines with “Babes
in the Wood,” and it hopes, if possible, to break
its past Xmas records in the matter of frill and
glitter and pictorial splendor. The “ Babes ” are
an old subject, but nowadays the subject of a
pantomime is so completely snowed up by the
miscellaneous accessories that it isn’t of the
smallest'consequence. Even if there was only
one subject, and all the theatres used it every
Xmas, it would be just the same. Some further
details about the bushed offspring may be looked
for in next issue.
** * *
Addie Conyers is now at Sydney Tivoli,
and the Kellino family of acrobats, who are
alleged, upon the management’s most solemn
colonial oath, to be engaged at a salary of j£L5O
a week, appear there on Saturday. There is a
certain freshness about this frank revelation of
the star’s salary, but the present writer rather
likes the new departure. Therefore he hastens to
mention that his salary is £165 a week, and, if a
bowelless management doesn’t increase it forth-
with, there is going to be trouble. The Haytors
and the loose-legged Wootwell are still at the
Tivoli, which is girding up its loins for an unusual
effort at Xmas.
** * *
Sydney Royal, which has been deserted
for quite a long time now, opens with “Jack, the
Giant-Killer ” under Harry Rickards’ manage-
ment on December 23. Rickards thus gets in a
day ahead of the Lyceum panto, and four days in
advance of Her Majesty’s. George Lauri, the
Haytors, the loose-legged comedian, and Misses
Addie Conyers and Lottie Moore are to be the
principal gems in Rickards’ constellation. How
Sydney will stand three pantomimes falling upon
it at once the Lord only knows.
** * *
For the present week, however, the Royal
is deserted, and the Criterion, Palace and Opera
House are in the same condition. So far, nobody
has announced any intention of opening the three
last-named, even at the season of the murdered
fowl when all manner of things are liable to
happen in the theatrical way.
** * *
All this time “The French Maid” has
been doing an extensive business at Melbourne
Princess’s, and the last two nights of that harm-
less fabrication will be rushed with mild enthu-
siasm. “The French Maid ”is less original than
the previous sample of broken-English humor,
but Melbourne seems to like it better. Pro-
bably the new people, Miss Ada Reeve and
Bert Gilbert, have grown in local favor since
they enlarged their sphere of usefulness and
let themselves go in an old-fashioned five-
act duet. It is much the same irrelevant duet
that other couples have treated for the last fifty
years, nevertheless the present revival has a cer-
tain air of striving to work on its own, and the
performers are distinctly brighter than the average
variety pair. “The French Maid” finishes on
Friday, and therewith a prosperous season of
cerulean jokes will cease to be. After which
Wilson Barrett.
** * *
Saturday is the appointed time for tho
first Australian appearance of Wilson Barrett and
his company of 23 souls, who will introduce them-
selves to the natives of this wild, untutored
country in “Claudian,”a play famous for a first-
class earthquake, and many other matters. Mr.
Barrett brings with him, in addition to the co., a
valuable personal celebrity as the author of the
“ Cross” drama, and unless the public feel dis-
appointed at not finding a halo round his level
head he will get a gigantic reception.
** * #
The calm that is apt to precede the Xmas
storm was a good deal in evidence at Melbourne
Royal last week, consequently “Trilby” bur-
lesque made way for “ Struck Oil ” on Saturday.
The season when a revival of Lizzie Stofel
wouldn’t fill the Royal has not yet been arrived
at, and the pulse of the treasury chinks health-
fully once again. Maggie Moore is always
young enough for the figure of the leading
lady in this frequent narrative, if we
make due allowance for the fact that Miss
Stofel was Dutch on her father’s side. Anil when
the popular institution comes to “The Chinese
Question,” and appears as a Chow maiden in black
overalls, The Bulletin is almost prepared to swear
she wasn’t more than 17 last birthday. Harry
Roberts nowadays gives a really strong perform-
ance of John Stofel in his Van Winkle stages.
The white-haired crank is more refined and less
aged than he ought to be ; he is wanting in the
suggestion of a fat man dwindled by sickness, but,
all the same, the power to hold an audience silent
as the grave for several minutes is in H. R.
Roberts, and he scores heavily. J. P. West, as
the oily Deacon, is likewise a very worthy sup-
port. “Struck Oil ” will hold the floor till Satur-
day night only, next week being required for
active rehearsals of the pantomime.
*# * *
The Biograph Vaudeville show concludes
a month’s run at Melb. Bijou on Friday. A
vision of V.R.’s Jubilee garden-party, also a
tunnel scene and Niagara, have been added to the
Bi. series, and the programme otherwise intro-
duces new matter in ail directions. The local
nobilities, headed by Gov. Brassey, were due at
the Bijou on Wednesday evening, and any mem-
bers of the common herd who wish to sample a
highly superior variety entertainment ere it passes
hence had better hurry up. The Opera House
show, which goes on as though no opposition had
happened, is rejoicing in the recent arrival of
Austin Rudd, a comedian who manufactures his
own songs and makes original grimaces whilst you
wait. Rudd is all right. Phil Newbury will
leave the Opera House shortly, but three gymnasts
from Paris and London are coming to fill the gap.
#* * *
Melbourne’s periodical craving for ivild
beasts, and beautiful ladies on bare-backed ’osses,
etc., is now being appeased by Harmston’s
Circus and Zoologia, a solid concern that pays
prompt cash to the printer and turns electric
light upon the arena. The first performance
of Harmston’s circus was fixed too late for
mention in this issue. Judging from the
magnitude of the tents and the dimensions of
the colored posters, and the florid eloquence of
agent Joe Brown, the entertainment ought to get
some favorable mention next week. Tho site
chosen for the new canvas town is a block of
desert land on the suburban side of Prince’s
Bridge, where Fillis pitched his tent in former
days: and the tramway co. offers special travelling
facilities to patrons.
At Poverty Point.
Haresfoot: “ Adelaide steadily goes down to the be-
nighted state of Geelong, where the theatre was first
turned into a brewery, and then into the Salvation Army
barracks. ”
Melb.: “The militia, in three nights’ terrific musketry,
over the Exhibition Tattoo, have not killed more than
half-a-dozen little ones in the Children’s Hospital
opposite.”
Haresfoot: “ Mr. H. R. Roberts’s Svengali is decidedly
pleasantei than Fax’s. I want to know why Roberts is
not in London as a rival of Seymour Hicks, and that
crowd, not to speak of the great Arthur Roberts, who
can no more live for ever than King Ahasuerus. Roberts's
Svengali indicates just the type of man in demand for
the fashionable musical comedy, nor would he be far
abroad in romantic drama of the ‘ Red Robe ’ stamp.”
Theorbo : “ Napoleon Boffard is quite the Melb. reign-
ing tenor, if Newbury be only regarded as a songbird of
passage. When Boffard was first announced I took him
as some masquerading joke, but he is a great reality.
This new Napoleon is a handsome, fattish little Victor
Emmanuel-looking fellow, or, say, a cross between that
monarch and Beaney. His being French seems to be one
strong point. A more palpable one is that he‘introduces
a sheaf of rattling French songs, the repertory of
Massenet, Saint-Saens, and the like, after our sickening
nauseation of ‘ Come into the Garden, Maud,’ * Death of
Nelson,’ and all that.”
Hilda Spong's part in the “Children of the King,” at
London Court Theatre (writes a Bulletin correspondent),
is entirely unsuited to her, and she accepted it against
her own conviction of its unsuitability. The piece is a
failure for adults, hut is to he tried as a juvenile attrac-
tion. Hilda has a six months’ engagement from the
lessee, Arthur Chudleigh.
The production of “ Franoillon ” at the Duke of York’s
Theatre has not found a happy family at that little house
(writes a Bulletin correspondent from London). Some-
how or other Arthur Elwood and Giace Noble did not
tender the most sympathetic support, and Nellie
Stewart's love of millinery induced her to tie knots of
ribbon on the furniture used in the stage setting, and so
spoil a really beautiful suite of real Louis yuinze. Then
the critics, even those —and there were many—who
acknowledged Mrs. 8.-B. as a leading actress, abused l.er
rendering of this play, which was too light to he drama
and too heavy to be comedy. Cora and Kyrle are going
to jaunt into the provinces presently and see how they
can manage “ on their own.”
May Glendenning and Herbert Sherwin lately ran two
concerts in Hobart. Miss Glendenning was in splendid
voice, but the public response for both concerts was
limited. Slowbart isn’t distinctly musical. Yet May
Glendenning should he good enough value for anybody's
money.
G. B. Allen, the best musician and composer who
has endured Brisbane for any consecutive number of
years, died, the other day, of kidney disease, aged 75
years. He was a sweet composer and a thorough musi-
cian, and in his day a fine vocalist. Of late years he has
been in the cold shade of restiLted means.

The Jubilee “cine." got a good, involuntary testi-
monial in Melbourne. A man who was once personally
acquainted with the Vic. Premier ran up to the screen
with extended hand and called out as the shadow went
by : “ Hello, George, old man.” He was quitp disgusted
when George took no notice of him, and went back to his

All the different people who are running cinematographe

Dear Bulletin,— Fancy old dead-and-gone Joe Dias
being described as “ the low comedian par excellence of
very old Bendigo and Ballarat,” and so on. The poor old
fellow was simply the traditional prompter —the old,
plain, ordinary, unvarnished prompter— of the Victorian
stage (Melbourne and Ballarat), dating from the late 40 s ;
and his steadiness and, later on, his antiquity, obtained
respect. “Old Bendigo and Ballarat ” that" is where I
come in—scarcely appreciate the idea that Joe Dias was
their favorite comedian of old days. — Ballarat.
Antoinette Sterling retains her old habit of firing
off “ Lost Chords ”at the weak and weary. After
singing, recently, at a Wesleyan chapel service, she heard
that several of the choir were absent through a typhoid
epidemic. Thereupon she visited the sick choristers, and
sang at each bedside. Result not known up to date.
Ada Ferrar, interviewed by Sunday Times, is reported as
saying : “ 1 have never had any experiences, either
amusing or otherwise.” “ I have never been taught any-
thing, except in the school of experience.” Further on :
“ The weather in the Bay of Biscay was awful; everything
was upside-down. After that experience, I intend to go
home again overland ! ”
Theorbo: “On the demise of Gladstone Wright, the
Melbourne musical coterie received its greatest shock
since Federici’s fatal disappearance through the trap, as
Mephistopheles. Wright tried England lately, but was
hardly good enough to cope with the many men of calibre
there in the basso line.”
Elsie Lander (Mrs. W. Freeman Kitchen) was a-hed in
Brisbane for about a fortnight with concussion of the
brain, the result of an accident met with during re-
hearsal, and her after recklessness in keeping faith with
the public on her opening night. She was billed to re-
open in “Demon Gold,” on December 8. That day's
Brisbane dailies contained telegraphic news of her hus-
band's suicide in Sydney. She did re open, however.
Not to have done so might have ruined the season, and
would certainly have put some members of the co. to
great distress and inconvenience. But there was very
little joy in the melodrama that night.
Another little prima donna goes down 40 per cent,
through everyone’s finding out she is married, and she
says she will leave ye stage.

It is announced that Durward Lely and wife will make
an Australian tour next April. The gentleman in ques-
tion is an eminent operatic singer, with a more than
ordinary melodious voice. He was a member of DOyley
Carte’s co., and toured with Patti and Marie Rose. His
latest, appearances have been confined to England and
Scotland.
The Misses Griffiths, of Sydney—pianiste, violiniste and
’cellist—have, upon the advice of some London concert-
agent, changed their surname to that of Cowan, and, as
such, have started to storm the metropolis—musically.

Young Stanley Moses, of Windsor (N.S.W.), has, in the
absence of the great Belgian violinist, Ysaye, who is now
in America, been placed in charge of the latter’s pupils.
To have attained to such a height in the Brussels Con-
-Bervatorium required no little genius on the part of the
young Australian. Ysaye, his teacher, predicted a great
future for the youth.
The Bianchis—husband and wife—ran a grand opera co.
in the Sixties. The lady was an enormous woman, with
a splendid voice. The Signor—a good tenor —was shaped
like a football, perched on two little bandy legs. One
night, at the old Prince of Wales, in Castlereagh-street,
Sydney, they were illustrating “II Trovatore.” Little
Bianchi came on the stage in a suit of scale armour.
“Bill, ahoy!” shouted a man-o’-warsman in the gallery
to a shipmate on the other side, “ don’t he look like a
blanky bull-frog?” The audience roared, and the show
was spoiled for that night.

Cockaigne : “Wilson Barrett has more endurance than
any other actor I have heard of. He performed in London,
for weeks together, a heavy- leading part every afternoon
and another every evening. His late brother, George,
was a good comedian, original Jaikes in ‘The Silver
King.’ Wilson Barrett was as poor as a rat three years
ago.”
Musically speaking, Sydney is now practically domina-
ted by Maoriland, or lengthy sojourners in that province.
The two leading tenors, the fashionable baritone, and the
two best-advertised instrumentalists and most prolific
song-writers, are all recent M.L. exports, and tenth-rate
vocalists and pianists arrive by nearly every steamer.
This, of course, doesn’t prove that the Maorilander is the
best musician, but merely that he has heaps more push
and grit than the languid local article.

hea'd of the job. “ Good enought ” said he ; “ I'd do it
meself for a pound a week and me tucker.”

Leoni Clark, who manipulated his trained cats in the
Firm’B last panto., was recently appearing at the Empire
theatre of varieties in Croydon (Eng.) with a boxing
kangaroo added to his menagerie. One day the mar-
supial escaped into the streets, and several of the inhabi-
tants who attempted to recapture it subsequently retired
into hospital. It was eventually' “ roped,” and Croydon
now knows more about the kangaroo than it ever learned
at school.
Recently a certain musical genius, whose signature is
familiar in Israel, for once in a way met his bill. The
lender's former experiences with the same paper having
taught him the vanity of any ordinary form of application
for payment, he wrote as follows and got his money forth-
with “You may recollect, in ‘days long, long ago,’
you cast a little composition of about. 40 notes upon the
financial waters. In the words of the Psalmist, it has
returned to me after many days increased fifty-fold. It
reminds me of that song you sing so exquisitely, which
ends up ‘ The bloom, the bloom is on the bill.’ It is ripe,
not to say ‘Cherry ripe.’ I would like to say ‘Good bye’
to it * for ever and for ever,' and trust that ‘ The hour of
parting’ may not be ‘Too Late.’”—Yours, &c., Aaron
Blankbnstbin.
“ Phoonix” wants to know why some inventive genius
doesn't strike a more secluded roosting-place for the
musical conductor, who, at present, has to make an absurd
object of himself in front of a chorus—wriggling, hoist-
ing, grinning, jerking, and utterly de-realising the stage-
effect.
One of Australia’s most prominent drapers, at an earlier
stage of his career, used to raise a moderately tuneful
voice at concerts, and was regarded as a songster with a
future. On one momentous occasion, he essayed to sing
some of the “ Messiah ” music. About half-a-dozen of
his employes went purposely to do some barracking, and
witnessed the discomfiture of their well-b loved chief,
who broke down ignominiously, after an unearthly crack
on a top note, and fled from the scene. The draper sent
for these six employes next morning, and swore them to
secresy. That top-note was an event of very long ago,
but in all these years, when employ! after employ! has
been sacked, those fortunate six are still undisturbed in
their billets.

The Bulletin will be glad to receive, for
publication, from all quarters, tersely-expressed
comments on the Suffield case—which it regards
as involving one of the most cruel injustices ever
perpetrated by law in Australia. The ease is

pamphlet, published by The Bulletin and post-
free from this office for 7d. in stamps.
Nathaniel Levi, M.L.C., Vic., now at
’Ome, has a long, ungrammatical letter in London
Jewish Chronicle begging funds to save the
mortgaged synagogue in Bourke-st., Melbourne,
from foreclosure. His bitter wail winds up with
a description of himself as “ Member of the Legis-
lative Council of Melbourne.” The spectacle of
Moses vainly striving to get his synagogue out of
pawn is deeply pathetic.
Queensland Premier Nelson thus describes
MTlwraith :
He can’t walk, but, if he is supported by a person on
each side, he can shuffle slowly for a distance of a few
feet; he can’t even lift either hand to his face to brush
away any flies that may settle there. The most that he
can do with his hands, arid this only by making a tre-
mendous and exhausting effort, is to turn over a page of
a book, if the volume is placed advantageously before
him for the performance of such a feat; and he has to be
fed like a hab.v and attended continuously. As for his
means, these are precisely nil.
To which a Bulletin correspondent adds : “ That
MTlwraith is dependent on his wife is well-known.
Personally he is penniless, as well as bed-ridden.”

N.S.W. revenue for November (.£781,524) was
£2582 less than that for November, 1896, and for the first
five months (July to November) of the financial year the
revenue is ±223',222 less than for the corresponding five
months last year. Considering that Treasurer Retd’s
finances are based on the assumption that the 12 months
revenue will be £40,000 more than last year, the above
results are pretty bad—especially as there are now only
seven months in which to get straight. So far t.he expen-
diture is £211,343 ahead of revenue, so the deficit isn't
nearly so large as it was a month ago. Still it is a bigger
deficit, by ±144,000 than the one which existed at the same
date last year, and then the Treasurer, instead of being
able to make up the leeway, ended the year with a very
large shortage What does he think are his chances this
year with £223,000 less revenue, a larger estimate to over-
take, and already a bigger shortage to make good ?
» * * *
The Victorian revenue for November was
£514,009, which is £17,448 more than that for November,
1896. Consequently the slump of October is made good,
and the takings for the past five months are £10,780
better than those for the corresponding five months of
last year, and Premier Turner jumps on his hat no louger
—for the present, at all events.

With further referenoe to the Provident and
Industrial Insurance Co. of Maoriland, commented upon
in the issue of December 4, the writer has hunted up the
balance-sheet for 1892 for purposes of comparison. Even
at that early date the alleged assets of the institution
consisted, in great part, of expenses which were made
into a species of suspense account to be paid off by instal-
ments, only this impudent device has been greatly
extended of recent vears. In 1892 the P. and I. professed
to have accumulated an assurance fund of £1640, but as
the assets against this included money spent in “exten-
sion of business, t 620 ” ; ditto, £659; and preliminary
expenses (amount not separately stated) and office furni-
ture, together £636, it certainly appeared that there was
no assurance fund, or very little. In 1897 the assurance
fund is represented as ±3054, and the assets against it
include; “Establishment of agencies account, £1163;
extension account, 1896, ±934 ; and extension account,
1897, ±1465 ’’—total, £3562. As the purely paper assets
are now £5OO more than the alleged assurance fund,
whereas in 1892 they were only about equal to it,
the concern seems now to be even a little less
solvent than in 1892-in fact, it looks the very
worst thing of its kind in the Seven Provinces. The
following is how the writer would fix up the
Provident and Industrial balance-sheet, going by the
materials contained in Us accounts as they appear in the
Insurance and Banking Record ;
Liabilities. Assets.
Capital paid £4,875 0 0 Calls unpaid £730 9 5
~ called 730 9 6 Deposit ... 6,000 0 0
Advance (ap- Agency
pare nll y balances 275 3 0
loan or over- Unpaid Pre-
draft) ... 3,320 0 8 mi unis ... 727 9 7
Deposit ... 340 0 0 Premises ... 1,267 16 9
Sundry Dehts 366 7 6 Sundry
Commission... 144 6 8 debtors ... 201 19 10
Medical fees Furniture
unpaid ... 89 6 0 and sta-
Reserve Fund nil tionery ... 382 17 0
Assurance Fund nil Deficiency... 1,239 14 8
£9,825 10 3 £9,825 10 3
This is made up by inc.uding everything bearing the
slightest resemblance to a real, saleable asset that tlie
writer can find in the Prudential and Industrial’s balance-
sheet even the office stationery. And it leaves the
concern with no assurance fund, instead of the £3054
claimed, and with a large proportion of its capital missing.
The lurid feature of the Provident and Indus-
trial’s mode of compiling its balance-sheet is that a co.
could, if it pleased, by this method, make the assurance
fund look bigger and bigger simply by saying that it had
spent more money on expenses than was really spent, and
a mode of accountancy which opens up such possibilities
is very weird indeed. For instance, it appears that the
co. expended, in 1897, ±2082 in extending the business,
and instead of charging this to current expenses it is
temporarily put among the assets, to be written off by
degrees. On the other side, it appears that the co has
an assurance fund of £3054. And it naturally follows
that if the amount spent on extending the business was
put down instead at 1 12,042, or £52,082, the assurance
fund would have to be put down at £13,054 or ±53,054 to
square the accounts. A method hv which it. is possible to
make the assurance fund or the reserve fund look ±60,000
larger than it is simply by pretending to have blewed
±50,000 more in expenses than was really spent is truly
amazing.
The Australasian Temperance and General Life
Assurance Society issues a moderately good balance-sheet
so far as its ordinary department is concerned Renewal
premiums (±29,427) are £1963 more'tlian last year, and
new premiums (£4812) are £447 less, therefore the total
premium income shows an improvement of £1516. In-
terest on investments (±8548) is £1764 better than last
year. Still the Temperance and General even now barely
earns 4.) per cent, on its total funds; last year it earned
about £3 17s. per cent.—an absurdly low figure. The
amount saved and added to the funds is £15,589—£116
more than last year, which is only moderately gooff, see-
ing that the income increased by some £3900. The ex-
pense account is the one really dismal feature about
the Temperance and General. With a smaller new'
business than last year, the expenses are £ll,BBB against
£10,885 in 1896 and £8134 in 1895. This year 34% per
cent, of the premiums go in expenses, against only 33J
per cent, in 1896, 26 per cent, in 1895, and
25 per cent, in 1894. Also, the expenses absorb 27} per
cent, of the total income, which is something enormous.
The Temperance and General may be solid enough,
but with a very moderate rate of interest on invested
funds and an enormous expense-rate it offers no
particular attractions. The balance-sheet is not wholly
satisfactory in one respect. Outstanding and accrued
interest represents * 2060, but the sheet doesn’t say how
much of this is oniv accrued, and how much is actually
in arrear. The sum involved in this case is very small,
still the practice of lumping these two items is in-
defensible. The amount of interest in arrear is almost
the only available test of the soundness of the invest-
ments—when it is unduly large it is a tolerably reliable
evidence that the investments are not healthy —and
arrears of interest should alwa3's he set forth separately'.
The Temperance and General also runs an in-
dustrial department whose present condition is some-
thing amazing. In 1896 this department collected
£8242 premiums and £1259 interest total income,
£9501. To do this, it paid in expenses £4489,
and, after allowing for claims, the funds of the
industrial branch diminished from £35,049 to £32,568. In
1897, it collected £11,753 premiums and £1324 interest
and fees ; total, £13,077. To do this, the expenses ran to
£14,741— something like 112 per cent., or £1664 more than
the entire income. After paying claims, the funds
decreased from £32,868 to ±25,301, so two years vigorous
hustling after business leaves the Temperance and Gene-
ral’s industrial department nearly' £lO,OOO worse off. An
increase of £3500 in the 1897 premium-income apparently
cost over £lO,OOO extra in expenses, and, even in the
miserably shiftless industrial business, this is buying
insurance a vast deal too dear. A very large new busi-
ness has been done, which is a good feature if it only'
sticks, and industrial business, as a rule, doesn’t stick
much better than a snowball in Gehenna. But it is a
solemn thing to consider that, if the new business had
only been four times larger than it was, this great hurst
of prosperity would almost certainly have left the indus-
trial department with no funds at all.

The South British Fire Insurance Co. (Maori-
land) realised £27,611 net profit on its year’s work, pays
17(<> per cent, dividend (£11.310), adds £lO,OOO to reserve,
and'has still < 6301 to the good. The South British has
now £176,000 of reserves and £10,211 to credit of profit
and loss. Somehow Maoriland is the only one of the
Seven Provinces where the local fire insurance business
seems to flourish, probably because it is the only province
where the inhabitant wouldn’t rather do business with a
foreign institution than otherwise. As a result its two
biggest offices—the New Zealand and the South British—
do almost, as much business between them as all the 16
institutions of Australia put together.
* • * *
Tlie National Fire Insurance Co. (Maoriland)
made a net profit, of ±15,229 for the year just ended, and
the usual 15 per cent, dividend swallows up £14,705,
leaving the co. only 1524 to the good. The National has
£98,037 capital, and ±111,317 of reserves and profit and
loss balance, and its assets all appear sound, so it may
fairly be regarded as a large and prosperous concern.
• * * *
The Colonial Mutual Fire Insurance Co. got its
balance-sheet out just before the great Flinders-lane
blaze struck it. The year’s profit is ±BB4B, as compared
with £8790 in 1896, £8584 in 1895, and £8192 in ’94. There
is also a balance of £2029 from last year. A 6 per cent,
dividend absorbs £2250 ; £5OOO goes to reserve, making it
£15,000 ; £2OOO is put aside to start a fund for possible
depreciation, and £1627 carried forward. It is a very
prosperous-looking sheet on the whole. The only item
which looks at all doubtful is the investment of £6144 in
building societies’ shares, which—considering the painful
condition of many building societies just now—isn’t quite
above suspicion. Anyhow, the Colonial Mutual has done
a wonderful resurrection of late years. In 1892 it had no
reserve, and was £19,911 to the bad ; now it has £18,627
accumulated, and has a steadily improving business. If
the big Melbourne fire had come along in 1892 it would
have about Onished this institution.
• • * •
The Cornwall Fire Insurance Co. had a particu-
larly luckless time for the year ended 30th September.
The’ whole profit was £469, and the dividend was cut
down from the usual 6) to ±3 2s. 6d. per cent. Even at
this rate is amounted to £2500 or more than five times
the earnings. The Cornwall never seems to strike any-
thing very great in the way of good fortune. It has
£80,"00 paid-up capital, no reserve-fund, no re-insurance
reserve, no provision for possible depreciation of securi-
ties, and only £5420 of accumulations. For a concern
which has existed 66 years and has paid away over
£190,000 in dividends, this is a woefully unprogressive
state of things. Tasmania is the home of ver.v small and
very old financial institutions which never do anything
in particular ; they are ancient and honest and highly
respectable, and they badly want the dust blown off them.
* * * *
The City Bank of Sydney holds £l5O, OOOof N.S.W.
public money at fixed deposit, and £3646 on current ac-
count, and it. also holds £166,884 belonging to the Savings
Bank of N.S.W. Which makes over £320,000 of State
money, or money for which the State is more or less re-
sponsible-equal to nearly one-third of the City Bank’s
entire deposits, and also nearly as much as its entire
paid-up capital. The City Bank is apparently the only
one which holds any N.S.W. State money on fixed de-
posit, and in one way and another the State seems, for
some reason, to support that institution very liberally.

• » * *
Block 14’s last half-year has been uneventful.
The co. has made a profit, of £17,763; £lO,OOO has been
paid in divs., and £6995 for plant and machinery. The
present position is that it has a surplus of liquid assets of
£27,372. But the most liquid of all assets—cash—was
scarce at the end of the half; the co.’s stock of it ran to
only £4B, while the Bank of Adelaide had advanced
£5878. Bulk of liquid assets consists of concentrates and
ore on hand (£23,094), estimated surpluses (£19,685), and
timber, &c. (£7641); there are sundry debtors, apart from
the bank, for £17,970. Certainly no reason is disclosed
why at the very least the ordinary two divs. shouldn’t be
paid this current half—in fact, it might easily be three.
* • • •
Block 10's half-year has been uneventful, too.
It has made a profit of 1 23,634, has paid £25,000 in divs.,
has a surplus of liquid assets (cash, concentrates, and
stores) of £22,644 over liabilities, and its directors see no
reason (assuming a steady market) why 3s. per share
should not be paid quarterly—£3o,ooo per half-year, that
is. This advance is relied upon in consequence of the
completion of the new concentrating plant, to be run in
conjunction wilh the present mill, while there are hopes
of locally treating the “ zinc ores ” (they aren’t “ tailings ”
any more, please note), instead of selling them at a mere
trifle, for shipment. Of these “ores,” which are not
treated as an asset, the manager estimates that 50,000
tons in the dump are worth 34 per cent, of zinc, 13oz.
silver, and 6 per cent. lead ; another 40,000 tons, 30 per
cent, zinc, 12oz. silver, and 7 per cent. lead.
Block 10 management is much more frank than
that of Block 14. The latter never quotes an assay.
Neither shareholders nor public know the average value
of either crude ore or concentrates; nor do they know
the grade of the new bodies exposed in the mine. Block
10, on the other hand, reports assay returns. Block 14
proceeds with its explorations below 300 ft. slowly, and
only says ore is plentiful ; Block 10 goes boldly into the
ore at 750 ft., and finds a good body, assaying 14oz. silver,
19 per cent, lead, and 28 per cent. zinc. Perhaps that is
the most important thing in this co.’s half-yearly report.
* * • *
Block 10 sulphide body at 715 ft. proves to be
14ft., almost pure ore, and averages 13 - 05 oz. silver, 21 ‘25
per cent-lead, and 26 percent zinc. That is fair, though
with depth the silver falls away seriously. Last half-year
the average assays of all ore put through the mill were
17 - 540 z. silver, 20 13 per cent, lead, and 28 63 per cent,
zinc.

* * * *
The special mining reporter of the Sydney
Evening News barracks for the amalgamation of the
Lady Maude and other shows in the Stewart’s Brook dis-
trict. “ They are sufficiently developed to be handled
straight away, for there is ore enough in sight to warrant
the erection of a plant of 20 head of stampers, to he in-
creased as development work progressed.” Then why in
Heaven’s name don’t they crush at one or other of the
water power batteries already available within sight of the
mines? All the shows at Stewart’s Brook, including the
Royal Standard (which is worth more than all the others
put together), cannot keep 10-heads running, and to put
up 20 on these miserable little holes called mines, in addi-
tion to the batteries already there, would be only repeat-
ing an old folly.
• * • *
The S.M. Herald reports that the mines of the
Grong Grong district (N.S.W.) “are looking well.”
Several men are employed in one mine, and an assay has
been made from the stone of another; and there are
several where mining operations are shortly to be re-
newed. One mine has actually crushed five tons, and is
reported to have 15 tons at grass. Of course, the best is
reserved till the last. “ The Harry Smith mine employs
a number of men, and has had a gold-record of 1200 oz.
up to date.” Look at that now ! It is fully a year since
it had a crushing, but little things like that don’t matter 1
» * * •
The forming of a co. to work the Mt. Kimo
mine, Gundagai (N.S.W.), is cabled from London, and, as
usual, the local hummer by wire has risen to the occasion.
“The ore-body is over 30ft. wide, extends for miles, and
assays have gone as high as 70oz. to the ton. A lot of
prospecting has been done, and plans for ihe plant have
gone to England, and, when erected, it will treat 500 tons
a day ; and, when in full swing, will employ fully 300
men.” Very easy to draw strong mining pictures in
newspapers. It probably never dawned on the Gundagai
men that it would take 200 head of stamps to treat 500
tons a day, and that, if the ore is “ highly mineralised,"
the working-capital of the new co. is not enough to pay
for the plant.

Another great report from the Golconda,
Parkes (N.S.W.): “ On the foot-wall about 4in. of kindly-
looking quartz carrying a little fine gold. About 8 tons
of stone at grass.” Also another call!

Dear Bulletin, —You refer to the holding capa-
city of Tamborah (W.A.) dishes. What about the £lO,OOO
worth of gold in two dishes—also in W.A.—advertised a
month or so back? Roughly, a ton of gold is worth
£lOO,OOO, so these dishes must have contained 2cwt. of
gold without any allowance for dirt. Half-a-ton of gold
could be put in a dish as long as the bottom did not drop
out in lifting it—and, if W.A. wanted a gorgeous ad.,
why in thunder didn’t they fill ’em up?—J.T.
Lake View Consols, for the month : 8551 oz., a
little over 2oz. average. Even this is regarded as disap-
pointing after the predictions of 10,000 and 12,0000 z. by
papers and persons who are fond of remarking that W.A.
has, in the L V.C., a bigger mine than Mt. Morgan. (The
Mount’s output for the month was over 18,000 oz.) Mining
men who have seen both mines say the comparison is
absurd. There is more ore in sight above the Grass Tree
tunnel, Mt. Morgan, than in any three of the Kalgoorbe
mines, well as they are developed. True, the average has
come down with tonnage, and so it has in the Kalgoorlie
mines and all others. Even if it were a question of turn-
ing out lOoz. rock. Mt. Morgan would win hands down.
No other gold-mine in the world is in the same street
with it.

Burbanks, Coolgardie. Month, 758 tons,
13220 z. The most consistent producer of the field. No
one ever hears anything now of Burbanks Consols, B.
Main Lode, B. No. i West, or B. North ; all English go's
working around the original Burbanks, and struggling
hard to live on its name. Some day they may strike
something 1 Burbanks itself was nothing to rave about
when the writer saw it.

* • * *
Zeb. Lane having returned to W.A. the nowS-
papers of that province, in the absence of the almighty
Kaufman, are now worshipping the footstool and foot-
steps of the new mining god. Some men apparently can
stand a lot of sickening slobber without flinching. Be
foie leaving London, Lone wns banqueted at the Hote'
Cecil, and the British Australasian devoted three page*
to the event—one page containing a plan of the table
and the names of the guests, principally W.A. boomsters
of the deepest dye. Fingall was chairman, and ladled
eulogy all over Lane, who had sense enough to say little,
and then narrowly escaped putting his foot into it. He
admitted being associated with Frank Gardiner, and that
“ had it not been for one or two little oppositions, F.G.
would have had the Great Boulder.” Much was said of
the richness of the Great Boulder and Lane’s connection
with it, but not a word of the other mines floated in Lon-
don about the same time—the Empress of Coolgardie, for
instance. *

KaJgoorlie wire : “ Owing to the failure of the

and a party is being equipped to prospect elsewhere.”
And what has become of the Kanowna deep lead, of which
the public heard so much ?

Charters Towers items: For fortnight ending
27th Nov. Bonnie Dundee crushed 360 tons for 2540 z.
A tributors’ crushing from same mine gave 87£oz. from
72 tons. East level of Kelly’s Block, w-hich lately looked
very blank indeed, has disclosed a foot of good-looking
stone. The Warrior recently commenced a large crush-
ing; when 87 tons had been put through and the clean-
up revealed only about 19oz. of gold the co. gave it best
and resolved to pick the ore more carefully in future ; it
will need it. Victoria and Queen, as predicted in a
recent par., is turning out a valuable property ; latest
crushing, 240 tons for 5130 z. ; fortnight's haulage, 256
tons; co. now £lOOO in credit, and a handsome Christmas
div. expected ; only 48,000 shares in co. Queen Cross
290 tons for 5240 z. ; crushing being continued at latest
advices; haulage for fortnight ended 23rd Nov,, 400
trucks; a mine with a promising future ; big lease ;
shares well held. Both drives in the Deep Levels mine
show stone of miserably poor quality. Big local and
foreign demand for Brilliant and St. George shares re-
cently ; holders, however, decline to part. Papuan
Brilliant and Victoria for fortnight ended 26th Nov., crushed
611 tons for 11610 z., and paid £2400 in a 6d. div.
Brilliant Block crushed 664 j tons for 3120 z. ; fortnight’s
haulage 820 tons. Share market dull, stale and unprofit-
able. The Brilliant has commenced sinking its vertical
shaft below the Brilliant reef, on the chance that other
lodes may underlie the last-named formation. The pro-
gress of this shaft will be eagerly watched, as prospects
of numerous surrounding mines will be affected by the
result.

More C. Towers items: Reef of good size, but
poor quality, struck in Kelly’s Block underlie. Victoria
shares hardened about end of month, improvement in
footwall reef the cause. Moonstone Consols, at latest
advices crushing 300 tons for anticipated good result. ——
A Towers youth named Arthur Wales unearthed speci-
mens yielding about £2500 worth of gold at Green hills
(Etheridge) recently. Brilliant Nov. result 2741 tons
for 2735£0z. Phoebe, at close of month, had about 370
tons hauled for reduction. Victory for fortnight ending
Ist Dec. crushed 113 tons for 505*0z. Brilliant Ex-
tended fortnight’s ore haulage, 2605 tons. During past
half-year the Extended netted £8595 profit from ore treat-
ment ; 14,995 tons yielded £34,447 worth of gold. The
co. has now paid off Dick Craven the £6OOO advanced by
him to develop the mine, and is in credit. Dividends are
expected early in the new year. Day Dawn Block and
Wyndham delighted its Bull owners by paying a Is. div.
on’ its 500,000 shares (125,000); co. obtained about 6000 oz.
during last month. Total divided during current year,
£75,000. Clark’s Worcester fortnight’s haulage, 40
tons ; promising developments underground recently.
Brilliant and St. George cleaned up on 2nd December,
21160 z. from 1269 tons, and declared a shilling div. and a
shilling bonus (£14,400), making £41,400 dividend during
past half-year, and £63,000 during current year. Fort-
night’s ore haulage, 1200 tons ; slopes and levels looking
well, but only a small reef in the sink of underlie. An
attempt to infuse new blood into the directorate of the
Brilliant Extended at recent half-yearly meeting ended
disastrously for the new blood ; the proxy collectors held
full hands and voted solidly on both sides, but the old
blood panned out about 50,000 better than the new.
Therefore the venerable gore remains in possession.

Towers calls for November, £8900; dividends,
£17,000. For 11 months of the present year the calls were
£BB,OOO ; dividends, £268,000. In the November returns
43,807 tons gave 34,7700 z. of gold. Of this amount mill
returns were 16,700 tons for 21,0000 z. gold—the rest being
cyanide bullion. For the past 11 months 386,400 tons
yielded 314,3000 z. gold, or 80,000 oz. excess over same
period of last year. It is calculated December dividends
will reach about £60,000.

November gold-returns both for Charters Towers
and Gympie are very good. Gympie from 7112 tons gave
12,8630 z. 18dwt. gold, or loz. 16dwt. 4gr. to the ton.
Compare this with last year and it shows an increase of
over 100 per cent., while the average per ton isBdwt. lOgr.
higher. Dividends for month just closed, £26,926 16s. 3d. ;
calls, £5440 3s. 4d.
* * * *
At Gympie, from the Columbia Smithfield to
the North Victory, all the claims—North Smithfield, 1
North Smithfield, Phoenix Golden Pile, Smithfield and
Phoenix Golden Pile, 3,4, 6 and 6 North Phoenix, and the
North Victory—are on gold, the North Victory only ex-
cepted. With the exception of 3 and 6 North Phoenix
and 1 North Victory, all are paying dividends up to 2s.
Christmas crushings promise to increase these.

No flourish of trumpets about this : “ Brilliant
(Charters Towers) Deep Levels, 50 tons for 7oz. 6dwt.”
This is from the official, Warden’s office, Nov. returns.
Two pennyweights, twenty-two grains per ton, and from
“ typical Brilliant stone,” as was alleged when co. bot-
tomed on reef.
• * • •
Mount Lyell continues to produce pups. Some
of the first, litter are dogs now, feeble with old age.
Others have wandered away into the wilderness and died,
and only their skeletons remain. Of the present litter
the North Lyell and Lyell Tharsis appear to be the most
healthy.
• • • •
Lakes Dora and Selina district, Tas., east of
Mount Tyndall, is being cut up into sections and floated.
It is a flat, cheerless, timberless hole of a place with the
usual formations, some miles across, running out of one
section into another from all points of the compass, with
the same old, unexplainable regularity. There is no
track wider than a platypus-run from Oormanston, and
managers and men hump’ out tools, tucker, and ammuni-
tion on their backs through indescribable scrub, cursing
like all possessed.
• • • •
New West Colebrook (Tas.) claims to have
found an immense lode “ equal to the Colebrook in size
and excelling it in value.” This announcement seems to
indicate that the Schlapp crowd has secured the bulk of
the N.W.C. shares at the knock-out prices lately ruling.

teer, Smith’s Section, Argent, and Silver Queen the chief
contributors.

COGMAG
The nine days wonder of the Merkara
Case is slowly fading from the Brisbane mind, but
it has left a bad taste there. The ground for this
action lay as far back as 1896, when the Rev. Jas.
Clarke —not the Rev. James Clarke Sydney
knows, but an aged and learned Church of Eng-
land clergyman from Ireland—went travelling for
the health of his eldest son, who was dying of
phthisis. Apparently the aged preacher-man
didn’t have any friends or relatives with whom he
uould leave his other four children—two well-
grown daughters and two younger sons—so he
decided to bring them all out to Australia, and as
his funds wouldn’t run saloon fares he brought
them steerage. So the 8.1.5. N. Co.’s Merkara
set steam, and presently Dr. Peters, the ship’s
surgeon (whose uncle, Sir James Mackay, is chair-
man of the 8.1.5. N. Co.), began to be friendly
with the eldest daughter named Maggie, and the
aged clergyman protested, and his life became a
burden to him. For that aged clergyman was
pelted with potatoes, and water was squirted at
him, and his respectable silk hat was filled with
water and kicked along the deck ; and, finally, he
was struck in the face, which is probably not the
kind of treatment that an aged clergyman expects
when he travels by the 8.1.5. N. Co. s R.M.S.
Merkara.
By-and-by the son died, but the daughter went
on, and was “engaged” to the doctor, and the
aged clergyman continued to protest when he saw
thedoctor’s arm round his daughter’swaist, and was
locked up in his cabin till the ship reached Bris-
bane, on the ground that be was “ a dangerous
lunatic.” His lunacy was affirmed by the health
officer at Thursday Island after hearing Dr.
Peters’ report, and was duly certified by Dr.
Peters. The captain of the Merkara “ bad no
hesitation ” in acting on the certificate—more
especially as he himself had observed curious
behaviour on the part of the aged clergyman, and
several passengers had signed a petition complain-
ing of the aged clergyman’s behaviour—a petition
which some of them say they signed because they
were asked to, and without knowing whether
some of the allegations were true or false.
When the Merkara reached Brisbane, the
dangerous lunatic was promptly emptied out on
the wharf, where the local health-doctor testified
that he was as sane as anybody else. So he
brought an action, claiming .£5OOO damages from
the doctor and some other officers of the Merkara ;
and a Brisbane jury last week gave him a farthing
damages against the doctor—-and Chief-Justice
Griffith found that this was tantamount to
a verdict for the defendants, and gave them
costs. As the costs run into thousands, the ques-
tion how the aged clergyman is going to pay
them seems to be a matter between himself and
the Church of England. Several witnesses gave
evidence that the doctor had behaved very kindly
throughout, and the aged clergyman had behaved
very badly, and had said naughty words, and
testified generally that the aged clergyman ought
to have been locked up. And Chief-Justice
Griffith summed up against the aged clergyman,
and the jury followed Sam Griffith’s lead, and prac-
tically affirmed that the aged clergyman deserved
all he got, and that the doctor, whose uncle is
chairman of the 8.1.5. N. Co., is a very nice
young man indeed. All the same, The Bulletin
advises aged clergymen of the Church of England
stationed in Ireland to give Brisbane, for the
future, a very wide berth—especially when they
have to travel in the steerage with a dying son

and four other children, of whom the eldest girl

Petone (M.L.), like Onehunga, has gone
in for a female councillor or alderwoman, and, at
latest advices, the lady was having a bad time.
Apparently the municipal bush-fathers resented the
presenceof a woman at their meetings, and, by way
of protest, some of them started to smoke. When
she complained they closed the door, and others
filled up with strong plug-tobacco and smoked,
and went slowly through the business while their
female colleague suffocated in their midst. Before
they were through the fumes stood in a dense mass
reaching five feet up from the floor, and the bonnet
of an asphyxiated lady just appeared above the
reek. When some pure air got in and the meeting
terminated, the Petone alderwoman made re-
marks about the councillors’ manners, whereupon
they recorded a vote of censure against her, and
fired her off all the committees. It has also been
demanded that she shall appear before the council
in a contrite spirit, and on all fours if possible,
and apologise, and, metaphorically, jump on her
own best bonnet in the council’s presence as a sign
of penitence. Altogether, the game of being a
Maoriland alderwoman seems hardly worth the
candle.

• * * *
The shepherd of a very unfinancial Mel-
bourne church was lately “ called ” to some dis-
tant region where his salary would rest on a firmer
basis. His late flock is now tracing the hand of
Providence in the fact that the shepherd’s new
church and most of his parish were blown away by
the cyclone before he had even time to formally
take over the business.
A young man recently lost the sight of one
eye, and consulted a certain Australian doctor
who told him that a tumor had formed, and that
the eye would have to be taken out. A few days
later he was put under chloroform by the aforesaid
surgeon, who took out an eye. It was the wrong,
one. When the patient awoke he was stone blind,
and he now has to be led about. Great efforts
were made to hush up the matter, but the story
of the dreadful mistake is getting about.

Writing from Auckland (M.L.) to his wife-
in London, a dear hubby who had gone wrong'
with another girl pleaded custom as the cause of
his fall. One of his phrases : “No man here loses
a chance of immorality.”

Suggested in South Australia, where the’
harvest has failed the third year in succession,
that N.S. W. Judge Backhouse should be hired to
go through the North with his “ Advantages of
Impecuniosity ” lecture. Of course he would
tramp it—that is one of the advantages.

“The most singular feature about this huge family [?]
that rarely in all the centuries during which it has been .

OUR SYD. SMITH (sadly): “And s-s-such is

The recent tornado in Vic. brought out a
host of begging parsons. Here and there a little
Bethel suffered from the gale, and immediately,
before the public mind had time to realise that
whole families had been rendered homeless and
penniless, the press was flooded by rev. mendi-
cants whose congregations had “ not where to
assemble themselves together,” as one gentleman
put it (why couldn’t they “ assemble themselves ”
apart?). There is much of the Spirit in this in-
decent haste to catch the first droppings of popular
charity ; there is also some considerable sprinkling
of the Flesh.

WEDDED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It’s us two when it’s morn in’,
An’ us two when it’s night,
An’ ns two when it’s troubled,
An’ us two when it’s bright.
An’ us two don’t want nothin’
To make life good an’ true,
An’ lovin’ sweet an’ wholesome
While us two’s got us two.
M.J.C.

THE BUTTERFLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She trims at morn her gaudy wings (my words are meta-
phorical)
(I mean she twines her curls afresh, renews her rosy
hue);
She flits about from flower to flower (my style is alle-
gorical)
(I mean —when lovers old have fled she seeks for lovers
new).
She dips her lips in honev-dew, of dainty hud deflow’r-
ing it
(So Delia taketh toll of all who love a winning eye) ;
She shakes the blossom’s dust abroad, in careless humor
show’ring it —
(So Delia waves her little hand and Damon’s dollars fly).
She loves the light, the summer sun, the garden’s gay
variety—
(The chandelier is Delia’s sun, the ball her bright par-
terre) ;
In butterflighty lexicon no blockhead word sobriety
Offends us by suggesting ever creature had a care.
For life is all a joyous time for rioting and rollicking,
For flirting and for flutt’ring until blood be waxen cool,
And she who will not festive be and join us in our
frolicking
May wizen in her wisdom while we wanton with the
fool.
And so she whirls, the merry moth, in round of pleasures
numberless,
And when the giddy game is o’er her epitaph shall be :
“ She furled her wings when Winter came and closed her
Summer cumberless,
—She lived upon the blossom and she sleeps beneath
the tree ! ”
Q-
P. LUFTIG.

The Hackshall Biscuib Company, at
Camperdown, Sydney, should commend itself
to the earnest attention of the public. This firm
has recently established itself in new and com-
modious premises ; got together the most experi-
enced workmen ; and, irrespective of cost, set up
the newest and most up-to-date machinery and
appliances. And the able i management guaran-
tees what is of still more importance to thte
public, viz., that the Hackshall Company’s biscuit
will be an absolutely pure and wholesome biscuit.
It intends making the purest biscuit only ; to
combine a maximum of nourishment with un-
equalled freshness and delicacy of flavor. Mothers
should make a note of the company’s name—
“ Hackshall ’.’ y they will find ifrnr boon. It is by
quality that the company intends to fight the
foreign biscuit which is now coming into the
market under, the Freetrade tariff, and, with: a
public that, is able to appreciate what is really
good, it is certain of success.* ;

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
The intellect of the average witness is a
fearful and wonderful thing. A witness recently
deposed'to the Brisbane P.M. that the blinds of
a certain house were drawn up on a certain occa-
sion ; that he wasn’t sure whether they were up
or down; that he was now dead certain they
were down. When asked by the amiable Pinnock
if he could conscientiously sign his name to his
evidence as truthful and genuine, witness breezily
responded “ Certainly ! ”
••* . *
The sort of woman who doesn’t get a hus-
band, according to her own testimony in Sydney
Sunday Times, is the “small, pleasant, neatly-
dressed individual ” who, instead of pounding the
piano and screeching after impossible top-notes,
cooks and manages for the household, makes her
own clothes, likewise “ our mother’s and the chil-
dren’s ” —and even repairs the family boots ! And
she reports that she is only 22. What kind of a
world is it where a pleasant, neatly-dressed girl of
22, who can repair boots, is thus neglected ?
* * * ♦
A Sydney municipal election incident : A
vote-canvasser called at a respectable-looking
house, and dilated upon the aldermanic virtues of
his candidate, but was cut short by the white lady
of the house with : “Ishe a real Christian ? Has
he got the Lord in his heart?” “He has,
indeed!” replied the glib canvasser; “and not
only that, but he conducts house-to-house services
on Sundays.” “Very well,” replied the lady,
“ when Ah Fat comes home, I’ll make him go
down and vote for the Lord’s anointed.”
CECILIA DIAZ JEWELL.
A reminiscence of the recent Spanish frauds. The
above is the alleged daughter whom the dying Spanish
parent has desired to entrust, along with an immense
fortune, to the care of a large number of Australian
residents—on receipt of money to pay some necessary
expenses.
■ A lady resident in the area to be irrigated
by a certain proposed S.A. water-scheme, peti-
tioned for the carrying out of the work “ because
she had not had a bath for 17 years.”
* * * *
, What a pious how! went through the City
of Smells quite recently when Miss Sutherland
hinted that some of the Melb. goody-goody owned
“ bad ” houses. Yet on a site now otherwise occu-
pied stood, not so very long ago, a dozen hovels,
the worst in Melbourne, averaging five “ unfortu-
nates ”to each. Robbery, &c., was a nightly
occurrence, and more than one murder occurred in
these dens. For years the rent, 12s. a week for
each house, was pocketed by two shining lights of
the temperance and religious world. And there
are plenty of shining lights in the same business
to this day.

Dear Bulletin, —That “ a prophet has no
honor in his own country ” is false. Since my
name has appeared under yarns in The Bulletin,
I have received the following :
We offer a sovereign for . . . the best 24 lines of poetry
(sic ) describing our stock, business, and premises—not
less than four lines to end with the letters B.D.C.
The mystic letters stand for the Beehive Drapery
Company, located at a village 13 miles from where
I live, which village consists largely of a pub., rail-
way-shed, and the remarkable B.D.C. store. The
“ stock ” in question comprises “gum-boots, cream
separators, stomach and liver pills, sheep dip, new
summer dresses, frog in your throat, women’s
bicycle colored hose, 1000 high-class music at 6d.,
worm powders, scythes, perambulators,” &c.
“Perambulators” might rhyme with “cream
separators,” “bicycle hose” with “new summer
clo’es,” “stomach liver pills ” with “ all the flesh’s
ills,” and so on, but I’m jiggered if I know what
to do with the “1000 high-class music at Gd.”—A.
At Roma, Q., current temperature is
103 deg. in the shade. Judge Paul, the other day,
discharged three would-be suicides, with a hint to
make a better job next time. One man had tried
to. cut his throat with a razor ; a second opened a
vein in his arm ; and a third drove a pair of
scissors in the direction of his heart. Probably
they wouldn’t be any better pleased if they had
made a neater job of it. The temperature Down
There must be a lot more than 103 deg.
, * * * *
One Gorman knocked an old man down,
in Melb., the other day, and plucked his beard
out by the roots. The Bench let Gorman off with
20s. in the poor-box, “ because he appeared to be
respectable.” Probably, if Gorman had come
along with a freshly-severed head in his hand,
instead of only a beard, the same fat-headed
Bench would still have thought he seemed to be
respectable. About the same time, at Footscray,
another man stole a pineapple, value Is. He got
a month’s gaol. Until some one deliberately
jumps on the chest of every J.P. in Australia,
it will never dawn on the average Bench that it is
worse to maim a human being than to be found in
unlawful possession of an odd copper’s worth of

Street - sweepers and “ gutter - snipes ”
working for Melbourne Corporation contractors
have to turn out and turn-to every Sunday morn-
ing at 4 o’clock to get the roads in good trim for
Sabbath-observers, who would swear if the streets
were not tidy for the church-goers. The holy
man often commits his sins by deputy.
* * * *
At a recent meeting of the Mothers’ Union,
an organisation of Melbourne matrons designed to
improve the methods of rearing other peoples’
children, a Miss Tripp read a long, lingering,
illiterate paper on “Literature for children,” and
harrowed up the assembled females with the.
shocking announcement that she knew quite
young girls who had read “Trilby,” and “The
Wages of Sin,” and “Robert Elsmere.” Even
religious books, she held, should not be placed in
the hands of the young if it was doubtful whether
religion or flirtation was the conspicuous element.
Then Miss Tripp instanced a few books as suitable
reading for children, “ and, above all, the Bible.”
Ah, yes, beloved “sistern,” the Bible is quite
safe, there is no flirtation there ; flirtation was not
invented in the days of the prophets and the
patriarchs. This accounts for the bald and awful
suddenness of the Bible love story, which often
begins and ends in one verse, and is always excel-
lent reading for children. The Old Testament
love story is generally a curt statement that some-
body took somebody and they had seven children.
What humorists these religious professors
arc. The other day several of them turned up at
the Melbourne Licensing Court to oppose applica-
tions for a couple of wine licenses at the eastern
ends of Bourke and Lonsdale streets. The chief
reason urged by the opposers was that the opening
of a wine-shop would disturb the quiet of the
neighborhood. Think of that ! The soothing,
restful peace of Lonsdale-street East, and the holy
calm of Little Bourke-street, where the Chow, and
the larrikin, and the lady with her hair a-hanging
down her back most do congregate. Further on,
one pastor strongly objected "to the “ temptation
which would be put in the way of church mem-
bers passing to and fro.” Apparently he reckoned
that a person who wasn’t a church member might
resist the drink-crave, but the member would rush
in and soak himself in liquor right off.

In a recent S.M. Herald memoriam-
notice six members of deceased’s family are to the
front, each with a separate “pome” of the usual
excruciating character. The unspeakable dread-
fulness of most obituary and memorial verse is
easily explained by the fact that the newspaper-
column grievist can have no sense of the grotesque,
anyhow.
• * * *
A police-officer, whose indiscretion led to
police-court proceedings and dismissal, found em-
ployment in Westralia, but his Eastern history
followed him, and he was asked to resign. This
is generally the fate of the civilian who has had a
“ bit of trouble,” and it is a policeman who “ puts
him away. ” The ease in question reverses the
order of persecution.
* * * *
The married settlers at a certain little M.L.
town have lately developed a unanimous tendency
to stay at home and work in the garden on Satur-
days. The new schoolmaster is young and hand-
some and unmarried, and there is no school on
Saturdays. They have also petitioned the Educa-
tion Department to take away the - beauteous
swisher of the rising generation, and send along a
married teacher instead—and, if they get him, the
settlers will probably want to go round and do up
his garden on Saturdays instead. Meanwhile, the
ladies of the district have requested that the pre-
sent teacher be retained, “ as he is quite up to his
work, and is giving every satisfaction.”

Holdar M. : “ Moonblindness ; another
case. Years ago I was acquainted with an other-
wise astute man who contracted the habit of sitting
‘on the benches in the Park,’ with the moonlight
rays streaming down upon him—and his best girl.
His vision quickly became obscured, and, as the
infirmity defied other alleviative measures, I ad-
vised him to consult a parson, after which he was
the victim of a wonderful recovery.”

A scoffsome letter from Melbourne :
If the lost sheep wandering round this city on Sunday
evening aren’t gathered in it isn’t for want of effort on
the part of the shepherd. Last Sunday evening, peram-
bulating the city, I ran against no less than seven differ-
ent bands of soul-savers —two at corner of Russell and
Bourke streets, one at Russell and Little Bourke streets
(this was a fitful and spasmodic sort of combination, con-
sisting of a blind man, two women, and a dog, but it wds
very fervent), two at corner of Small Bourke and Stephen
streets, and, later in the evening, one at corner of Bourke
and Stephen and Bourke and Russell streets. In addition,
there were no less than five theatres or music-halls telling
the glad tidings. The Cambridge show comprised about
a dozen plain singers (and a parson, of course), and the
audience showed its appreciation by rolling up—-to the
number of 22. The Gaiety offered more attractions ; here;
was a string band of eight instruments, including two
pretty and young violins, but the shepherd was tedious
and a vexation of spirit. He had about 60 sheep, mostly
two-tooth ewes. At the Hanover dance-rooms a
minister and two M’s.L.A. were holding forth to a
full house on social matters. At the Victoria
Hall Pastor Edgar’s Central Mission held forth, assisted by
a brass band of 20 and presided over by a uniformed
nurse —Sister Alice, of Melb. Hospital. Here an audience
of about 300 cheered the soul of the worker in the vine-
yard. At the Harmy ’All—where was no band, but many
singers, plain and fancy—about 120 were present until
8.30, when quite a number of women —evidently taking
their religion, like their medicine, in large doses —arrived
from the churches for a further supply of spiritual con-
solation. Meanwhile, Bourke-street. was crowded with a-
miscellaneous assortment seeking a means of passing
time, and cursing the dismal Melb. Sabbath ; and in’eight
large churches I visited, including St. Paul’s, Presbyterian,
Unitarian, &c., the sum total of worshippers would not
amount to 300. All which leads to the unfortunate but
necessary conclusion that it is not the Gospel nor the
Shepherd that entices the lost sheep, but the brass bind
and the big drum !

They took a poll the other day on the question
(pub. or no pub.); 70 per cent, of the ratepayers
voted, and every vote, save one, was cast in favor
of granting a license. The solitary exception was
an informal -vote-. -
THE REASON OF IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My lips with sorrow’s stal are set,
The savour from the salt’s departed,
No gladsorreness from aught I get,
And I’m confoundedly low-hearted.
By one great tragic fact oppressed
I sit apart, nor end my grieving,
And hug unto my woe-racked breast
A sorrow' that is past relieving.
I have not backed a stiff’ned horse
Nor of my goods have “ bums ” bereft me;
I am not stricken by remorse,
Nor has my wife cleared out and left me;
But joyousness has fled away
And I am hopelessly despondent:
I met a well-known man to-day
Who W’as not named a co-respondent.
F.L.P.
THE UNJUST JUDGE.
“To enter his court was to enter the den of a wild
beast, which none could tame, and which was as likely: to
be roused to rage by caresses as by attacks.”— Macaulay
on Jeffreys.
The Judge was dead, his soul sped on,
It cleft unmeasured space, ! •
And came where fiery vapors shone
Above the Dreadful Place.
The gates flew wide, the woeful sound
Smote his appalled ear
Of billion souls in torture bound 1 ;
He shrieked in hopeless fear.
Within the gate awaiting him
He saw with horror then
The faces strained, and blue, and grim, :
Of many hanged men.
A halter hung about each throat,
And as he came the host
Seized each the red rope’s end and smote
The Judge’s naked ghost.
He ran with howls, and like a pack
Of Satan’s wolves they sped, ,
With hate-hot eyes, upon his track
And scourged him as he fled.
With lightning thongs their lashes flay,
With laughter wild they yell;
And so the men he hanged for aye
Will harry him through Hell.
E.D.
