A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 14, 1897.
Mr Dear Myek—
From a dressy aspect, the test-match was a
brilliant suocess. All the ladies’stands exhibited
a gratifying collection of fine raiment, and all the
wearers seemed absorbed in the game—solid rows
of expensive millinery were unbroken all the after-
noon. Country visitors were chiefly on the grand
stand, where backblocks modes were seen in all
their glory. The Western men all look exactly
alike in the same kind of sad-colored grey tweed
of roomy cut, and most of them drape their hats
with fly-nets. The women favored white, stiff
and starchy and yellowed by many washing-days.
After a week’s quenching, plumes in many hats
looked like wet poultry, and it would have been a
boon if Saturday’s rain could have dropped a tear
on some of Monday’s finery and blotted it out for
ever. One or two conspicuously glaring costumes
with tinted hair and lavender cheeks to match
were flaunted up and down in front of the stand.
One was a pink chiffon frock, crisped up like
strawberry ice. A most beautiful dress of white
silk had a warm tinge of yellow glowing through
• the insertion-bars ; also a dove-like grey silk, with
flimsy bodice, represented much capital in its
demure little way. A pink-and-white plaid was
barred like a wax-vesta box. The turban toque was
universal, but the art of putting it on was not.
The woman who tries to arrange the jaunty shape
without a ribbon-loop at the back is lost. Lack-
ing that friendly backstay the structure wobbles
and droops tipsily over the left eyelid.
Somebody writes from Toowoomba to say that
the English cricketers appeared there to quite as
little advantage as at Glen Innes. On the second
day of their stay it is alleged that a lady and
gentleman provided afternoon-tea for them on the
cricket-ground ; and, when they had eaten abund-
antly, a local cricketer offered to introduce them
to their host and hostess. Whereupon the team :
“ No, thank you, we would rather not ” —and
marched out with noses in the air. Now, Messrs,
the Visitors, do you own this rude impeachment ?
The closing of the Society of Artists’ Exhibition
was socially a much - bewailed event. As a
pleasant meeting place, the show went with a rush
from beginning to end. But, totting-up business
done, the officials find that sales weren’t satisfac-
tory. None of the big canvases were carried off.
The artists should push trade by having a big
clearing sale, with a staring calico sign, and offer-
ing such attractions as a cheap line of good reliable
pictures in fast colors, marked down to half-a-
guinea.
A cheap but dressy circle greeted the first night
of “ Arrah - na - Pogue. ” The crowded houses
which the present Lyceum management rejoice to
behold have made a rule of their own about ap-
propriate theatre-garb. The front row is in regu-
lation dress, the boxes are worthy of a high-priced
house, but there is much shabbiness and faded
finery lurking in the back ground. The cheapness
of Australian shows makes visitors open their
eyes. I heard an English Johnnie, who was hang-
ing on to the back of the d.c. on Saturday night,
expressing his amazement at only having to
“part” two shillings for the best part of the
house. The most glitter in the stage-clothes be-
longs to H. Diver’s pink satin suit, in which, as
the rebel M'Coul, he roams around at night among
rocks and ruins. Arrah and her friends wear
much scarlet cloak, and their male escorts bright
trreen coats and robin-redbreast vests. Miss M.
Williamson, in sable pall, goes sweeping by most
of the time in a velvet riding-habit.
After the curtain falls on the quarrel-scene be-
t ween the two Empresses in the “ Royal Divorce,”
the rival royalties come on for an enthusiastic
call, and show their stage characteristics through-
out the applause. Josephine Ferrar seems pale
and exhausted as a lady would who had been
“ having words ” with a supplanter, and hardly
raises her eyes, while the stolid Marie Louise
beams upon the house or concentrates herself into
one killing smile aimed at one of the boxes. Miss
Page occasionally plays the second Mrs. Bona-
parte in her own brown hair, which is more be-
coming but less historically accurate, for Miss P.
studies old plates and authorities to get the true
Austrian carrotty hue for her locks, and usually
sacrifices beauty for soulless accuracy. Mrs.
Maesmore Morris, the understudy, rustled ner-
vously through Marie Louise’s part at a recent
matinee, and lay down to be trampled on by the
mob to great applause. As if any mob could be
so unpicturesque as to even dream of leaving its
bootrnarks on Mrs. Morris’s fair cuticle.
Managers this panto, season have had to deal
with an uncommonly large number of stage struck
damsels of sweet sixteen who respond to the advts.
for “extra ladies,” and meekly wait their turn
among the crowd of regular hands seeking re-
engagement. The professional pantomime girls
are all alike, they all wear enormous hats with
spotted veils twisted into a little knot under the
chin ; every girl calls every other girl “dear” at
sight; and every one has the same contempt for
playing “girl ” and ambition to be “ boy.” The
“lady” contingent are generally high-spirited
girls who think they prefer the stage to type-
writing or teaching as a money-raising concern;
they stand apart from "the other girls,” and
shudder when a blue-chinned super cheerily asks,
** Hullo, Siss, going on in the panto. ? ” and are
usually choked off at the wily manager’s first sug-
gestion that they should don tights. Then when
the aspiring amateurs have been weeded out, and
the final selection made, there is much mourn-
ing and weeping and even fainting among the dis-
appointed who are not chosen.
One would think that just about now was the
very worst time of year to «end out to the world a
new waltz tune. A perspiring public has just
shoved into oblivion memories of the strains footed
last season, and doesn’t feel inclined to take on
any fresh ones till the temperature is lower and
something beyond iced drinks and the lively
mosquito can be taken seriously. For all this,
W. A. Gilder has chosen the present season to
bring out a waltzlet (Nicholson and co.) appro-
priately entitled “La Reine D’Or.” The compo-
sition contains one or two distinctly danceable
movements, and will not tax the abilities of the
most modest performer. “ Love’s Lament,” a
song by Sydney Turnbull (Paling and co.) is a
mournful wail full of "Ah me's ” and other
lamentations. It closes a somewhat depressed
career by a succession of tremulous chords and
arpeggios.
Elsie Hall, the child-pianiste of ten years ago,
has come back to Sydney grown-up, staid, and
spectacled, with a long list of triumphs to recount.
She looks like a girl who would conscientiously go
through her work, and her record, as given to her
interviewers, shows that during the ten years sho
must have put in some hard study. Elsie Hall’s
musical loves are Chopin and Schumann, though
her studious appearance suggests rather a quiet,
steady-going affection for Mendelssohn than a
grand passion for the above stormy and erratic
mcslerns. While having her musical education
made in Germany the young Australian was
brought forward as a juvenile prodigy at a Stutt-
gart concert —an unusual compliment, declares
Miss Hall, "because in Germany the authorities
discountenance precocious platform talent as
much as possible.” The Germans, you see. are a
truly musical folk. On the same principle, in tune-
ful Italy barrel-organs are put down by law.
The Y.M.C.A. Hall was crowded at Mr.
Lawrence Campbell’s recital, which went off suc-
cessfully. Some ingenious decorators coped with
the customary dreariness of the platform and took
away the good-young-man-that-died look which
usually adorns that sanctified structure, by
draping the back wall with a cheerful yellow cur-
tain hung in full rich folds, and frivolously dotting
bright notes of color about by means of red lamp-
shades and green fern-fronds.
An unusual amount of farewelling, accompanied
with flowers and other tributes of affection, took
place over the departure for ’Ome by P. & O.
fast week of Lieut, and Mrs. Harvey and family.
The good-bye on the Quay has become quite a
feature of fashionable life, and gifts are distributed
around as at a wedding. Mrs. Harvey, whose
plump and pleasing personality has been a
familiar sight in many ball-rooms, will shine no
longer locally. Her friends souvenired her to the
extent of a large flat gold heart and numerous
diamond and pearl trinkets.
The duties of a Mayoress in Sydney seem to
consist largely of presiding at wearisome and
meaningless committee-meetings, where the assem-
bled talkers wrangle their shriekful way point by
point through the alleged “ business ” of the after-
noon, waste an hour or two over the hire per
dozen of tea-cups for charity picnics, or discuss
with warmth and much bickering the proper way
to wax a ball-room floor. The woman who can
preside at such functions without flushing nerv-
ously when called upon to read a statement, and
calndy quell half-a-dozen excited women talking
all at once, yet still preserve an unruffled
smile, is the ideal Mayoress. What the average
ladies’ committee wants is to be firmly trodden
upon. Mrs. Matthew Harris, wife of the newly-
elected Mayor, is an experienced committee-
woman, and ought to tread with success. She is
a dignified, handsome matron, usually dressed in
elaborate black.
When woman-questions are treated “in the in-
terests of humanity’’all men must feel thankful
that they don t belong to the down-trodden sex.
Women at first rush madly at a new opening “ for
love” instead of advancing on sound business
give-and-take principles, and find subsequently
that the enthusiastic overwork of the first moment,
is expected from them after the novelty has worn
off. The old brooms must go on sweeping as clean
as the new. That is how the bottom has fallen
out of the nursing biz. The list of duties of
Prince Alfred Hospital probationers in the notice
which Mr. O’Sullivan is laying before the As-
sembly, shows that nursing, as a trade, is too
arduous for anybody unless done on old Sairey
Gamp lines. The mistake lies in considering
nursing a trade at all. Human nature can’t stand
the pressure of the work unless there is a feeling
of personal interest in the patient. To cool a
fevered brow at so much per hour, or smooth a
sufferer’s pillow at regulation rates, can’t be of
much recuperative value in any contract. It is
this confusion of philanthropy with paid labor that
is at the bottom of all the much-vexed hospital-
nurse question.
Hobart folk are grateful to three local Naice
youths who went on a little escapade lately, and
so gave the sleepy town a new topic of conversa-
tion. It wasn’t a very direful deed which the
young men perpetrated, but as it involved spend-
ing money on two or three attractive variety
artists, and led to the stern legal father of two of
the delinquents rushing the hotel at which the
party of girls and young men were having a little
supper, and sending his boys home quelled and
contrite, it has been quite a boon in the way of
sensation in the slumbersome Tas. capital.
Hardly a day passes but the list of alleged co-
respondents in mythical divorce-cases is increased
by well-known names. It’s a kind of joke now—a
very poor one—for meeting acquaintances to ask,
“ Have you heard who’s in it now?” and every
fresh name is greeted with shouts of laughter.
The last two men “ in ” are an ecclesiastical swell
and an advertising draper. As to the printed
denials which have gone forth announcing a Very
Exalted alleged divorce-case (which never had the
slightest foundation in fact), the tale round town
is that “ all the papers have been squared.” The
public absolutely refuses to be deprived of its
Exalted divorce-case.
Photographs of the English cricketers adorn
cricket-shop and other windows. The white men
are all a-penny-plain in their pictures, but Ranji.
has a beautiful twopence-colored variety of photo
all to himself—brown skin, black eyes, blue-and-
whito blazer all painted correctly in. Is the boom
all genuine, or merely a well-timed ad. for the
dusky prince’s book ?
A lecturer discoursed in Sydney, lately, on the
art of reading character from bumps and facial
expression. Nobody believes much in phrenology
now, but everybody has a hankering, more or less,
to read his fellow man's life-story in the lines of
his face. There is no more infallible guide to dis-
position than the face, and there is, also, nothing
more conventional than expression. People can
train their faces to express anything—or nothing.
The Very Nicest people all contort their faces
with, the tame smirk of bored amiability, and a
drawing-room of fashionable matrons reflects the
same restrained simper on every face with irritat-
ing monotony. The lecturer took great stock in
noses as character-indicators, dividing them into
two classes—straight ar.d curly, or Greek and
Roman. Straight-nosed races originate, and
curved beaks propagate new notions ; folks with
pugs and snubs can’t follow their noses to any
distinction according to this expert. Come to
think of it, people are very like their noses.
Worried getters-up of charity concerts and other
shivoos have learned by bitter experience the
worth of the so-called patronage which adorns the
programmes with a list of high-toned names and
eats into the profits by swelling the printer's bill.
The personages written to by the promoters of
every weary little festivity faked up for asuburban
Bethel blighted by debt, or an out-of-harness pro-
fessional also badly stumped for funds, rarely re-
fuse themselves the free ad. of having their names
in the list, but often they return the tickets sent
them, and sometimes turn up at the concert and
send back the remaining tickets, conveniently for-
getting to pay for those used ; often they return
neither tickets nor money. Some sassiety lights
are heavily indebted to the various causes to vvluch
their lofty “patronage” was supposed to give a
propelling shove. They open a ticket-selling ac-
count which runs into double numbers, but they
don’t put in an appearance at settling-time, and
pay no heed to imploring notes of importunate
hon. secs, ui’ging an immediate squaring-up.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Pianiste Elsie Hall was a pale, sinall-sized
10-year-old, all forehead and musical precocity,
when she left Australia for Germany. Even then,
however, she was a hardened old harmoniste,
having begun her musical career at the age of two,
when she clambered on the piano-stool and did a
self-taught rendering of ‘Nelly Bly.’ Now,
after 10 years’ European study, the young Aus-
tralienne returns to her native land, a slim, dark-
haired damsel, far more girlish than her portraits,
in spite of her pince-nez.
“ As a performer, Miss Hall holds trump-cards
in her exquisite delicacy of touch, finished execu-
tion, cultivated intelligence, and artistic feeling.
Nevertheless, she displays less grip than Mark
Hambourg in her attack of stormy, stressful
passages, demanding vim and impetuosity of
treatment.
“ At the first Hall concert, the heroine of the
evening, in white brocade with a fan-tail watteau
train, lavished melody over-liberally on an audi-
ence which seemed lukewarm, in spite of the
stifling night. Sara Lewis, notwithstanding her
becoming blue matelasse, found her hearers still
less receptive ; and Napoleon Boffard fared little
better, though he, too, was arrayed in all his
glory in the shape of pleated shirt-front, diamond
ring, a red rose in his button-hole, and a fascinat-
ing little top-knot curl, trained to stand straight
up on end.
“ Talking of an attractive professional alias for
their tuneful townswoman, Rock ham ptonites
cling to the familiar ‘Florence’ with ‘Fitzroy’
(after the local river) as a surname. There might
be less euphonic titles than ‘Florence ’ or ‘ Flora
Fitzroy,’ though the choice of a name, like a hus-
band, should be left to the future owner’s discre-
tion. As a general thing, however, the poor state
of the marriage-market ties a woman down to
‘ him or nobody,’ giving her virtually as much to
say in the matter as she had in picking ‘ Sylvia
Keren ha ppuch,’ or whatever other insane appella-
tion ‘Pa and Ma’ chose to saddle her with in
those far-off times when they were drunk with the
wine of new parental pride.
“ Talking of the ‘ French Maid ’ clothes, Juliet
Wray is a bird of far finer feather than in the
‘Gay Parisienne.’ Her white evening frock is
quite fascinating enough to excuse Peachey’s
chronic yearning. The French Maid herself, in
parlor-maid uniform, tas a more wayworn expres-
sion than formerly, though later on she drives dull
care away at the Casino in her tri-color ‘ Merrily
round the Maypole ’ dress. Admiral Geo. De
Lara is convincing in his uniform, and alto-
gether Suzette Reeve moved in a brighter world
than when, in a former existence, she tripped
about as Julie Bon-bon.
“ At one time high-kicking juveniles frequently
figured at Austral Salon entertainments. Then
leg-iteins ceased abruptly, and members suspected
a disapproving committee of having put down its
foot. Anyway, at a recent ‘afternoon’ the smart
song-and-dance medleys, contributed by sprightly
Rose Musgrove and M‘Nieol Turner, were re-
ceived with some reserve. Miss Musgrove looked
fair and Frenehy in pale-grey befrilled with blue
chiffon and a hat caught up with black
loops and plumes. But ankle upheavals, even
when conducted on the airiest and fairiest of lines
and with pretty stage kissings and coquetteries
thrown in, seem to need gas, glitter, remoteness,
and what-not. In the garish day the airy dan-
seuse is apt to look like a mere business person
struggling to lift something heavy. As for the
19th century Adonis in plain, every-day costume,
who brandishes his long legs on a gim-crack stage
in the merciless l'ght of afternoon, for all his
smartness nothing under heaven could keep his
performance from setting spectators’ teeth on
edge.
“ At present a sad story showing up the heathen
in his blindness goes the round of local mission
circles. The other Sunday a young lady teacher
in a cabbagee suburb asked little Ah Sam, ‘ Who
was the first man God ever made?’ Her pupil
replied ‘ Lold Blassey ! ’ Benighted ignorance,
wasn’t it? And yet Yellowboy apparently is not
alone in his opinion. One vice-regal offshoot, at
all events, acts as if she agreed with him.
“ A rich Southern parvenu, with one sweet
daughter, chanced, some time ago, to find a
broken-down Oxford man, who could paint, teach
French and piano, tend and drive the horses, all
on the cheap. The retired shopkeeper has no
daughter now. She ‘ disgraced ’ him by marrying
the literary man-of-all-work for love, and they are
waiting, in a poor but respectable manner, for
‘ papa ’ to either relent or leave everything to the
hospitals.
“A Melb. morning pressman was ‘doing’ the
annual shivoo of one of the many children’s skirt-
dancing establishments which infest Melb. when
the dancing ‘ inarm,’ learning his identity, rushed
him off to a small private room. Expecting the
usual whisky and cape-and-dress descriptions of
several columns’ length, the man of notes awaited
developments philosophically. To his surprise
the lady shoved a gold coin on the table and said,
‘ There’s half-a-sovereign, and now I want you to
write me a nice leading article in the Aoegus to-
morrow.’
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Every man in Australia should read the
record of the Suffield case just published by The
Bulletin in a 64 pp. pamphlet, post-free 7d. in
stamps. The proceeds, after payment of ex-
penses, go to the fund for assisting Suffield to have
his case heard at an open public enquiry.
Both Premier Nelson and Secretary Tozer
told Q. Assembly that, if the Federation Enabling
Bill, providing for the popular election of Conven-
tion delegates, was carried, they would not stand
as candidates. Nelson said that “it would be
more his duty to stand aside and watch the pro-
gress of events.” Tozer merely stated wearily
that “ he had no desire ” to stand.
Cooktown (Q.) Independent, after indi-
vidually greasing each member of the Boodle
Ministry, remarks that but for “the time wasted
on the Q.N. Bank report and other matters of
minor importance,” these people would have done
something really great this session and been a
blessing to the country generally. “Minor im-
portance” is particularly good in such a connec-
tion.
Anarquia : “ How delighted I am with the
full reports —the many columns—about the gowns
worn by the female baggages of the aristocracy in
England at their races, fancy balls, and what not.
The upper-classes used to have a wise instinct,
which kept them from publishing such inflamma-
tpry details, but got so eaten up with vanity
that they say, ‘ After us, the Deluge. It will last
our time.’”
A Q. shoe-shop proprietor found his best
workman hanging under a staircase behind his
shop the other Monday morning when he opened
his establishment. Did he cut down the body?
Not he. He ran around, with bulging eyes, to
call his neighbors, or passing acquaintances, to
“come and see tfie thing.” Tr-en, on advice, he
went for a policeman to officially cut down the
corpse. In this case the man was dead enough,
but in seven such cases out of ten a live man is
left to dangle and strangle because of the absurd
law-fright which deters a private citizen from
cutting down suspended man-meat. What the
exact nature of the generally prevalent fear is re
the cutting-down of a suspended man or body
would be hard to define ; but the general public
impression seems to be that “ you’ll get into
trouble if you do.” Why ? And how ?
Under severe pressure, Premier Reid so
far agreed to let up on the widow that estates
worth less than .£lOOO were to be exempt from pro-
bate duty in N.S.W. As £lOOO, at present inte-
rest rates, means about ,£35 a year, the proposal
to take .£lO from the old woman, to start with—
about three and a-half months’ income—was
pretty rough. Even the revised idea, however,
was far less merciful than the Victorian system,
which Premier Reid declared he was going to fol-
low, and this was the ostensible reason why the Leg.
Council threw the Bill out so decisively. Victoria
exempts all estates under £1000; on estates under
£5OOO, it exempts the first £lOOO ; and where the
inheritor is the widow, child, or grandchild of the
departed capitalist, and the estate is worth less
than £50,000, only half-rates are levied. The
Vic. system may be a trifle too lax, but the
N.S.W. one, even with the £lOOO exemption, cer-
tainly wouldn’t be.

Cabled that the body of Miss Arbuthnot,
an English lady overwhelmed by an Alpine
avalanche 30 years ago, has at last been found in
the ice. Death had preserved in youthful freshness
what would otherwise by now be a withered old
woman.
Fitzgibbon, of Melb. Metropolitan Board
of Words, believes that the big Vic. strike of
1890 was knocked out by a little “pome” of his,
and he reprints the “pome” in proof. If that
poem killed the strike, the strike perished without
rhyme or reason.
Vosper, of the Mane, Westralian M.P.,
instantly attempted to atone for doing so conven-
tional a thing as getting married by spending his
honeymoon at, of all places on God’s earth, White
Feather—a treeless waste relieved only by heaps
of day, more or less suggestive of graves.
Vic. : “Graham Berry was undoubtedly
the best Agent-General Victoria ever had, and he
might have kept the billet to this day, only
Munro wanted it for himself. James M. only
kept it a few weeks ; the stink of his broken banks
made his recall absolutely necessary. Neither
Munro nor Gillies nor Smith was a patch on
Berry as A.G. Now the billet is vacant why not
let the old man have another turn at it. There is
no better candidate in view at present.”
Grevillean : “ Napoleon 111.
wanted Princess Mary of Teck,
before she was Teck (of course,
though, it is never surprising in
a monarch to want a lady after-
wards, for he is above the pre-
judices of inferior men). So she
had the pick of the French or the
English throne. Then Nap. went
for Princess Adelaide of Hohen-
lohe, and, it is said, would have got
her but for the opposition of a very
near relative, the British Queen.
Having hawked himself to the
Czar’s sister and all round the Euro-
pean courts, he popped to Eugenie
in an alcove at a State ball in the
Tuileries. Before this, Vandam
relates, he cried to take advantage
of her after returning from a hunt,
at Compiegne, but she struck him
on the face with her riding-whip.”
Dear Bulletin,—Re Mar-
garet Catch pole. Born 1773. Tried
before Chief Baron Macdonald and
convicted of horse-stealing, Aug.
9, 1797. Sentenced to death. Re-
prieved—seven years. Served a
little over two years, then broke
gaol, and bolted with her lover.
Was caught, tried again 3rd Aug.,
1800. Condemned to death. Sen-
tence altered to transportation for
life. Arrived at Sydney Dec. 20,
1801. Assigned to Mrs. Palmer,
matron of Female Orphan Asylum.
Free 1811, married 1812. Died
Sept., 1841. Lived for many years
in a cottage on the site of the
present General Post Office : it
was standing in 1860. Theßeibeys
of Tasmania are her descendants,
and, all things considered, ought to
be proud of the fact. If they in-
herit her virtues, Tasmania should
be proud of them.— G.B. N.B.—
There used to be a fine portrait
in oils of M.C. in the lodge-room,
Hero of Waterloo Hotel, Fort-
street.
Rossi, one-time police-magis-
trate at Sydney, had a weakness
for lecturing delinquents. An in-
corrigible was brought before him
one day—an Irishman. “Aha,
villano ! ” screamed the magis-
trate, “ah-h-h-h ! is it you again
I see? I will give-a you sixa
montha, brute, to eata of the
hominy ha!” “Faith, yer
honor,” replied the man, “ I’d just
as soon eat skilly any day av the
week as maccaroni—so I would —
and that’s all, they tell me, they
get where you came from.” “ Six-a
month-a ! ” yelled the Italian, “ in
the irons.” “It can’t be done,
yer honor,” said the prisoner,
“ even in this counthry. Sure,
ye’re not George the Fourth ! ”
“ Takea him away-a,” sobbed
Rossi, “and hanga him to the
lamposta.” In the old days the
Sydney magistracy often threw
awful sentences about promiscu-
ously, and its understrapper got
over the difficulty by entering the
maximum legal sentence in the
book. Sometimes the maximum
was three months, and the beak
had wanted to have the prisoner
flogged and disembowelled, but
they had a way of getting over
these little discrepancies.
When George Bean, the Vic. Lands Office
accountant in trouble over illegal adhesion to
a sum of £7BB, was before the police-court it
transpired that the departmental cash is inspected
at the end of each month, so the accountant could
blew a whole month’s takings—one-twelfth of a
wear’s land revenue —at poker if he liked before
there was any serious risk of discovery. And the
man who had such a tremendous temptation put
in his road received a salary of £lBB a year—after
13 years’ service—to keep him straight.
Victoria owes a deep debt of ingratitude
to one “ Deaffy ” Thompson, who, in days of old.
used to assist Duncan Gillies’ political career, and
finally bequeathed £5OO to the same undeserving
object. Still, there was some unconscious satire
about Thompson ; £5OO wasn’t much, but it was as
much as Duncan deserved, and quite enough to
lend all the encouragement called for to all the
career that Duncan was capable of.
When the Melb. City authorities looked
for the landlord of the burnt “ Fink’s Buildings”
that they might order him to shift the dAbris, not
a soul would plead guilty to the ownership. Ap-
parently about the biggest structure in Melbourne
was an orphan which collected its own rents auto-
matically. It is seriously alleged, however, that
Bismarck is the real proprietor, and also that he
owns two other huge tenements thereabouts.
Keast, the young man who has just been
awarded four years’ gaol in Maoriland for for-
gery, has been evangelist, circus clown, carter,
engine-driver, also it is alleged that he once eloped
with a waitress. His evangelical career began at
Bendigo (Vic.), where he appeared as one who
saw visions. His most successful dream included
a sight of the enemies who had spoken despitefully
of him hanging by their tongues on hooks driven
into one of the main girders of hell.
John Norton, of Sydney Truth, now has
in hand five or more first-class libel-actions, in-
cluding the two criminal-libel charges brought
against him by Lands Minister Carruthers. John’s
tenacious pugnacity recalls the bull-pup at whose
nose the present writer saw the end of a Manilla
rope dangled from the top of a tall warehouse-lift
the other day. The pup instantly seized the rope,
fixed his teeth in it, refused to let go, and was
hauled right up to the top landing, five storeys
high. The Bulletin hopes that John won’t let
go the rope.

The illustration of a new edition
of Dickens should prove a tough job
even for Phil May and C. D. Gibson.
The personalities of Dickens’s leading
characters have become so crystallised,
as it were, in the public consciousness
that an entirely fresh reading would
probably be resented, even if it could
be attained. We can no more have a
new Pecksniff or Pickwick or Sl-iggins
or Swiveller than a new Sliako'p.-are
or Napoleon.
Old Possum : “To my mind none
of tli£ various obituaries 1 have read of
the late ex-Judge Windeyer fairly put
the man as lie was. Long of intellect,
strong on the scon- of duty, and abso-
lutely unassailable in his devotion to
the principles of moral rectitude. This
was the this side-up Windeyer. Weak,
cruel, and stubborn was the other Win-
deyer—the dominant Windeyer—the
Windeyer, totally without ballast, who
sat upon the wall of impartiality and
dropped bricks of partiahty upon
those who came, or were dragged, be-
fore him. Impulse, indignation, and
a dangerous gift of intuition were
his ruling incentives ; to accuse, with
him, was to condemn, and to con-
demn, to call for revenge in the
guise of Justice. In short, lie was
a woman —a new woman.”

The lately-deceased Mrs. Shirley Baker,
wife of the fallen boss dictator, and missionary-
premier of Tonga, claimed descent from Henry
VIII. of England, and was supposed to present a
marked personal resemblance to Queen Elizabeth.
Albert Nixon, who recently tried to
murder his wife and her friend, H. M. Addison,
then chairman of S.A. State Bank, has had his
term of 18 months’ gaol reduced to nine months.
It will be remembered that he threatened to
“ complete his work ” when he came out, which
makes the situation interesting.
G. H. Reid is the only one among recent
N.S.W. Premiers who had not to rough it in early
manhood. Even Alexander Stuart took a turn at
the gold-diggings and live-stock trucking. Jen-
nings, Farnell, Dibbs, Parkes all earned their
bread for years with the work of their hands;
but Reid has belonged to the lily-handed brigade
all his life.
Philpott, accused of having shot his mate
Hawthorne from behind and buried him in a misfit
grave at the Hutt Valley (M.L.), is almost the
only person accused of a sensational murder in
recent years who wasn’t piously dragged up. The
“ draggings-up ” of Philpott, so far as is known at
present, were of the godless variety—which is a
refreshing change.
Concerning a recent par. to the effect that
Judge A. H. Simpson has never yet taken his
seat on N.S.W. Equity Bench, but always tackles
it standing—a Sydney solicitor, who lately had
judgment given against him, wanted his barrister
to apply for a new trial on the ground that the
court wasn’t sitting during the first hearing. The
barrister refused, so a beautiful legal point was
lost.
Melb.: “ Demise, barrister Duigan. His
long, strawy. Dundreary-like weepers of whiskers
bordered a sharp, shrewd aquiline hatchet-visage
of parchment, whose indication was not so very
much in advance of his capacity, though, indeed,
he always looked just a little more clever than any
ordinary man could manage to be. I daresay he
would have made nearly as good a County Court
Judge as Casey.”
Vic. : “Graham Berry should get a sub-
stantial dole from the police. As Premier, he
squelched a plan, by Chief Commissioner Standish
and Supt. Winch, for the establishment of a
cadet school to furnish gentlemen officers as in-
spectors, leaving the ranker only the meagre
chance of promotion to sergeant. Berry’s stroke
of promoting six sergeants to the rank of inspector
revolutionised the force, from a democratic point
of view. Every constable ought to look in the
pockets of his first captured drunk for something
grand to put down to a Berry testimonial.”
The Sewer Bard, Fitzgibbon, has un-
earthed the poem in which he pointed the nioral
of the maritime strike, and had it reprinted in an
Argus letter. In the Pantheon of Bathos it forms
the twin companion poem to Douglas Sladen’s
“condensed milk ” lyric. Sample slabs
It plainly says to labor,
Be more thoughtful of your neighbor;
Don’t sacrifice the innocent to gain your selfish ends.
Farmers, traders, clerks, shopkeepers,
Squatters, mothers, babes (poor weepers),
Be certain if you do they will not reckon you as friends.
Ricardo: “Melb. financiers acknowledge
that a past-master of their craft has departed in
Robert Guidemann, ex-Under Treasurer. His
name betokens Swedish blood. On Black Wednes-
day, Berry, Lalor, and co. did not dare to sack
Guidemann, who was practically in himself the
whole Victorian financial system. Guidemann’s
Under-Treasurership dates back to the wild,
whirling times of M'Culloch and Higinbotliam.
And, at the age of 70, the veteran suicided by
hanging. Old age, melancholia, and loss of speech
through partial paralysis were the causes of his
exit.”

Aboriginalities.
In a little N.S.W. country town recently a
shearer got on the spree, and came to blows with
a local resident who was also spreeing. It was a
very cordial scrimmage. The shearer knocked his
man out, and a little later found himself collared
by the local policeman and run in for being drunk
and disorderly. He was taken straight into the
court, and found tho gory ruins of his late anta-
gonist sitting on the bench. Before the prisoner
had got over his first astonishment at this unex-
pected re-union his old friend spat out two teeth,
and mopped up his blackened eye, and passed
sentence of two years’ hard labor. When the
magistrate had sobered up the C.P.S. had an
interview with him, as a result of which the
prisoner was again hauled into court and asked if
he would like to have a little knocked off bis sen-
tence. He replied that nothing would please him
better, and the Bench thereupon took off the two
years, fined him a pound, and told him to get out
of the town. The shearer paid the fine, saddled
his horse, and got out “quick and lively.”

Mbs ton on th j Gayndah (Q.) aboriginals :
“ Painfully conspicuous is the number of quad-
roons and octoroons ; some of the younger girls
being sufficiently light-coloured to be mistaken for
Europeans. The Chinese strain is apparent in a
decided majority. Some of the young girls are of
fine physique and have beautiful eyes. With
regular diet and proper care these girls would
grow into very attractive womanhood. More
than one respectable European resident is mar-
ried to women showing both aboriginal and
Chinese ancestry, and these women have made
excellent wives.” Meston thinks they should not
be married to full-blooded aboriginals. To whom
then ? As a rule the white man won't marry them.
I lie task of suitably mating quadroons, octoroons,
and nondescripts of Chinese and aboriginal ’’
ancestry might well puzzle a wiser head than even
that of the Sacred Ibis. In nearly all countries in
the world the hybrid female ’is the sport of
both parent races and rarely recognised as a fit-
ting mate for either.

for an opening, but instantly turned and landed
mm one on the jaw. He looked a bib surprised
for a second; however, he returned the compli-
ment, and as the tram rattled over the Duck
Haver bridge l measured my length in the coal-
bunker. At. Granville he said he would report
me for striking him when on duty. I replied that
I would repoit Am for striking the first blovv. He
6wore that / started the fight by hitting him on
the jaw. To which statement I gave him the lie
direct, which led to an agreement to settle matters
with our fists when we reached Campbelltown.
When we were steaming out of Granville, and I
opened the fire-box door to pub on some more coal,
my mate picked up something that was lying on
the foot-plate in front of the fire-box. At Fair-
field he handed me a dead bat, with the remark:
‘ There, you d d fool, there’s the fellow that
struck the first blow.’ ”

A Bulletin correspondent : I recently
travelled the “Gilbert” district and heard several
stories of the gang. Saw the tree under which
Gilbert was shot, and the ruins of the hut he last
slept in. Two of his gang were once run pretty
close by the police. They reaohed a house. Two
of the girls dressed in male attire, and mounted
the rangers’ horses, carrying firearms. The police
saw them and at once gave chase. The rangers
got away. After a merry ride of some miles, tt o
girls surrendered. Tableau ! On another occa-
sion Gilbert’s horse knocked up, so he went to
the stable of a pub. kept by one Magennis, and
took a fine-looking mare, the property of the son
of the house. The boy showed fight until Gilbert
told him who he was, and gave his promise : o
return the mare. A few days later she was found
tied to the fence, with a boy’s brand-new saddle
on her. One of Capb. Moonlight’s amusements
while he held up Wantabadgery was booting the
men who showed signs of disapproval. They were
ordered to stoop, and the captain then drew back
and let them have it. Everyone suffered the
humiliation at first time of asking, as the request
was backed up with a loaded revolver.

denly. The instant the tree crashed down, a rock
weighing fully a cwb. whizzed over the settler’s

Editor Bulletin.—From our local (Q.)
paper:—
A man named John Oatey died suddenly at Nocundra
last week from a general break-up. No enquiry was con-
sidered necessary. Con Yek, one of our pioneer Chinese
gardeners, passed away yesterday and was buried to-day.
He was 68 years of age, and suffered from a complication
of diseases, but gallantly hawked his “ cabbagee” around
until about a week ago.
Observe ! the white man merely “ died ”
pegged-out, anything you like. No inquiry neces-
sary. The Chinky “passed away”—gradually—
perhaps piece-by-piece—while dispensing—ugh !
cabbagee to the grateful Caucasian trash.— Exile.

In connection with these easy-running machines, we have just issued a daintily
got-up brochure, fully illustrated , entitled “ Cycling for Ladiescontaining
some very useful hints for beginners , and for those who are anxious to look as
smart as possible when wheeling. A really good hint given and received at
the right time often decides the all-important question of whether a lady looks
awkward or elegant when cycling. For those interested in cycling from the
racing standpoint , we have also issued a little souvenir booklet , containing an
account of the distinguished
rider , C. F. Barden , whose appearance ALONE on the scratch mark of the
Austral Wheel Race made the Swift the cynosure of all eyes. This Xmas
season we are sending to all our old customers an entertaining Xmas story ,
entitled “ Xmas in Australiaillustrated by our own special artist. Should
any of these esteemed patrons be accidentally omitted from our list which is
a very long one a post-card to any of our depots will command instant
attention.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Recently a M.L. policeman received £6 from th®
Govt, police reward fund for saving the life of a boy who
had wandered on to the straight of a certain racecourse
just as a galloping finish was on. The boy, apparently,
was right under the hoofs of a horse when the man in
blue snatched him away scatheless, but not quite in time
to save himself from being knocked down and much hurt
by another animal. Now, at the time of this particular
race, the backers of the favorite (which didn’t win) and
the newspaper - scribes (who most likely baoked the
favorite) said that—to quote a local print exaotly—“ the
chances of were spoilt by an over-offioious policeman
running across the track and throwing the horse out of
position.” t t , ,
They were fishing off Manly, and in a few minutes
hooked and hauled up four young pointer sharks, which
they put their knives into and let go. Same thing went
on for half-an-hour until lawyer Ayrault Burns brought up
a shark nearly six feet long, which, with his arm under
the fin, he yanked into the boat. Then, two or three
thumps on the nose with a stretcher, and the fish was
apparently dead. “Full of this, boys,” said Burns,
“ let's pull in.” They did, and about 20 yards from shore
Burns slid the shark over the gunwale, thinking him done
for—but as he was sinking he moved his tail, and they
yelled at each other for not knifing him. They ran the
boat on to the beach and were getting out when, swim-
ming along by the shore, came the shark, bumping and
floundering about, and evidently not knowing where he
was. Burns ran into the water, put his arms round the
fish under the fins, lifted him, and threw him kicking on
to the sand. Some hundreds of persons were collected on
the beach, and were so amazed to see the gallant lawyer
rush into the water and seize the shark that a crowd
gathered round the hero, who stood in apparent dignified
indifference to the whispers of “ That’s him —that’s him 1
Caught a shark with his hands!” “By gum, it’s the
hottest thing I ever saw ! ” —and so on. And when
presently an elderly gentleman asked, “Did you really
catch that shark with your hands, sir ? ” he answered—
“ Certainly, sir ; I have caught many that way down at
the Islands. Come on, you chaps 1” and he strode mag-
nifioently away.

A Southern sporting community takes great in-
terest in the local angling competitions, and a good deal
of money is wagered at times on the results. The fact of
onesporting genthaving won several competitions straight
off aroused suspicion, and he was watched the night
before a recent match, and seen to plant and anchor half-
a-dozen big live bream in a certain portion of the river.
The rules don’t permit of any boat starting to fish on the
fishing-ground before sunrise. Before daybreak the
watoher shifted the suspeoted one’s “plant” to ground
his friend intended to fish, and the sporting gent, during
the matoh, had the mortification of seeing his own
“p'ant” lugged up by a rival, who eventually won the
match and let the sporting man in for a heavy amount he
had wagered at odds on himself ; and he dared not enter
a protest either.

Dear Bulletin, — That was a plucky wheel-
ing trip of Murif’s across the continent alone.
Away far north to Barrow Creek the camel-track runs
through stretches of God-forsaken desert country, lonely
laps of sand and spinifex, with here and there “ good
going,” where many caravans have trodden the path as
hard as a cinder-track.—Overlander.
» * * •
Prof. R. C. Carpenter, of Cornell University,
U.S., after elaborate experiments on cycle friction, con-
cludes than no form of gearing can possibly equal the
best chain for efficiency and durability. With the best
chain, the frictional loss is i to J per cent, of the total
power transmitted, this result being attained with a
chain which had previously been ridden over 2000 miles
by a rider soaling over list. With ohains not so well-
made, the loss was 2 to 6 per cent.; even an old chain
which did not fit its sprocket properly lost less than 10
per cent, of power. The very best level-geared bicyoles
lost four times as much as a good average chain, and over
six times as much as the best chain.

“ If men must gamble,” quoth Tom Price, M.P.
(S. A.), “ let the plague-spot fester in one plaoe instead of
allowing it to spread through the community, and force
them to that quarantine of the betting disease, the race-
course. Then the coward, the sneak, and the hypocrite,
who affects to oondemn gambling and who yet slyly puts
his money on the street and shop ‘ totes,’ will be forced
to do his betting openly.” In common with other Labor-
members, Price demands that if the totalisator is given a
monopoly the raoing clubs be compelled to start a “ Is.
tote ” for the use of the poor.

A new variation of the confidence-trick which
failed at a late Hawkesbury meeting. Young man from
the bush, apparently a “ mug,” is accosted by dapper
swell with a request to see race-card, which leads to talk,
and statement by dapper one that he is waiting for his
uncle, who will presently bring him the straight tip for the
next race. Uncle arrives, irreproachably got up, says he
is not quite sure yet; is introduced to bush youth, de-
parts, returns just as race is starting, a little breathless.
“ Only just in time—Echo carries the pot, it’s certain. I
got two hundred on for myself, fifty for you, and twenty,
five for your friend.” Bush youth appears to regret that
his cash is held by his mate, now lost to view in the crowd.
Swell accompanies him to hunt up invisible mate, and
they continue hunting till the race is over and Eoho a
bad second. Then the bush youth suddenly lands the
uncle a stiffener on the nose and yells “ Police 1 ” “If
Echo had won,” he reported afterwards, “ I’d have made
him pay up or knocked his gizzard out.” When the police
did arrive the bush youth was gone, and a disconsolate
uncle was pouring nose-blood over the railings on to the
oourse, and the uncle’s partner was denouncing him as a
flat and a bloomin’ mug.
* * * »
Great things are usually prophesied about crack
scullers’ sons, but they seldom come-off. Bill Beach’s
son, as a youngster, promised well, but he never even-
tuated, and there are several young scullers able to beat
him on Lake Illawarra. Mick Rush’s son also was spoken
of as a likely champion, but he too faded out. Young
Pearce, son of “Footy” Pearce, looks the most likely
amongst the present lot, but time alone will tell. The
eighteen-year-old son of American professional Ten Eyck
this year beat all the English amateurs at Henley, the
victory being received in a dead silence by the crowd for
an alleged breach of etiquette in doing his trial on the
quiet. However, there is usually a deathly quiet when,
the Britisher is beaten on his own dunghill.

This is the sun’s picture of Arthur Hoey
Davis, the young Queenslander whose selection-
sketches, over the pen-name of “ Steele Rudd,” 1
have delighted so many readers of The Bulletin.
Mr. Davis was born on the Darling Downs in ’6B
—eighth in a family of 13. The father was (and
is) a warm-hearted Welshman ; mother, Irish.
Of young Davis’s history till 74 nothing is re-
corded; after that, when he wasn’t watering cows
or driving plough-horses, or hunting kangaroos or
bears, he was at school—until the age of 12 or so.
He doesn’t know if he was very bright at school ;
ARTHUR HOEY DAVIS .
Photo., Poulsen, Brisbane.
but the schoolmaster did once call him the smartest
boy in his class—for a good while he was the only
one in the class. Stuck by the selection till he
was 18; but the last four years were mostly
spent on stations. Made a trip or two
with cattle, and took a turn at . Jiorse-
breaking. At 18, found himself deciding be-
tween the Bush and an office. Preferred the
Bush, but was persuaded into the office. Charmed
with oity life for about a month ; then yearned to
clear out—but would have had to walk, and never
liked footing it. Until he entered the office Mr.
Davis had only read one book beside school-
books ; and he was 20 before he discovered there
was such a paper as The Bulletin. Now he is
making up literary leeway, and at every holiday-
opportunity getting back to the selection. And
when The Bulletin publishes his collected stories
—some time next year—it may be found that old
Dad, and Dave, and Joe, and Sarah have come ta
add permanently to the realistic humor of our
growing literature.
The N.S.W. Lager Bier Co., at Waterloo,
Sydney, opened its big brewery, the other day,
-with a magnificent fizz and bang. An army of
leading citizens—2oo ’strong—led by the capacious
Premier Reid, rolled up to do honor to the festive
and momentous occasion. And Premier Reid
never before looked so jolly and fat as he did with
his face beaming behind a glass of the new brew.
And thereafter, being primed with his subject, he
spoke with the joyous success which his inspira-
tion warranted. He gave the bier the praise it
deserves for its admirable digestive qualities, for
its trifling percentage of alcohol, and for its deli-
■ciousness of flavor. It is necessary in a climate
like N.S.W. for a brewery to be independent of
■climate, therefore the new brewery makes its own
temperature. All air used in making the bier is
filtered through sterilised cotton filters well known
ito bacteriologists. All water used is perfectly
filtered—how is a profound secret. After the
bier is boiled for three hours in the immense
■copper kettle, sterilised so that bacteria and germs
are, rendered harmless, the problem for the brewer
then is to save it from contact with bacteria-
laden agents such as air and water. All practical
brewers know that this desideratum cannot be
reached with the ordinary mechanical appliances.
In this brewery, however, the bier is passed from
■one boiling kettle direct, through the deckebach—
a patent cooler —so constructed that the bier does
not contact with the atmospheric air. Filtered air,
however, plentifully saturates the liquid with
oxygen—the essential for good and healthy fer-
mentation. When cooled the bier reaches the
sterilised starting-tanks, where the yeast gained
by Dr. Hansen’s system is “ pitched.” So far, the
bier has been cooled under absolutely sterile con-
ditions and “ pitched ” with absolutely pure yeast.
Next, the bier is transferred to steel glass
enamelled tanks (3000 gals. each) wherein fermen-
tation is conducted under a partial vacuum ex-
cluding infected atmospheric air. After fermen-
tation the bier is cooled to the freezing point of
water; then filtered through two high-grade
filters; and on leaving these it is perfectly bril-
liant. It is now re-charged with its own gas—the
carbonic acid gas generated during fermentation.
The old way of giving the bier its life (froth and
foam) was by adding 10 to 25 per cent, of Kraeusen
■ a very young bier which, though it re-started
fermentation and generated the needful carbonic
acid gas, necessarily deteriorated the older bier’s
quality. To sum up—the new lager bier, from the
time it leaves the kettle boiling hot, until it
reaches the glass of the consumer, never once
comes in contact with atmospheric air.*
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-

POLITICAL
POINTS
Vic. M.P. : “ Let’s see. There’s Income-
tax—and Berry-tax. D ! ”
When J. P. Abbott drops out of Went-
worth, Allan Lakeman, formerly of the neighbor-
ing Balranald, will probably drop in.
N.S.W. Leg. Council threw out the Probate
Duties Bill by 28 votes to 9. Of Reid’s 10 Free-
trade Democrats, one-half didn’t roll up.
“Hypothesis again ! ” interjected Premier
Nelson, in Q. Assembly. “ Hypothesis? ” turned
Kidston, “ the hon. gentleman is more like hip-
popotamus ! ”
Queensland Labor-member Stewart : “I
seldom pray, but I do pray with all my heart that
the Premier may be removed to another place, for
which he is better suited. And, you know, ‘ the
prayer of a righteous man availeth much.’ ”
During the “stonewall” in Q. Assembly,
the other morning—when the House had been
sitting over 22 hours—Labor-member Dawson re-
ceived per messenger a parcel. Opening it, he ex-
tracted a large rock bearing the inscription
“ Every little helps ” !
All the members of Q. Assembly were
ready to spit at the distant carcase of MTlwraith.
But even members of the Opposition were ready
to fawn on Premier Nelson—the man who is still
on the spot able to dispense patronage that can be
converted into dollars. Human nature.
Tas. Parliament is just about adjourning
until April. The cognoscenti aver that Braddon
will never lead another session—he suffers, The
Bulletin is sorry to hear, from a serious internal
malady. Another zoological collection (Fysh—-
Bird) is quite on the cards as the next Ministry.
According to Longmore, there are now
only four teetotallers in the Victorian Legislative
Assembly. In the glorious days of Munro and
Mirams, they were about 18 strong, and when old
James, of the Federal Bank, reached the Premier-
ship they reckoned the Gold Tea millennium had
begun.
Gillies is now fairly reaching out for the
leadership of Vic. Opposition. Over the Danger-
ous Buildings Removal Bill he simply brushed
away the impossible MTntyre, and took command,
and the Tories followed him from force of old
habit. Even the Age sees Gillies leader of the
Opposition in the near future.
If a man be tried for his life on a murder
charge, the Law is of opinion he should be defended,
and the Crown provides (somewhat stingily)
therefor. But if a man be tried for his life on a
charge of rape, the Law is of opinion he should not
be defended, and acts accordingly. Smith, of
Gympie, recently sentenced to death for the latter
crime, was totally undefended during the trial.
Moral ? If you commit rape wind up with murder,
and things will go better with vou.
REID AND UPPER HOUSE REFORM.
" When the appeal to the constituencies is made
the electors will have a ... specific cry before
them.” —Reid in “Telegraph.”
At Trenwith’s instigation, a clause was
inserted in the Vic. Dangerous Buildings Remo-
val Act providing a minimum-wage of Is. 3d. per
hour for workmen engaged in removing danger-
ous buildings, and large compensation in ease of
injury or death through the premature collapse of
the structure. The minimum-wage idea keeps
moving on. Strange to say, the Vic. Leg. Council
accepted this Democratic idea quite peacefully.
Berry and MTlwraith, both ex-Premiers,
both great men in their day, and both announced
as practically penniless, rise up as joint evidence
of the unprofitableness of polities. Parkes’ con-
stant poverty was proverbial. Dibbs was only saved
by getting a billet and a legacy in his old age.
Robertson would have been a stony-broke patriarch
but for his .£lO,OOO grant. Vogel is supposed to
be hustling for a very moderate living. Ditto,
James Munro.
AT THE CHURCH CAKE FAIR.
LYDY STALLKEEPER : “ I'll give you a kiss if you buy one of my kikes.”
BOLD BAD BALD OLD GENT : “ I hope, my dear, your kisses are lighter them your cakes.”
S.M. Herald: “The only desire of a
number of gentlemen,” Mr. Reid pointed out,
“was to land him in a deficiency. . . . He
had pledged his word to the people that that
should never happen under his administration.”
And the callous N.S.W. Government Statistician
has just published another annual return in which
he charges Premier Reid with having added over
£440,000 to the accumulated deficit of N.S.W.,
and asserts that the last year when there wasn’t a
deficit was 1894.
A shriek of horrid mirth went up from the
Sydney Free - raid press about “ M‘Laurin’s
widow ” when Mac., M.L. A., objected to Reid’s
proposed taxation of very small estates (com-
prising, perhaps, some clothes, and a silver watch,
and the cottage furniture) left by the old man
who is dead. Yet, when Dibbs’s duties went on,
these same papers were full of bitter grief about
the alleged girl who found that the price of
her dying brother’s cod-liver oil had “ riz.” Who
was that girl with the dying brother, by the way ?
Bryan O’Loghlen, Victorian M.L.A., says
that for the House to adopt the referendum is to
abdicate its function. Pathetic gag. What are
the functions of a democratic House, anyhow ?
It has only one —to do the will of the people—and
the House can’t be abdicating its functions in
allowing the people to explain what their will
really is. According to the bald Irishman from
Warrnambool, Parliament is an institution created
by the people for the purpose of denying the
people those things which the people most ar-
dently desires.
Special to Wanganui (M.L.) Chronicle,
Oct. 30 :
The most severe speech made in the debate (on the
Financial Statement) was contributed by Mr. Taylor,
member for Christchurch, who literally dragged the
Premier all over the floor of the House, and metaphori-
cally kicked and buffeted him to his heart’s content. The
Premier, meantime, sat with pale and cloudy eyes, and
squirmed on his seat. . . .
The author of which glittering piece of special
journalesque is a M.P., who is spoiling for the
position of Minister for Education —agin the time
when Seddon is defeated.
Q. Chief Justice Griffith’s explanation to
the Brisbane newspapers concerning his Govt.’s
borrowing of £600,000 at the end of ’92, to pre-
vent Q. from becoming a defaulter (to prevent the
Q.N.B. from going bung), has polished up public
recollection that, the following year, Sam made
the salary of the next Chief Justice £3500 a year
(£lOOO increase) and rushed into the luxurious
haven himself. Did he foresee ?
The idea of the Poxton lands administra-
tion (Q.) is that one-fourth of the “plums” re-
purchased from the squattah should be reserved
for Englishmen. “It is not fair to ask the English
farmer to come out here unless we can place him
among conditions approximating to those he
leaves.” The mere Queenslander is to keep on
pioneering in the back-blocks and the scrubs,
while the new-ehum is to be given, on easy terms,
farms ready-made !
The proposed Aliens Restriction Act shuts
out the Chinese missionary from N.S.W. in the
same way as any other Chow. Which is quite
right; but why, in the name of logic, doesn’t some
one try his hand at a law to prevent the anaemic,
hysterical Cornstalk she-dreamer going on missions
to China ? If China isn’t strong enough to eject
that lady, is it fair, in view of the above legisla-
tion, for this country to take advantage of its
weakness? Besides, the periodical yarn about
the horrid butchery of that mission girl is fast
ruining the digestion of this young country.
N.S.W. Parliament has as little love for
Local Government as for Federation. The man
who can’t keep in Parliament by his ability or his
eloquence keeps there by hustling industriously
after grants, and the Local Government system
would knock that resource from under him. Of the
£31,000 spent last year on unclassified roads,
Garrard got £1568 for his electorate, Frank
Farnell £314 for his, and Smailes not one penny
for his. Now, if Smailes were a Gladstone (which
he isn’t), what chance, as constituencies go, would
he have for re-election in opposition to either of
the other two ?
Premier Turner asked Yic. Assembly to
hustle through the Dangerous Buildings Removal
Bill, because the burnt shell of Fink’s huge struc-
ture was tottering over a busy city thoroughfare,
and liable to wreck adjacent buildings in its fall
at any moment, and the City Council had no
power of compulsory removal. Then Tory Mad-
den got up and from sheer force of habit entered
his usual protest against “hasty legislation,”
Madden will probably be heard at the general
resurrection telling Gabriel, the archangel, that
the matter requires deep consideration, and
mustn’t be hurried.
The official cry re Suffield (and in all other
instances where a sensational miscarriageTjf justice
is the outcome of a criminal trial) is—“ We must
not interfere with the verdict of the jury.” As if
the jury-verdict were held sacred in other cases
where the finding is against facts ! If a jury in a
tinpot libel-case returns a verdict against evidence,
that verdict cannot stand. If a jury finds against
facts in a trumpery promissory-note dispute, the
verdict is upset. Why should a verdict be only
“ sacred ” when it involves a man’s life or liberty?
Why should a citizen be able to appeal against an
illogical verdict only when it affects his breeches-
pocket, and not when it affects his neck or his
liberty ?
H. : “Even Gillies, an inexpensive old
man with neither wife nor family, could not save
largely as Agent-General. Berry entered on the
billet just as his three girls were at the most ex-
pensive period of education. He had to keep up
style. His mining affairs went wrong. When
recalled to Melb., he had to sell out most of his
Melb. Mercantile Bank shares to pay passages.
He entered on the Speakership cumbered with a
load of debt which it was impossible to wipe off,
still less was it possible to make any provision for
the future. He has not lived with miserly
economy, but has been far short of the slightest
extravagance. Do the Victorians want a mean,
hunky Speaker ? They need not look far for one.”
The results of the Sydney aldennanic elec-
tions show that the people of the metrop. either
take no interest in municipal affairs, or else are
too thick-headed to perceive when a change of
men in the council is urgently needed. One of
the successful candidates, at a public meeting
which he addressed in his ward, refuted the charge
of incapacity hurled against him by his opponent,
in words somethinglike these : “ They say I’ve got
no ability. Good God ! I’ve got a shop. I’ve
worked up this business myself. I treat my work-
men well; I let them go at 6 o’clock,” &c. The
mover of a vote of confidence in this gentleman
said, among many other wise things, that Mr
Blank must have ability, because he was a
licensing magistrate !
John M’Kenzie, the evil genius of the
M.L. Government, has been breaking out again.
The Ministry recently appointed as public valuer
an insolvent whose certificate, had been refused
by Judge Kettle in the most uncomplimentary
terms. Naturally the Opposition attacked the
appointment, and did it when Big John was on
deck—with a blessed conviction that that roaring
Highlander would drive another nail in the
Ministerial coffin. And M’Kenzie promptly got
up on his two hairy legs and accused the Judge
who refused his friend’s certificate of being a
“ tool of the Bank of N.5.W.,” and otherwise
acted the giddy bear, and when he sat down the
Seddon Government was another long stride
nearer its downfall.
Nobody seems quite certain what Drake
meant by his suggestion that the Q. Parliament
should vote <£2so to Thomas MTlwraith, whom
Premier Nelson declared to be bed-ridden and
penniless. Possibly it was intended to express
Drake’s sardonic opinion that MTlwraith’salleged
poverty and wretchedness were not quite so
awful as alleged. A few years ago, when finan-
cial institutions were going down like houses of
cards, almost every boomer was announced more
or less definitely to be so poor and broken that it
was better not to harry him at law, but rather to
take a refined vengeance by leaving him to the
horrors of his position. And when the storm blew
over, how these pathetic wrecks rose again to
affluence through the unexpected death of a rich
relative in Yurrup !
J. L. Jacobsen, chess champion of Aus-
tralasia, was born in Hull, England, in 1862,
where, at the early age of 14, he was recognised as
a first-class amateur. He won the chess champion-
ship of Queensland and gold medal on March 1,
’B7. Mr. Jacobsen is a prominent chess-journal-
ist, having successfully conducted the chess
J. L. JACOBSEN, chess champion of A ustralasia.
columns of the Queenslander, the Illustrated
Sydney News, and other papers. He is at present
chess-editor for Sydney Daily Telegraph. Since
arriving in N.S.W. in Nov., ’B9, he has won
numerous championship tournaments, and his
recent record of seven games to one gained by
Mr. Wm. Crane, jun., in a match for the cham-
pionship of Australasia, proves anew his remark-
able chess talent.

The Maoriland Government is considering
the advisability of appointing salaried State soli-
citors throughout the country to advise poor
people gratis, adjust disputes, draw up wills, and
the like. As the State solicitor would have no-
thing to gain (while the private lawyer has every-
thing to gain) by promoting litigation, it is
reckoned that he would probably save the State
almost as much directly in the cost of running the
law-courts as his salary would amount to. And
the amount of the possible indirect saving would
be enormous.

Tub Red Bird Wilson chain, when in conjunction
with our cycloidal sprocket make a perfect running-
gear. The chain-wheel has raised a centre which ob-
tains a half-inch pull on an inch pitch chain.
They are made with the only practical hardened
rivet which is proof against wear; unlike the roller
pattern, they do not stretch. When you buy a cycle,
select one with a chain which has the greatest speed
and does not require to be replaced every few months.
Tub Red Bird Wilson chain with the half-inch pull
on an inch pitch sprocket.
The world’s mile record in competition is held by
Martin on this chain; time, 2min., unpaced. This
chain also won the great Austral Wheel Race in
record time.
SIIE DIDN'T RIDE A RED BIRD.
“ And when your wheel broke down seven miles
from home,” said the old man, “ you repaired it all
by yourself, did you ? ”
“ I did,” answered the typewriter proudly.
“ It seems mighty funny to me, then,” he continued,
“that when the ribbon on your machine needs shift-
ing, you have to call on that dude of a book-keeper
to fix it for you every time.”
All the good points of bicycles are not confined to
one wheel. There are wheels and wheels, so there are
distinguished features in each. The RED BIRD
combines more practical, progressive improvements
than any other cycle. Light, swift, and rigid, it is
pre-eminently the wheel for racers and road-riders.
Its past record is convincing proof of the strength
and easy running qualities.
The new model is improved beyond all others. If
you have not examined one, it will pay you to look-up
the nearest agents.
Don’t waste your time disputing figures; they sel-
dom lie, except in cyclometers and gas-meters.
Don’t think every sad-eyed man you meet doesn’t
ride a cycle. Most likely it is because he hasn’t a
Red Bird.
