The Boule d’Or—a sort of Brassard, giving the
three placed men £2, £l, and 10s. a week respectively—
fell to Walne, Walker, and A. H. Clinton. The race, a
scratch mile, brought out all the champions, and was
noticeable principally for the failure of Joe Megson in the
preliminary. The final included the two Clintons (A. H.
and J. E.), and as the crowd came round the final turn
200yds. from home J.E. collided with Payne, and, touch-
ing Green and Ainsworth, the whole four fell. Walne, at
the rear, rode wide, and walloped Walker and A. H.
Clinton in the last run.
The Victorian Mile was Kellow’s, that erratic
rider making a splendid dash in the last lap; while in his
Austral heat he faded away to nothing when most
required.
After two falls, bald-headed Britisher Green
managed to stand up in the Ten Mile scratch, and win it
with comparative ease, little Gargurevitch being a popu-
lar second, and Body third. Green's winnings during
the last month reach the nice little amount of £205.
* • • *
A fancier of A. J. Body for the Austral sent
along this belated conundrum :
Gin A. Body meet a body
In the Austral spin,
Gin a body spill A. Body,
Can A. Body win?
• * * *
The Austral Wheel Race of 1897 proved an
eloquent object-lesson on the inutility of flat grass tracks
for men accustomed to banked asphalt. Both in the
heats and the final the race was an utter fiasco. Even the
champions on the first day were unable to steer straight,
and Englishman Green, who had a mortgage on the big
prize, fell when the heat was all his own. He tumbled in
company, too, for scarcely a heat was run without half-a-
dozen or more sprawling beside their machines, while the
final last Saturday, composed of really the cream of Vio.
cyclists on the different marks, saw no less than six men
come down out of the 20 starters.
Taylor, who ran Walne to a few inches in the
preliminary, came down early and alone, but. another lap
saw all the chosen men of the race knocked out in a lump.
“ Newhaven ” Jackson touched another man’s wheel, arid
recovery being impossible on the slippery grass, over he
went, bringing down Farrell, the lightning lad on 180yds.,
who won his heat by a street. Bucknall and Jewell came
on top of them, and Walne, riding over a fallen machine,
was thrown out of his saddle and out of the race also.
For the Queenslander this was especially hard
luck. The arrangements for pacing between Herd, Buck-
nall, Herman, Farrell and Murray, all on 180yds., were so
complete that few thought that Body and Walne (on
20yds. with a 40yds. gap ahead) would ever catch the
field. But both these men rode so well that the field was
all one long string before the first mile was scored, and,
as far as Walne was concerned, the race was his, for there
was not a man riding who could live with the Banana-
lander’s flashy finishes. He made the fatal mistake, how-
ever, of following the field instead of working into second
or third place. The result was that another’s fall snatched
200 sovs. out of his hand.
The last lap saw Body go out to leave the field
as he has often done before, but though he gained a
break of three lengths towards the end the veteran
“ Jimmy ” Carpenter dashed across the gap and battled it
out with the Maorilander, crossing the line a short length
ahead. Carpenter started from 85, and though he has
been doing little of late, the veteran —he is 27 years old,
but veterans age early on the cycle track—has a long list
of track successes as well as his road performances.
The Austral final had a lamentable failure at
the very start. Lober, on 180, fell as he was being pushed
off, and “no race ” was called in error by one of the
judges. J. T. Scott, the limit man, heard and sat up, not
knowing the race was going on until the front batch
rushed past him. However, he lost nothing by the mis-
take and got third place, so his growl is not worth con-
sidering.
The same Scott rode well, qualifying for the
finals of the Austral, the M.B.C. Plate, and the Flying
Stakes, but going no further. The M.B.C. fell to March,
the pacer, and the Flying Stakes to W. Matthews, who
has been a favorite for the two last Australs.
The Five Miles scratch was Body’s, the Maori-
lander following up his sterling Austral performance by
walloping Jack Green and Ken Lewis out in the final run.
Barden showed himself on the ground on both
days of the Austral, but did nothing beyond disabling a
couple of machines, which is little to be wondered at,
considering the clumsy way in which he gets going.
Though big, Barden does not impress one as particularly
powerful, but he must be, for it is nothing but strength
that gets him along. The average sassiety female of 18st.
is a more scientific rider than the much-advertised Eng-
lishman.
Despite the threatened loss of interest in cycling,
the Austral attendance on Saturday was the largest on
record, the takings being £l24o— as airainst £lO3B last
year. Also the “ deadhead ” reserve, by reason of the
improvements, held over 1000 more than previously. The
attendance is estimated at 27,000.
The League of Vic. Wheelmen again published
its spine|essneBs by inflicting a month’s disqualification on
A. H. Clinton. Clinton started with his brother, J. E.,
in the final of the Scratch Mile, on Saturday week, and,
according to the statement of several witnesses, he called
“ Run him out,” loudly, as Jack Green was making his
sprint. J. E. Clinton, at the moment, collided with
Payne, and Green fell with the two of them. The English-
man got up furious, and protested loudly that J. E.
Clinton had upset him wilfully, owing to instructions
from his brother. The truth or otherwise of this allega-
tion is immaterial, but, after finding J. E. innocent, the
L.V.W. exhibited the blatant idiocy of declaring A. 11.
guilty, and awarding him a month’s disqualification. If
there was no satisfactory proof of Clinton’s guilt, he
should have been acquitted ; if there was, he should have
been, wiped out for life. The offence of which he was
accused was absolutely the most brutal and dangerous
any cyclist could be arraigned upon.
As compared with the Austral meeting of 95,
where Martin, Porta, and Harris were new luminaries and
the rather absurd Parsons “ pedestalled ” in all his glory,
this year’s gathering was a tame show. The great Barden
fizzled ignominiously ; Green, with the best of luck, will
never be a sensational item ; Bill Martin stood around in
his straw Ilalpine ’at trying to spot winners ; Porta, who
is but a ghost of the idol he used to be, was out of form ;
ditto Megson, Payne and Kellow ; and VVajne is not a
sufficiently well-established Australian champion to excite
much national sentiment.
• * • •
Eleven of the more notable riders came to grief
in one or other of the Austral heats, including the final—
Green, Tebbutt, Forbes, L. Barker, Ainsworth, George
Carpenter, J. A. M‘lnnes, “Newhaven” Jackson, Matter-
son, and Walne. Yet the public will roll up in their tens
of thousands to witness these rough-and-tumble contests
on the M.C.C. grass-track so long as the game retains its
popularity. Sporting interest has evaporated in a great
measure from cycle racing, but the catastrophe element
appears to have a charm for the average sightseer, and
the show this year flourished more than ever. Under the
circs, the possible refusal of L.V.W. patronage to any
further racing on this Tom Tiddler's ground raises a ques-
tion as to whether the M.B.C. isn't in a position to raise
an insurrection against the League’s authority. After
all, the ruling power in bike racing is the power that
draws the biggest gates. Where there is likely to be a
champion attendance, the attendance of “ all the cham-
pions” can be commanded.
* * * ■
Two causes combined to make Carpenter’s time
for the grass-race Usee, slower than Ivellow’s last year.
Limit man Scott didn’t begin to travel till he was caught,
and thereafter the dread of tumbles at “Sleight’s bend”
and other slippery points gave a certain amount of pause
to the bolder and more sprinting spirits.
* • * •
The half-mile “ Flying ” Stakes, cut up into
eight little heats with only £l2 as ari inducement to fly,
was about the slowest entertainment ever placed on an
Austral programme. A Megsonian spurt enlivened the
N.S.W. man’s heat, but the final was an uninteresting
race between three long-starters. Even the hoisting of
the wrong number on the board failed to thrill the audi-
ence. Talkie* of the board —that cheap arid complicated
arrangement needs reforming altogether, also the youths
in charge of it ought to be born again.
* * * •
If t.ho Austral stake could have been allotted by
a plebiscite vote of the multitude Berry would have got
it, as a sort of token of good feeling for the ex-Speaker.
The wish being father to the t hought, Berry was a popu-
lar “lip” ali around the oval, and when his grey
guernsey was seen to have lived into the final lap his
name sounded loudest of any. The Jewish-lookingyoung
fellow achieved just the empty honor of fourth place.
Little Gargurevieh had a try at the grass records
during the week, and managed to cut down all world’s
times between one and 10 miles ; but (so careless are
many sportsmen in things like this) he had the disqualified
A. A. Middleton on one of his pacing-machines, and it is
questionable whether the records will hold.
* * * •
One busy Sydnoy man posted up this notice
when the present cricket season commenced :—“ Visitors
please note that no interest in cricket or cricketers is
taken in this office. I neither know nor wish to know
anything about the game.”
* * * *
When the last English Eleven was visiting Aus-
tralia a patient suffering with cancer of the tongue was
admitted to a private hospital in Sydney. The operation
performed was the entire removal of the unhealthy mem-
ber, a kind of surgical lapsus lingua!. Two days after, the
man, well enough to sit up but. now entirely speechless,
motioned to the nurse for pen and paper. Being supplied,
his first request was to know the result of the last test-
match, which had been finished during his illness. He
afterwards read the account of the game with absorbed
interest and, being English, satisfaction.
* * * •
The first test-match with the Englishmen was
preceded by a squabble in the daily papers about isjdney
Cricket Association’s action in changing the date. Last
week, the rain was continuous ; the ground was mush ;
and the authorities showed their sense by postponing the
match till Monday. Stoddart and co. (who are beginning
to presume a little on Australian hospitality) protested,
and sent indignant cables to England, and the English
papers promptly protested also—knowing nothing at all
about the matter. It was said that, according to
cricket rules, the umpires are sole judges as to
whether the ground is fit for play. So they are,
when their duty begins. Similarly, a‘captain commands
his vessel as soon as the voyage begins, but the voyage
doesn’t begin till the owners give the word—and if they
choose the owners may postpone sailingdates for a month.
And the umpires are precisely in the captain’s position.
Stoddart & Co. apparently thought they would have
prospered in a mud-fight—the inference being that they
wanted to win a lot more than they wanted to win on
their merits.
Results showed that the Englishmen had pro-
bably cried before they were hurt. The match com-
menced on Monday on a perfect wicket, and at close of
the day’s play the visitors had scored 337 runs with the
loss of five batsmen. Stoddart, in consequence of the
death of a relative, did not play ; and Ranjitsinhji was
only included at the last moment, being barely convales-
cent after a fortnight’s illness. MacLaren cap-
tained the team, and scored 109 to his own
bat the first half of his innings being per-
fectly played, which makes Mac.’s third century on
the Sydney ground this trip, and he put up another in
Brisbane. Hayward made a smart 72 before he was
picked up by Trott at point. The Australian bowling on
the first day was shared by M‘Kibbin, Jones, C. M'Leod,
Trumble, and Trott; but there was no devil in the ball,
and runs came fast or slowly, but always came. The
fielding was spotty—Jones being a good spot and
M'Kibbin a bad one. And it was noticeable once
more that the Englishmen played to win—played as
if they were playing for their lives —as if this
was the only match in Eternity—and never threw a chance
away. The Australians, or some of them, played as if
there were lots of other good matches to come ; and,
although it would be pleasant to win this one, what was
one match, after all ?
Clem Hill had a bad buster on the asphalt
cycling-track. The trustees, grabbing Mammon with
both hands, have turned their first-rate cricket-ground
into third-rate or worse. A fieldsman at full-speed may
break nothing when he slides over the asphalt, or he may
break his neck. So the sensible man goes quarter-speedi
gingerly, and the game becomes third-rale too.
The demnition total of the Englishmen’s first
innings on Tuesday, when this paper went to press, was
551—0 f which sick man Ranji made 176 ! And this with-
out their captain. Verily, he that winneth the toss on a
good wicket is greater than he that taketh a city.
• * * »
Dear B., —Regarding Ranji. and his plati-
tudes. His eye and his ball-bearing wrists make
him a cricketer. Now, the Australian native black
has as good an eye as any. Witness his tracking feats.
Also witness his boomerang throwing as proof of the
suppleness of his wrist and the power of his arm. Catch
a blackfellow young, give him an English (cricket)
master, send him to a university to study more cricket,
and if he is an ordinary nigger I don’t see why he
shouldn’t beat Ranji. at his own game.—P.
• * * *
Ranji’s cheeky references to the England v.
S.A. match, in Review of Reviews, are very amusing.
“ There can be no doubt the S.A. score was mainly the
result of one man’s batting, but. for whom it would hardly
have reached 200.” Yes! And Ranji made nearly 200 in
the same match, giving, in all, six chances (Clem Hill
gave none at all), the first one before he scored ! What
would the English score have been without India’s con-
tribution ? Ranji contemptuously says the S.A. bowling
lacked class. The w r riter saw every ball bowled, and avers
that the trundling of Jones, who got seven wickets, and
had chance after chance missed, was superior to anything
on the English side.

Ranji. ’s nasty references to Australian ericket-
dressing-room accommodation recalls that one-half the
Hinglish dressing-rooms, to the disgust of visiting Aus-
tralians, don’t even boast a shower-bath.
* * • *
“ Bill ” Holden, the Sydney boxer, travelled to
Melbourne recently, looking for fights, and was taken on
by M’Gowan, one of the cleverest of the Melb. light-
weights. They met in the Victoria Hall, for £25 a side
and all the available glory. Holden fights from the jump,
with all that’s in him, but on this occasion he was “ for-
ninst”alad with icy head and the temper of a well-fed
saint. M’Govvan is neat and nice, but as solid as a pyra-
mid and as hard as a brick chimney. He fights with the
calm and benevolent appearance assumed by a young
curate at prayer-meeting, and plugs the other fellow on
the chin or the eye as if he loved him like a brother.
Holden 8 vicious right got home several times on M'Gowan
in the place where his corporation will he when lie retires
and becomes an alderman, and this stroke would possibly
have told on the Melb. man in an hour or so had he not
knocked all the vim and stiffening out of the visitor
before the end of the third round. Holden stood up for
two more, and M‘Gowan hammered him with judicial
severity until he was an unpleasant red object. Then he
retired, and the Melb. man took the cash.
* * » •
The Vic. sculling championship on Saturday
was a sorry farce. G. Brown, of the Essendon club—a
man not up to the form of the average maiden—was the
sole representative of Victoria. C. Chapman, of M.L.,
was the only other starter, and before half the distance
was rowed he had almost lost sight of Brown. The
Champion Fours, on the other hand, provided a tip-top
contest between the Mercantile and the Banks—the
former winning by a couple of lengths after a hard-rowed
race.
• * • •
“ Ernie Cavill reduced 151 b. to swim the Ameri-
can champion, M'Cusker, a quarter-mile in the West-
minster baths,’’ writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, “and about fifty persons all-told witnessed the
contest. The water in this gorgeous municipal nata-
torium—no other word is grandiloquent enough—was
warmed by three steandng hot showers o’erhead, and to
such a degree as to interfere with the time record and to
color Cavill with a boiled-lobster hue. The odds were 3
to 1 on the Australian without takers, a level £lOO taken
the same afternoon settling all business. The Yank did
not appear to have a show after 88yds. had been covered.
He swam more weakly and less straight than his oppon-
ent., who apparently had his measure soon after the plunge.
M'Cusker is two inches shorter and an older man than
Cavill, and says he will revenge himself in the 300yds. go.
One individual spectator who paid his half-dollar only to
find most of his companions were deadheads, indignantly
demanded his coin back, and got it, and then looked on in
comfort."

What the Sydney bicycle boom last year was
worth to a good business man and a good bicycle can be
estimated from the “ Acme” business done by Mr. J. M.
Maud—£4ooo for new bicycles alone. Add to this repairs
(a speciality with this firm), sundries, &c., Ac., and it can
be understood why the “ Acme ” and J. M. Maud still
figure prominently.
Thomas M‘llwraith has finally retired from
his nominal membership of the Queensland Minis-
try. As he is now t»2 years of age, very ill, and
very much discredited, this is (barring miracles)
his final disappearance from public life. He
entered Queensland politics in ’69, so he has had a
28 years’ run after the loaves and the fishes. And
now he apparently hasn’t got the money, and the
Q.N. Bank certainly hasn’t got it, and no one
knows where it is.
The death of J. A. MacPherson, M.L.A.
leader of S.A. Labor-party, is a serious loss to
the politics of his province. MacPherson was
straight and honest, an intelligent politician, and
one of the best samples of the Labor member in
Australia. N.S.W. has a good Labor-party and
no leader worth mentioning. Victoria has an
able Labor leader in Trenwith, but not much in
the shape of a* distinctive party behind him.
has a strong Labor-party, and until Mac-
Pherson died it had a good leader also.
On Nov. 26 the shade register at Broken
Hill at Bin the afternoon was 106. A dust-cloud
presently rolled up from the Never-Never so
dense that work had to be suspended in the after-
noon newspaper-office till candles were got (the
town’s electric-light is not available till 5) ; and
then within eight hours the glass had fallen to 54.
The Bulletin supposes this was the storm in
which (as another correspondent reports), in order
to let the Broken Hill express out, a special train
had to take down a party of navvies to clear the
line. From one cutting, through solid rock, 70
trucks of sand were taken—say 350 tons.
The recent Victorian hurricane, which
mopped up the town of Nhill, was even more
thorough-going than the one which blew away
Nevertire some 12 months ago. Nhill lies out on
the sandy western plains, and presents a beautiful
mark for a tornado, and, in 10 minutes, half the
town was blown into the mallee. One woman,
with a baby in her arms, was carried 100 yards
and blown over two fences ; and a soulful tale is
told of two girls who were caught by the storm
out on the open plain. They lay down and hung
on to a root apiece, with both hands and their
teeth. When the trouble was over, one of them
was still there, and the other was 400 yards away ;
the one who iDlew away lost a good part of her
clothing, and the one who hung on is alleged to
have lost all hers, except her corset, and the sleeves
of her dress, and her boots. Luckily it was nearly
dusk by that time, and, being concealed by the
tail-end of the duststorm, the pair fled across the
plain in pursuit of their lost skirts and things, and
ultimately overtook enough of their wardrobe for
all practical purposes. Nhill, at latest advices,
looked as if it had been sacked by Joshua and
those enthusiastic wreckers, his army of bygone
Israelites. Also, the Nhill hurricane did something
towardsaunion of the local churches. Fourwooden
tabernacles mingled their ruins at various points
of the township, a special feature of the proceed-
ings being the aerial flight of the Baptist pulpit
to the place where the R.C. building had been
established before it blew into violent collision
with the Presbyterian kirk. The hurricane, for
some reason or other, was very rough on places of
worship. Perhaps it regarded them as windy
rivals.

No agent who speculates on his own
account is looked upon as a thoroughly reliable
representative. People in the country hundreds
of miles away from the scene of action are really
at the mercy of their agents, and it must be a
sweet satisfaction for them to entrust their busi-
ness to such as Pitt, Son and Badgery Ltd., who,
during a long financial life, are able to pride them-
selves on their business purity. Verb sap.*
A French medical man has just discovered
some very curious effects produced on all the
members of a family, owing to the lower part of
the house where they dwelt being used as a coffee-
warehouse. The volatile essence of the coffee had
penetrated every portion of the living apartments,
and the nervous systems of the inmates had
becotne entirely demoralised. Hypochondria,
hysteria, and angry demonstrations were the
order of the day, but these symptoms disappeared
on the family taking up new apartments. This
points to a moral, if coffee has such effects when
«e live in its vicinity, it cannot but be injurious
when taken as a drink.' On the other hand
cocoa (that is not simply ‘ pure,’ but also properly
prepared cocoa, not mixtures, mind!) while afford-
ing the necessary amount of stimulation, does not
injure the nerves. The cocoa known as ‘Van
Ilonten’s,” is perfectly pure, and owing to the
special (the original) method employed in its
pn paration, it is rendered soluble, while the
flavor and aroma are developed to a maximum.
1 1 is easy to make, delicious to taste, and one of
the cheapest drinks in existanee.*

The Tichborne Case .
Is Wm. Cresswell Really the Missing
Sir Roger '/
Contemporary journals speak of Cresswell, the
lunatic, as “ the new claimant ’’—but claimant to
what? They also refer to him as “the probable
heir ’’—but, in the language of Richard 111., “ is
the throne unfilled?” They forget that even
'though Sir Roger Tichborne a hundred times
over, he cannot inherit the estates of that name
in England. First, he is a lunatic; second, the
Tichborne estates were finally settled on the jamily
now in possession by Act of Parliament. It may
be argued that the law which gave can takeaway,
but this is unlikely. The long-lost Sir Roger may
be dead on earth or alive in the Parramatta
Asylum; he is certainly dead in British law.
What is infinitely surer of a successful issue
than the efforts of the gentlemen now interesting
themselves in Cresswell’s behalf is a course of
Minerva Tonic, the greatest restorative known to
modern science, and which is invaluable in all
cases, no matter how pronounced, arising out of
general debility. A few of the “ signs ” of this
are : Nervousness, palpitation, irritability, dizzi-
ness after exertion shortness of breath, alternate
fits of depression or elation, loss of energy, uncer-
tain memory, shooting pains in the limbs,
numbness, neuralgia, insomnia, frequent head-
aches, restlessness, feeling of faintness, &e., &e.
Minerva Tonic is a phosphorised iron syrup
combined with certain well-i< nown strength-pro-
ducers of very great value, and shows its efficacy
by enriching the blood, promoting the formation
of bone and muscle, increasing the secretions,
stimulating the brain and nerves, improving the
appetite, and giving a healthy tone generally.
Very Rev. Dr. O’Haran, private secretary to
His Eminence the Cardinal Archbishop of Syd-
ney, says that Minerva Tonic is “ appetising and
energising”; Mr. Hart, F.H.M.S., Melbourne,
that it “has the desired effect”; Mr. Cole, late
head-master Petersham C. of E. school, that
“four bottles of it made him feel ten years
younger.” Compare such names with those ob-
tained in support of some of the nostrums now on
the market villainous compounds which are testi-
monialised, by unknown or irresponsible persons,
for “ a consideration,’’ and whose affidavits are not
infrequently obtained, lest, later, they should “ go
back ” on those who employed them.
Minerva Digestive is a purely vegetable prepa-
ration, consisting of a number of remedies which
have a, specific action upon the liver, stomach and
digestive organs. Taken in the prescribed doses
immediately after meals, it will assuredly stimu-
late the liver, cure constipation, tone up the
bowels so that their action becomes easy and
regular, and increase the proper flow of bile, and
purify the blood.
Minerva Liver Pills (pink) are guaranteed free
from calomel or other mineral ingredients. Small,
sugar-coated, and therefore easy to take, they act
as a gentle aperient, steadily, surely, and without
griping.
Minerva Preparations are on sale by all
chemists.
Special Notice. —Minerva having been openly
praised in letter and print by some of the leading
citizens of this country and the best papers pub-
lished in Sydney respectively, unscrupulous per-

nostrums, have pretended doubt as to the genuine-
ness of Minerva testimonials. The Minerva Co.,
therefore, offers .£lOO to any person proving that

all persons curious enough to call at the Sydney

originals of the different complimentary letters

When the Loan Bill came before the
N.S.W. Legislative Council last week every pri-
vate member who spoke (nine in all) denounced it
in the strongest terms. And then the Bill passed
its second reading and went through committee
without even a division. What is the use of tho
Leg. Council, anyhow?
Hindoo plague story about a corpse which
didn’t lead trumps, given on the authority of the
British Medical Journal
A party of searchers came upon a group of natives in a
house intent upon a game of cards. Something in the
stolid appearai.ce of one of the players attracted atten-
tion, and one of the search-party placed his hand on the
man’s shoulder. The figure swayed, and fell over prone
on the floor. On examination it was found the man had
been dead some time, but, to avoid the house being
branded plague-stricken, the friends of the dead man had
propped him up and stuck some cards in his hand on the
news that the search-party was approaching.
The North German Lloyd S.S. Co. makes
a sensation in Australasian shipping circles every
time the magnificent Kaiser Wilhelm Der Grosse
comes into Australian waters. This great steam-
ship, the largest afloat, demonstrates the pro-
gressiveness of German ship-building. She has
steamed 5(14 nautical miles in one day—the world’s
record. She has ample accommodation for 750
passengers—4oo first and 350 second. Her crew
numbers 450 all told ; the engines total 08. The
pumps for fire can pump 3000 tons of water per
hour. The main boiler installation is divided into
four groups, each divided from the other by bulk-
heads. The ship is remarkably steady at sea, and
is handsomely furnished throughout.

THE AGED.
A Mias Bedgood, who has recently been
be-Bibling the Maoris, was horrified to ;be told by
some of them —
We have heard that there is a God, but we are not sure
that there is really one, and we are looking 1 for Him, but
cannot find Him.
W hich is exactly the last word of the most elabor-
ate intellectual research of the day. The meet-
ing of the extremes again !
Clergymen should, according to Mr. H. S.
Martin, 8.A., LL.B., be superior sort of people.
Speaking at the Baptist Union yesterday, he
greatly disparaged the idea of clergymen being on
too familiar terms with the hewers of wood and
drawers of water whom they condescend to visit
occasionally. “A clergyman,” he remarked,
“should be looked up to,” and as a means to this
“consummation devoutly to be wished,” Mr.
Martin advocated a good collegiate training for
all ministers and missionaries. “I like,” he said
in conclusion, “ to see a family which would make
a knife and fork go round when they are by them-
selves put on a full complement of cutlery, with
an extra knife for the butter, when the minister
comes along.”— Melb. Argus.
A male correspondent of Modern Society
(London) deplores in print the “ decay of
modesty.” The latest culprits are the bonnie
lasses o’ Scotland as represented by some
half-dozen Paisley girls, who (so writes their
accuser) not only bathed from the open beach
“ mid nodings on ” but coolly asked the complain-
ant aforesaid, as he strolled blushingly by, to
mind their clothes neatly piled up on the beach.
Later on, when the mermaids came out of the
water and approached their garments, they
thanked the custodian, after a preliminary titter
or two, for his watchfulness, explaining that the
day before some joker had hidden their “olaes”
amang the rocks, giving the owners siccan awfu’
fash. After all, perhaps, the poor things were only
commendably economical. Siccan sinfu’ waste, ye
ken, to drabble good raiment in the water. [Also,
there is no more beach at Paisley than there is at
Broken Hill.—Ed. B.]
Gavan Duffy, in his new Contemporary
article on “An Australian in England 30 years
ago,” relates an anecdote of Disraeli which throws
a sidelight on Vic. history. Duffy called on
Dizzy, who was then Premier (it was in ’6B). The
trouble about Gov. Darling had lately occurred.
Duffy says he told Disraeli that the row was
“attributable to the blunder of sending a man
to work responsible Government who had never
seen a Parliament.” “Well,” replied Disraeli,
“that grievance disappears, for we have now
sent you a man who was born in Parliament
House, and bred up in the shelter of the Speaker’s
robe.” As a matter of fact, Darling had had as
much experience of Parliamentary affairs as
Manners-Sutton, and that was none at all. And
if the sending out of Darling was a blunder for
any such reason, it was equally a blunder to send
out Governors Hopetoun and Lamington. The
Governor who has had most Parliamentary experi-
ence is Brassey, who is a notorious and tiresome
failure in the part.

Please mention this paper when writing.
Melb. : “ Lady Munro is extant here
again. Really a nice, quiet, modest woman to
talk to, as she meanders along a retired street
knitting. A little genuine feminine sympathy
would make her a charming Methodist.
“ A Brother-Shark The patriotic Mr.
C. G. Heydon, Q.C., in replying to the eulogiumß
on his self-denying labors in codifying the hf.b. W.
statutes, seems not to have mentioned that tne
first Commission (which cost the country abou
£7000) left a legacy of several bills already
drafted which he has utilised. Mr. Heydon
also appears to have omitted to acknowledge
the services of the two draftsmen the
secretary who prepared the work for him, and
for whose services the bleeding country has to
pay. Mr. Heydon has certainly undertaken a
large amount of labor for no direct reward, but so
far as he is concerned, it would appear that the
work was being done by him alone and without
cost to the country.
Gipsy Poll was a notability of old Hobart
—a big, swarthy, beetle-browed dame, with the
voice of a boatswain, the tongue of a bullock-
driver, and the heart of a true woman. She
“ came out ” in a ship called the Twelve Apostles,
and was proud of it. She was then very good-
looking; but she could take care of herself, and
worked out her sentence an immaculate. Then
she .went in for making money, and made it.
Cared for nobody. D d everybody who
offended her, from Governor to pot-boy. You
ought to have been a man, Polly,” said Sir John
Franklin. “ A man ! ” she echoed. “ What pull
has the man in the picnic? She has the babby, he
pays the doctor, and she knows best whether he
ought to. A man ! Not me ! ”
Duffy, in a recent Contemporary, has Ho.
2 of his reminiscences of the Commons. He
found Lowe always weighty there, but not of
much personal influence outside. Lowe had ms
speeches by heart. Yet they captivated. He
could not see his hearers, which was a drawbaok.
Clever Mrs. Lowe did all she could, so she told
Duffy, to persuade Lowe to return to Australia.
Lowe said : “ Oh, how I used to enjoy my ride
into Sydney every morning, through the intoxicat-
ing air ! Nothing like it here.” Mr. and Mrs.
Lowe, by showing pictures of their Sydney home,
and what not, had much influence in persuading
Duffv to emigrate. Duffy cannot say enough m
praise of Disraeli’s kindness.
A Melb. barrister in criminal practice re-
lates how he was once asked by two hard-jawed
persons to defend a friend who had been arrested
for burglary. Jotting down the points of the de-
fence, the man of briefs looked up and said that it
would cost ten guineas. The deputation looked
embarrassed. “ Well, yer see, sir, we didn’t
know as how ” “ Haven’t got the coin, eh ?
interrupted the prospective defender. “No, sir ;
ter tell ye the truth, we ain’t. But,” with a
sudden lighting up of the eye, “ we’ll pet it fur
yer ! ” Shortly after midnight the barrister was
awakened by a knock at the door ; and, on attend-
ing in his pyjamas, a bull-necked gentleman
shoved 10 sovereigns and a handful of silver into
his hand, with the observation: “ Ef it’s all the
same to you, sir, I’ll ’ang behind yer ’edge till the
cop goes by.”

MEDITERRANEE

LAGER B€€R.
Mr. John Young.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have made up my mind, owing to unkind re-
marks by fair-weather friends of ours—friends who
used to smoke papa’s cigars and go to his theatre-
parties —to write the true history of my sister
Bertha’s marriage.
Before the stoppage of the banks, papa was
quite well-off ; he had building-society shares, and
bank shares, and a fine station on the Murray.
Ah ' those good old days of Goternment-house
balls, and dances on the warships, and the subtle
pleasure of being introduced to the very Nicest
people as “the Misses Conobie, of Conobie s run ;
and the delight, the pure delight of hearing the
introducer whisper to his friend — heiresses to
the finest station in the southern district, dear
boy, and heaps of oash in the Longwool
and Tallowfat Banking Company ! I want
you to understand that we two girls, Ber-
tha and I, were right up on the very
front seat of the band-waggon of the social circus.
I don’t mean to pretend that poor papa is quite a
gentleman, although he was a colonel in the volun-
teers, and used to clank round in his warlike iron-
mongery. Any big man who likes to put soap
on the points of his moustaches can look convinc-
ing in a gaudy uniform. He was brought up in
the haberdashery, you know, and I have always
had an intuition that well-bred people who kow-
towed to him in the days of our prosperity could
detect in him little vulgarities reminiscent of the
commercial-traveller, and that they recognised in
his pseudo-stateliness but the magniloquence of the
shopwalker.
Now, mother is a lady. Her grandfather was
an Irish viscount, although she herself was only
a poor governess at Albury when papa married
her. Ido not mention this out of any feeling of
pride, but to show that we girls can claim to have
some good blood in our veins, which perhaps still
further increases the remarkableness of Berthas
marriage. .
After the bank-smash and poor papas insol-
vency, he got a big brass-plate made, and called
himself a commission-agent. He told us hope-
fully that with his social and military connection
he could easily make a couple of thousand a year.
“ Mary,” he would say to mamma, “ we will
have our carriage again in a brace of shakes, but
meanwhile we must go into a small house at Pad-
dington. Just you wait and see how my friends
will rally round us! Very few months will see
us back at Darling Point again.”
And then mamma would shrug her shoulders
doubtfully, and I am sorry to say that her want
of confidence in papa’s rose-colored prophecies
was justified. Oh, the misery we have endured !
For he could not earn more than a beggarly pit-
tance, and the barest necessaries could scarcely be
obtained for our poor home. Our dresses grew
old-fashioned and dowdy, and the people we
hardly recognised in the old times—the Mosely-
Melehisedecs and the Cholmondeley - Smiths—
started to cut us; we “got off ” the books at Govern-
ment House, and even the young fellows in the
“Lands” bowed to us coldly. Papa’s friends
did not rally round him, and poverty and neglect
became our daily portion. Sometimes we girls
earned a few shillings addressing envelopes, but,
fenerally speaking, our affairs kept on going from
ad to worse.
One morning Bertha, who was reading the
Sydney Mobning Herald— it’s only a penny now,
you know-exclaimed “ Wilhelmma ” (that’s me),
what do you think of this ? ” and then she read
the following advertisement: —
AYATRIMONIAL. The Advertiser, a gentleman
_LYL owning a large and lucrative business in a coun-
try town, desires to correspond with a well-educated
young English lady, O. of E., with a view to the above.
References exchanged. Please forward photograph
in strictest confidence to Mr. John Young, Boggin-
gully, N.S.W.
“ Just fancy girls replying to an advertisement
like that! ” said I, who had strong caste notions ol
the methods whereby, and the environments in
which, ladies should be wooed and won. Ah, yes !
The soft echoes of one of Strauss’s waltzes stealing
into a conservatory, dimly lighted with fairy-lamps,
flowers and lycopods and tree-ferns, glimpses of a
crescent moon shining through the waving ten-
drils of wistaria, the air laden with stepnanotis
and jonquil, and the kneeling swain in full war-
paint hanging rapturously on to one s twelve-
button su&de glove and entreating permission to
provide one with board, lodging, and pin-money
for ever and a day.
I conveyed these vie ws to Bertha,but Berthabeing
so “ practical,” replied : —“ Oh, stuff ! Those are
dreams of the past. I believe the gentleman who
advertises here really wants a wife, but is too
much engrossed in his business in an out-of-the-
way country district to go looking for one. And
the name, John Youngs, has a manly, honest ring
about it—the sort of name that you could fancy
written in a firm, round hand, without any
flourishes, at the bottom of a good, big cheque.
“Rubbish!” I interjected, hotly; “more likely
it is some clod-hopping boundary-rider’s joke. I
can fancy him laughing as he scans his easily-
acquired album full of would-be missuses.
But Bertha refused to budge from her impres-
sion. She urged that the Fairfaxes, of the Hekald,
were very nice people and would not permit their
journal to be made the medium of any low practi-
cal joke. Now if it had appeared in some papers
there might have been a little in my contention ;
but in the Hekald, never ! The more I bantered
her the more stubborn she became, and next
morning I was not much surprised when she told
me half-jocularly that she had replied to Mr. John
Young’s advertisement.
“ And sent your photograph ? ’ I asked m
dismay. .
Bertha nodded with an affectation of brazenness.
“ And how did you get the stamp ? ” I queried,
knowing that there had not been a penny in the
house for the last two days.
“ I sold half-a-dozen bottles to a ‘bottle-oh,
she answered, quailing a little under my indignant
gaze.
Bertha’s conduct, including that awful recourse
to the bottle-dealer, placed her beyond the pale of
criticism, and, besides, I was too much mortified
to pursue the matter further.
* * * *
Some weeks passed, and my sister’s indiscre-
tion had almost been driven from my mind by the
chronic unpleasantnesses of our every-day life —
the worry of duns, the heart-breaking, hopeless
trudge round governesses’ institutes and registry-
offices in search of employment —when one morn-
ing Bertha came bounding up the stairs, blushing
to the ears, with a large envelope addressed to her-
self in a neat, small hand-writing. Nervously she
opened it and exclaimed hysterically : 4 Yes,
Wil., it’s from Mr. Young,” and then she read
this epistle :
My dear Miss,— „ . ,
From a large number of replies to my aavt.,
after long consideration, and after consulting my friend,
the Rev. Josiah Jackson, who has been twice married, I
have selected yours as the most likely to be from a, lady
who will meet my wishes. Mr. Jackson says that your
face shows vou to be good-tempered and refined, and that
your letter'indicates culture, and your admission of your
father’s poverty is evidence that you have no false pride.
I think he is right. I regret that there is no photo-
grapher here, so I cannot send you my photograph. lam
35 years old, healthy, belong to the Church of England,
and have no relatives in this country. I enclose letters
about myself, and if, after perusing them, you are willing
to become my wife, please name the time when you can
come up here, and we will be married from the parsonage.
Yours very faithfully, John Youno.
The enclosures were letters from Mr. Jackson
certifying that his dear friend Mr. Young was a
member of his church, most prosperous in busi-
ness, and “ prominent in all good works in this
district,” while the other was signed by the
manager of the Bank of New South Wales, Bog-
gingully, and was addressed to Mr. Young. It
ran as follows : “In reply to your inquiry, I beg
to inform you that the amount to your current
account on this date is .£1625, while you have also
.£14,000 at fixed deposit, and securities valued by
the bank at £5OOO.
“Well?” I asked, feeling that, despite every-
thing, Bertha had scored.
“ I am going to marry him,” said Bertha, with
decision.
“ Perhaps he drops his h’s, or, maybe, he has
got a hare-lip, or a squint ? ” said I, a little spite-
fully. .
“My mind is quite made up. I will write this
very night,” said Bertha.
Of course, mamma had to be told, and, as I an-
ticipated, she nearly took a fit. “Oh, Bertha,”
she moaned, “ that a daughter of mine should do
such a thing ! It is terrible, terrible ! ”
Bertha was much distressed at the shock she
had given to mamma’s susceptibilities, but she
gently pointed out that desperate diseases needed
desperate remedies, and that a monotonous, plea-
sureless, hopeless existence in a mean street cer-
tainly came within the category of desperate
diseases. By marrying Mr. Young, she urged,
she would, no doubt, in some measure be able to
restore the family fortunes. Mr. Young was,
beyond question, a substantial man, a good citizen,
and possessed of religious principles.
Poor papa threw all the weight of his influence
on Bertha’s side and pooh-poohed mamma’s
scruples. “ Besides, Mary,” he said, “ a man with
a store like that must have a turnover of £30,000
or £40,000 a year, and he should need a buyer.
Now can you tell me, Mary, who could buy better
for him than his father-in-law, Colonel Conobie,
at your service ; and wouldn’t it just give tone to
his business to have a real live Colonel to do his
buying! And a partnership would no doubt
follow, with a city branch. Young, Conobie and
Co—-or, perhaps, as lam the eider, Conobie,
Young and Co.” And papa fairly glowed as he
built these airy castles.
The difficulty of providing funds to obtain for
Bertha the very simplest trousseau, to say nothing
of the money for the expensive railway - fare,
seemed insurmountable, but the very next day
papa announced that he had borrowed £lO on the,
furniture, “ which, Bertha,” he added, “you will
be able to get out of John before the honeymoon
is over.”
So it was arranged that Bertha should take he*
matrimonial leap in the dark. There was not
enough money to admit of any of us accompany-
ing her. The night she left Redfern station poor
mamma cried for hours, and I had a most uncom-
fortable premonition of disaster.
m # * *
Two days later a telegram came from Bertha
“ Arrived safely this morning. Was married at
noon. Writing. Love.— Bertha Young.”
Mamma and I breathed more freely, but it was
a fortnight before we heard any further from our
darling
“ Dear Mother and Wi11.,” she wrote, “ I could
not find it in my heart to write before. I was so
wretched.”
“Humph!” interjected papa. “Meregirly-
girly nonsense. They soon get over that. Haw,
haw. Don’t they, Mary ? ”
Mamma looked at him disapprovingly.
I proceeded with the reading of the letter:
“ And I am glad that I did not write, because 1
feel in a little better spirits now. I reached here
with half-a-crown in my pocket, and when I first
saw John —I am managing to call him John now
—I nearly fainted. My first impulse was to ask
the station-master to give me a passage back, but
then I remembered all our wretchedness —the
mortgage on the furniture, and the utter hopeless-
ness of everything in Sydney, and I determined
to go through to the bitter end. All the town was
decorated with flags in my honor. After I had
dressed we went straight to the church and were
married. I felt quite dazed and went through it as
if everything was a long way off. The house is
lovely, and John is—oh ! —so kind. He speaks
English quite well
“ Wilhelmina ! ” said mamma, as if she had not
heard me aright.
“ Hail smiling morn ! why shouldn’t he ? ”
blurted papa.
The letter fell from my fingers as though it had
burnt me. For the last words in it were—“ I
didn’t mean to tell you, but I can’t help it. John
says I must. My husband is a Chinese.”
Mamma and papa and I just sat for an hour
without saying a word.
J. A. PHILP.

In this trying climate of ours the woman who is
exempt from troubles peculiar to her sex is a great
exception. Mrs. Messer, of “Glenbrook,” No. 6
Union-street, Erskineville, Sydney, is no exception
to the rule. To a reporter, a few days ago, who
interviewed her, she said : “ For several years I
have had terrible bursting headaches, throbbing in
the temples, indigestion, dizziness, insomnia, and
various other ailments, the results of female ail-
ments. I became so ill that I went for a course of
outdoor treatment at the Prince Alfred Hospital.
Here I was treated for acute indigestion and severe
headaches, but never a bit of good did the medi-
cines do me, although I took them religiously.
Then I consulted my doctor —a well-known physi-
cian in Erskineville—and so seriously did he regard
my case that, at the beginning of this winter, just
passed, he said to me : ‘ Mrs. Messer, you’re suffer-
ing from a chronic ailment. It’s no good for you
to take medicine. A change of air is the only
thing that’ll benefit you, and, if you don’t go
away, you’ll never get through the winter.’ But I
have lived through it! ” the lady remarked, with
a smile, “ and I hope to live through many more
winters yet,” she continued. “How did you
cheat the medical faculty ? ” we inquired. “ Well,
one day,” was the “I read an adver-
Pills for cases somewhat similar to mine. I con-
cluded to try them, and after a few doses I was
surprised to find my appetite improving. This
went on, my spirits rose, my strength increased,
and all the pains from indigestion and influenza
slowly left me. Sleep came to my aid, brightness
to my eyes, and agility to my step. Not even the
headaches remained, and with every trace of in-
fluenza and indigestion, and their attendant
maladies, gone from my system, I felt a new
woman with a new body. Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Pills worked this marvellous change, and
worked it entirely, for I used no other remedy.
My husband and family and my friends were not
less astonished than myself at this most unprece-
dented recovery ; and their delight and mine
knew no bounds.”

awful splitting headaches. At times he would
become terribly depressed in spirits, and felt that
he was at death’s door. Mr. Hutchinson continued

Matilda Maude’s Penchant.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sing of Maude Matilda, who
Got spliced the other day,
And, just to give the girl her due,
This much I’ll truly say :
She must have been an instrument,
Within the hand of Fate,
To carry out the high intent
Of something good and great.
For Maude was good—that is, not bad —■
As far as we know wrong,
But from her kiddyhood she had
A curious penchant.
Of all the tints we gaze upon
One only charmed her view ;
For Maude Matilda doted on
A yellowistic hue.
Her clothes were yellow to a dot—
Down to the boots and socks,
And gen’rous Nature topped the lot
With flaming yellow locks.
Each budding fancy, too, brought out
The ruling passion strong,
Until the neighbors talked about
Her curious penchant.
When Maude Matilda reached the age
Of childhood’s greatest joys,
She soon evinced a perfect rage
For yellowistic toys.
But, chief of all, though least in size,
The darling used to hug
One perfect porcelanic prize—
A yellow China mug.
And thus the keynote of her fate
Was struck in saffron song—
As though to fitly celebrate
Matilda Maude’s penchant.
At seventeen our heroine
Became an “Army ” lass,
And sighed that such a thing as sin
Should ever come to pass.
’Twas hers to caution in the street
The unregenerate;
To stand where politicians meet
And warn them of their fate.
And when she painted hues of Hell
You’d smell the sulphur strong —
Till sinners fell would cry, “How well
She sticks to her penchant 1 ”
There was a Chinese gardener
Among the favored few,
That every Sunday followed her
To learn “ what you must do.”
She promised him in Heaven above
A lasting, rich reward :
Until, inspired by Christian love,
He worshipped—“Missee Maude.*
And that was how in time there came
The sequel to this song :
For yesterday she signed her name
“ Matilda Maude Pong Chong.”
BUCKENFOURA.
Bats!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rats ! why, you never saw none; leastways*
none like them" aboard the Runnymede.
She was a bit of a barque, owned by a widow
woman, out of Port Chalmers, and run by old
“ Oarnaru ” Black ; and if ever there was a “ balla-
hoo ” kept afloat by the devil to damn the sailor-
men’s souls, she was one. She was chock-a-block
of rats ; big, grey devils of things, with tails on
’em as thick as your finger. The whole ship stank
of ’em. When she’d be knocking about, we on
deck could hear ’em, above the groaning and creak-
ing of the cargo under hatches, squeaking and
scrambling and scuttling about in the ’tween-
declcs, and up and down the stringers, like as if
they had charge of her. There was nothing in the
cargo for them to eat but cement, so they chawed
holes in our oilskins and sea-boots before we was a
week out.
Of a rainy night the rigging, half-way up from
the sheer-pole, would be alive with them, licking
the rain off as it ran down the shrouds. How we
got to hate the brutes ! They’d fight and make
love, and eat one another down in the fore-scuttle,
as we lay and watched them from our bunks. If
one of us hove a knife or a boot at them, the old
bucks of the crowd would sit up and grin at us,
twitching their whiskers and showing their ugly,
yellow teeth.
We caught them all kinds of ways, but, durn
my rags, there was no getting to windward of
them. They thieved our biscuits, and sugar and
beef; and never failed to leave circumstantial
evidence behind them. We got it in our limejuice
cold, and hot in our coffee and pea-soup. Them
as shipped aboard the Runnymede couldn’t afford
to be dainty.
There was one of them rats as we used to call
‘ The King,” on account of his size—a big, mangy
old buck with a blind eye ; the daddy of the lot, I
reckon, for he was silver-white about the head and
nose. The others were all afraid of the old terror ;
and we were always trying to catch him, but he
knew too much for us. But one night a chap
called Laban, or some such name, gets the blind
side of his majesty and harpooned the old beast
with a sail-needle on the end of a stick. He was
a bit careless in taking a hold of the brute though,
and the “King” managed to mitten him by the
thumb before the life was squeezed out of him.
Well, after the old buck’s death, the rest kind
of fought shy of the fore-scuttle, and Laban he
nails the skin up on the bulkhead alongside his
bunk. But in a couple of days his thumb begins
to throb and swell, and he has to lay up.
His whole hand and arm seemed like poisoned,
and he’d lay in his bunk and rave away about the
rats till we, as was below, would wish him and the
rats to the devil. We was in the forties then ;
and one night, when it came on to blow, all hands
was roused out to shorten sail, and we was hard at
it for a couple of hours before we got the old hooker
snugged down. We was short-handed, and nothing
worked easy on the Runnymede, for she was
rigged at second-hand from all the wrecks on the
Maoriland coast.
The fore-scuttle was closed down, for the craft
always made foul weather of it, and in anything
of a sea she was as wet as a half-tide rock. When
at last we opens up the hatch and goes below the
slush-lamp is out and all is dark.
“Laban’s feeling easier,” says somebody; my
foot goes squelch on a rat, and then we strikes a
light.
The slush-lamp had been knocked off its hook
on the beam, and was lying on the deck in a pool
of stuff that looked like treacle. Another match
was struck, just as the first went out, and twas
then we saw Laban, huddled all of a heap, up in
the eyes of her. The stuff on the deck was blood,
and came from him. His face, and throat, and
hands ! Well, never mind —it was good that he
was dead, anyhow. ,
We all makes a break for the deck, and fetches
the riding-light. The place was strewn with dead
and dying rats. Laban had fought like a madman
for his life, and they—well, they had fought like
We was cabin-passengers for a-while, and slept
aft in the saloon till the fo’castle was tidied up a
bit. We put Laban, or what was left of him, over
the side the next day.
No, we wasn’t troubled over-much with them
devils the rest of the passage, and I didn’t make
another trip in the Runnymede, neither. That
one was enough for me.

One of the staunchest trades-union bodies
in Australia, bitterly anti-alien, has been charged
by some of its non-official members with having
accepted the tender of a German band to play
at its recent picnic in a Sydney suburb. One
official’s reply is that “ the tender of the German
band was lowest, and we haven’t money to throw
away”; another official’s reply is “that German
bands are nearly all Irish.”

as an advt. a photo.-group of Lady Hamp-
den and daughter, with an aide-de-camp on each

thravels a step widout two unicorns at the laste
wid her !”
Remarks the Kwong Yik Wah Bo (Sydney
Chinese paper):—
A Christian sect called the Wesleyans have decided to
send Christian missionaries amongst the Chinese in
Queensland. They decided to do this by only one vote. In
all these Christian bodies who do missionary work, pre-
ference is usually given to the kind of work that appeals
to their sense of the picturesque, that enables their mis-
sionaries to wear foreign clothes and to go amongst
foreign people. The decision of the Wesleyans is a
common-sense decision. Surely, if Christianity is good,
it is well to make Christians of those who are close at
hand. If they convert any of the Queensland Chinese to
their way of thinking, the converts will make good mis-
sionaries to send to China, but it is useless to hope to
convert many of our people while the Christian teaching
is so far away from the practice of the Christians. The
best way to convert the Chinese is to treat them with
absolute justice, so that when they return to China they
will be able to say that the European Christians practise
what they preach.
The Musician.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
L ™ vv aC aTnon f? st the sweet green grass
where the lilies glowed like white faces in thefight
of the. new summer moon, Bill felt the world
swinging under him to the strains of delicious
music. Ihe music came, prosaically enough, from
the open window of a neighboring house, but it
stole through many blooms under the dark clus-
tering boughs and then there were the moonlight
and the young man’s mood. At any rate, the en-
chantment waff complete. At first it was the
music only ; it came to him in the evenings, and
took hold of him—possessed him. He had dreamt
of such melody, had heard of it on starry lakes,
and swelling mysteriously in scented avenues in
the untrodden bush, and he had awakened with the
haunting cadences in his ears. Then the woman
was suggested : young, tall, dark, beautiful,
radiant., _ Bill was big and fair.
The neighbors were new arrivals. Bill had seen
none in the family to correspond with his idea of
the sweet pianist, and he was as eager as a lover.
The thing had become serious. He longed, with
a ridiculous ardour, to see this beautiful woman.
Ordinarily music had not appealed to him; but her
music enslaved him. It was wonderful and uncanny
in its glory; it led his thoughts; and this big,
healthy, hearty man of affairs relapsed under its
influence into a helpless sentimentality unspeak-
ably delicious.
To-night the music was all of love; women’s
voices seemed to whisper all about the young man
arid soft kisses rippled in the air. He fancied he
heard through all an appealing cry.
Bill suddenly arose, and made his way to the
dividing fence, which he scaled with little diffi-
culty. Then, keeping in the shadow of the trees
and shrubs, he stole to a fir from the boughs of
which it was possible to see into the room where
the pianiste sat. The young man climbed the tree
very cautiously, and presently leaned forward and
peered through the open window.
A woman sat at the piano, and Bill’s face
blanched as he gazed at her. She was young; her
face, deeply seamed and scarred, was hideous in
its deformity. Her body was small and twisted,
and her thin, stunted arms terminated in mere
distorted bunches of fingers, piteous to see, and
repellent beyond expression ; but they swept the
keys with marvellous dexterity, and made divinest
music.
Presently the girl ceased playing, and dragging
herself across the room on sprawling limbs tremu-
lous under the weight of her poor wasted body,
passed from the sight of the man without.

ill. I lost all appetite, could not sleep was incapacitated for work, and
suffered from severe pains in the head I tried several tonics, but they did
